The Red Page
The Russian Novel.
Though E. Melchior de Vogiid’s masterly
analysis of The Russian Kovel was written
(in French) in 1886, it is only from the
eleventh French edition that it is translated
into English (Chapman and Hall, 7s. 6d.).
This tardiness emphasises the slowness with
which the Russian novel is making its way
into the consciousness of English literature.
A manual such as this has long been needed,
and in itself the book will accelerate the
translation into English of the great modern
realists of Russia. The author, made a
member of the Acaddmie Frangaise because
of this work, was received by the Russian
Government with honor when, in 1909, he
went thither to take part in the celebration
of Gogol’s centenary. He died in Paris in
1910.
Without the historical perspective so
clearly delineated by de Vogtid, without, too,
the knowledge of the origins of the Russian
novel so cleanly laid bare, such a novelist as
Dostoevsky, when read in translation, offers
too many obstacles to complete comprehen-
sion by, say, the average intelligent Aus-
tralian. No reader could fail to recognise
the genius of this novelist; but, for a fairer
appreciation of him it is necessary for us to
“place” him, and other modern Russian
novelists, in their right environment, to see
them as the natural blossom of the strange
literary soil of Russia.
A country of vacuous minds, like those
of the seafarer: self-centred, long-suffering
and resigned, subject to sudden passions of
desire. A soil better suited to dwellers in
tents thafl in houses, where the ideas are as
nomadic as are the inhabitants themselves.
Just as the cold blasts from the White Sea
find no obstacles in their way to the Black
Sea, so do invasions, calamities and the sor-
rows of slavery pass rapidly and irresistibly
over these vast, empty spaces. \
<£*
Five landmarks are selected by the author
for his survey—-Pushkin, Gogol, Turgeneff,
Dostoevsky and Tolstoy. The novel in
Russia is of humble origin, and its history
prior to 1840 was neither long nor brilliant.
Gogol was its founder. All the later novelists
have drawn upon him; are derived from
him. In form and substance Gogol has done
everything for his successors. The majority
of the types presented to us in the Russian
novel have their beginning in Dead Souls.
Such a character of the energetic landed
proprietor, full of vague ideals for the better-
ment of the serfs, who, failing to transform
his ideals into reality, grows disgusted, with-
draws within himself and enters the final
state of torpor, reappears in many forms,
under many different conditions, “but always
carrying the same half-dozen master-strokes
of description assigned to him by Gogol.”
“A fund of characters and a fund of ideas!
The. great currents of thought that are to
fertilise the Russian mind will ever flow
from this first source.”
Dead Souls has never ceased to be looked
upon, as a true personification of ancient
Russia. For the last 40 years it is the
mainspring of national life. Every joke
has passed into a proverb; every character
has a permanent place in the ideal society
to be found in the standard literature of
every country. The stranger who has not
read Dead Souls often comes to a standstill
in conversation, out of ignorance of the an-
cestral and family traditions to which refer-
ences are made at every turn.
The author emphasises another of the dif-
ferences that so significantly divide the Rus-
sian soul from ours. The Russian public
does not read its novelists for diversion, nor
does it expect a novel to be a work of art.
“What is to us a superfluous luxury is to
the Russians the soul’s daily bread. The
writer is the guide of his race, the master of
a multitude of confused ideas, and in a small
measure the constructor of their language.”
The lower classes are only commencing to
read, and read with avidity, with faith and
hope, as we read Robinson Crusoe at the
age of 12. They are “virgin soil.” Their
imaginations are not deadened, as with us,
by the vastness of our means of educa-
tion. . . . After reading Father and Son or
War and Peace we say to ourselves: “It is
only a novel.” But to the tradesman in Mos-
cow or the son of the village priest, to the
small landholder, whose bookshelves contain
volumes of Pushkin, Gogol or Nekrasoff, re-
presenting the encyclopaedia of the human
mind—each of the first-named volumes be-
comes one of the books in the National Bible.
<£
Another of the characteristics of Russian
literature that appears strange to us—the
fact that the Russian writer never attacks
an abuse —has an obvious explanation. The
press laws suppress anything of the kind.
Thus Russian literature must proceed by
indirect means. The novelist merely depicts
without arriving at any conclusions, since he
is not allowed by the police to arrive at any
conclusions; he is forced to get his effect by
an appeal to the feelings of compassion
rather than those of anger. Thus when
Dostoevsky publishes his Recollections of the
House of the Dead —that terrible personal
recollection of ten years in Siberia —“he
writes just in the same way, without utter-
ing a single rebellious word, without a drop
of gall, appearing to find all he writes about
as quite natural, perhaps a little sad. This
is the national characteristic in everything.”
It is in Dostoevsky that this national
religion of suffering finds its final expres-
sion. De Vogiid says that the Russia of the
last 20 years will remain an inexplicable
enigma if one ignores the books which have
made the deepest impression and created
the greatest disturbance in the country dur-
ing that time —the books of Dostoevsky.
Of this writer’s literary method the author
makes a penetrating analysis.
What is most remarkable is the way the
dialogues are woven together as if by the
thinnest of electric wires through which a
mysterious current flows uninterruptedly.
A word which we may have left unnoticed, or
the slightest fact, mentioned in one line, has
its significance 50 pages further on. One
has to go back to them to understand the
transformations of a soul in which these
germs, fallen there as if by chance, have de-
veloped in the dark. This is so true that if one
skips a few pages the rest become quite un-
intelligible. One feels angry with the
author for being so prolix, one runs on
ahead of him, and, all of a sudden, he is no
longer understood—the electric current has
been interrupted.... Grime and Punishment
never bores, but it tires one, as does a thor-
oughbred always on the prance ; add to this
the necessity of finding one’s way among a
crowd of cunning people who, like shadows,
glide about the background. All this com-
pels the reader’s closest attention, and to
have as good a memory as is required for
the study of a philosophic work. It is a
pleasure or an inconvenience, according to
the quality of the reader.
And, like this page in its recent review of
this author, de Vogtid finds that: —
These people never eat, they drink tea, and
only at night. Many are drunkards. They
seldom sleep, and, when they do, they dream.
There are more dreams in Dostoevsky’s
books than in all our standard works
put together. They nearly always have
fever. In every 20 pages we come
across the expression, “feverish state” at
least once, ... Of the innumerable people in-
vented by Dostoevsky I do not know one
whom Charcot, the famous alienist, could
not have claimed as his own under some
guise or other.... Dostoevsky is like a trav-
eller who has been all over the world, seen
everything, but has travelled only by night.
He is an incomparable psychologist, but only
as a student of dismal and mangled souls.
He is a clever dramatist, but only amongst
scenes of terror and compassion.
De Vogiid sums up Russia by the striking
metaphor that it is the spirit of an English
scientist in the soijl of a Buddhist.
Dostoevsky
There is one pleasant fact about Fyodor
Dostoevsky, you can spell his name as you
like. E. M. de Vogiid, in The Russian
Kovel, spells it Dostoyevsky; Constance Gar-
nett, in her translations of his works, terms
the author Dostoevsky ; and C. J. Hogarth,
in his translation of Letters from the
Underworld, speaks of Fedor Dostoieffsky.
The pronunciation is Dostoydvsky, with the
“o” long in both cases and the “y” sound
strong, thus —Do-sto-ye-vsky.
The reader is solemnly warned, in read-
ing Letters from the Underworld and Other
Tales. (Dent’s Everyman’s Library, Is. 3d.
out here) to omit the section titled “The
Underworld.” In this the author states
that his hero presents himself add his views
on life, and purports to explain the causes
which have created his personality. He
talks about himself very wearisomely for
48 pages, and the self that he depicts, how-
ever brilliantly analysed, is a character
whose prolixity wearies the English reader.
Thus, this voluntary denison of the under-
world finds pleasure in toothache. He ex-
plains in four pages that the groans of a
person with toothache express the sufferer’s
delight. For if he found no pleasure in
them he would not groan. Apparently it
never occurs to the sufferer to suppress his
groans, or to go to a dentist.
“Apropos of the Falling Sleet” leads us
into more interesting country. Here we
have the story of a night of dissipation
told with an extraordinary realism, in tfie
course of which the hero —though one doubts
whether Dostoevsky would apply that term
to his chief character—finds his way, in
pursuit of revenge on an officer who has
insulted him, to a brothel. He passes the
night there, and, shamed and angered with
himself, he finds a devilish way to recover
his self-respect. With deliberate malignity
he stirs in the soul of the poor girl her
suppressed longings for a better life; but
when his words have worked on her so
that she attempts to escape from her life,
and she shows him that she sincerely loves
her deliverer, he cruelly insults her, and
confesses that he had been merely experi-
menting on her soul. The character of this
girl is one of the most pathetic figures in
literature.
J*
“The Gentle Maiden” is a remarkable
achievement. Dostoevsky explains his
method.
Imagine a man by whose side, on a table,
there lies the dead body of his Wife. Though
some hours have elapsed since she hurled
herself from a window the man is still so
distracted with the shock that he cannot
collect his thoughts. He passes from room
to room of the house, and endeavors vainly
to realise what has happened and to rally
his faculties. He is an inveterate hypo-
chondriac —the kind of man who is for
ever talking of himself; and this he is now
doing, as he recounts the history of the
affair and tries to devise some explanation
for it. As for consecutiveness of speech, he
only contradicts himself, both in logic and
sentiment. The one moment he is trying to
justify himself and to lay the blame on her,
and the next moment he is launching out
into a string of extraneous reasonings.. Grief
and profound agitation have possession of
him. Yet by degrees he begins to under-
stand better, and to focus his thoughts. The
flood of recollections irresistibly aroused in
him at last leads to the truth: and that
truth ends by uplifting him in soul and in
spirit. Indeed, towards the close of the
story the whole tone of his soliloquy differs
from his disorderly beginning, for there has
dawned upon the unfortunate man the
reality of things—or what he conceives to
be such.
And Dostoevsky unravels the story of a
pitiable tragedy, in which it was life that
was to blame, from its stark beginning, “So
there she lies —beautiful, as always!” to its
noble ending:—
Everything is dead, and dead men are
everywhere. Only human beings are here,
and over them there broods a silence. That
is the world. “Men, love one another.”
Who said that? Whose command was it?
The pendulum continues beating insen-
sibly, malignantly. It is two o’clock in
the morning. Her little boots stand there
by the bed, as though awaiting her. Ah,
but, in very truth, when they take her away
to-morrow, what will become of me?
But for Dostoevsky at his highest, for
the supreme quality of romance, for pathos,
mystery, adventure and terror, you must
read “The Landlady.” This story is one of
the most remarkable ever written. It is a
romance that for tenseness of interest
easily outbids the sensation-monger, yet the
characterisation is so realistic that the
melodrama of the story becomes immedi-
ately credible. And the character of the
heroine is a triumph not only of psychology,
but, as so often with this author, of patheo-
logy. And the reader closes the book in a
mental impasse. The whole structure of the
story has been deftly shattered, and the
reader is left vainly wondering whether the
heroine was not the villain in disguise. It
is a tour de force that has no rival in Eng-
lish literature. This volume is cheap and
accessible. The Australian reader, hitherto
ignorant of the work of this genius, has here
a lavish feast.
' «**
While The Brothers Karamazov occupied
over 800 pages of close type, The Idiot and
The Possessed stretch a mere 600 pages.
Though Constance Garnett’s translation of
the The Possessed (Heinemann, per Dy-
mock’s Library) is masterly, the book re-
quires an effort for digestion. It is as
formless, as inarticulate, as any of Dos-
toevsky’s long novels ; and its characters, as
usual, range through the whole gamut of
insanity, from its hero, who is merely “pos-
sessed,” and Lizaveta, who is merely in a
continual state of overmastering excitement,
to the pathetic and harmless mental dulness
of Marya, the gentle and lovable foolishness
of Stepan, the mystical sanity of Kirillov,
and the madness of the Nihilists. The value
of the novel to the foreign reader lies in its
elucidation of the springs of Nihilistic
action. , The plot centres in a Nihilist group
in a provincial town. The psychology of
every member of that group is analysed
with a knowledge subtle, merciless and
astoundingly exact; while the ramifications
of the organism and its methods of govern-
ment are set forth with the authority of one
to whom Nihilism was an inevitable com-
monplace of Russian civilisation.
It is impossible to detail the plot, so vast
and so vague does it appear, and so many
are the subsidiary characters upon which its
mechanism is dependent. The Possessed
obtains our interest solely by its brilliant
character sketches of Russian types—to us
abnormal types —and its many moments of
intense dramatic appeal. There are enough
adventures and thrilling situations in the
one chapter headed “A Busy Night” to keep
the English writer of sensational fiction
going for a decade. One scene may be in-
stanced to show how Dostoevsky can clutch
and keep his reader’s emotions. Pyotr, the
clever, callous organiser of the Nihilist
“quintet,” determines upon the murder of
one of the members of the society: it is
necessary not so much because that member
may become dangerous to himself, nor even
because Pyotr hates him, but because it is
advisable to bind the other members to-
gether in a “blood-bond” that will prevent
them from betraying the society. If all are
equally guilty of the murder, none will dare
to inform the police. But it is necessary to
put the authorities off the scent. And it is
here that Pyotr’s scheming becomes bril-
liant. He had long known that Kirillov
had meant to kill himself, simply because
that was the only way this man could prove
himself greater than God. But for a man
to kill himself thus casually seemed to
Pyotr a waste of good Nihilist material.
He manages to get hold of Kirillov, by giv-
ing him the money to return to Russia—
he wants to die in Russia —and in return
exacts a promise from Kirillov that he will
not commit suicide until Pyotr allows him,
and, also, that he will leave a paper behind
taking upon himself the blame for some
Nihilist outrage. Thus the police will be
misled and the Nihilists will be given time
to escape. Immediately after Shotov has
been brutally murdered Pyotr goes to Kiril-
lov and informs him that the time has come.
But Kirillov was a friend of the murdered
man; and Pyotr finds himself confronted
with the dangerous possibility that Kirillov
will not kill himself, or will not sign the
dictated confession of his responsibility for
the crime. This scene, in which Pyotr,
maddened at the delay in his plans, and
knowing that if he fails his own life will be
forfeit, uses every resource in his mind to
force Kirillov to consent, feeling that at any
moment Kirillov, who hates him, might first
turn his revolver on his tormentor, and yet
conscious that to shoot Kirillov would spoil
everything—this scene could be depicted in
its horrible realism and its heightening ten-
sion only by a master of the morbid like
Dostoevsky. And there are many other
scenes of a similar poignancy.
It is, too, his creation of characters like
Marya, the crippled mad girl, and Stepan,
the lovable, foolish man of letters, that
makes the genius of Dostoevsky unmistak-
able. It is curious to note that Tolstoy’s
last pilgrimage, when the thought of his
imminent end sent him out secretly travel-
ling through Russia, with no thought of
any destination but death, has been antici-
pated in this novel. Stepan, a gentle, child-
ish fool, goes forth to his pathetic death on
the highway in The Possessed; and Tolstoy
followed him. The Russian looks upon the
novel not as a work of art —which, indeed,
in Dostoevsky it seldom is—but as a new
and inspired chapter in the Russian Bible.
•
Once again. Every week some correspon-
dent pathetically asks this page where he
may obtain some book reviewed here. There
are certain places in every city where, on
inquiry, a customer may obtain boots; there
are other places where a customer may
obtain books. The latter are known as
bookshops. The proprietors of these book-
shops, the address of which can be obtained
from any directory, rather like selling books.
If they have not got the book asked for in
stock, they have been known to order it from
other kindly people known as book pub-
lishers. A bookseller’s perennial delight in
selling books has even led him to advertise
on this page, in the naive hope that people
who desire books might get into touch with
the person whose business it is to sell books.

ot this charming Sydney suburb where he has taken an active

recommended to him. jA Read his remarkable letter.
CLEMENTS TONIC LTD.
Years ago I became completely debilitated, and for six months I was
unable to follow my occupation. My nerves were in such a state that sleep
was almost impossible. My appetite had left me, I lost weight rapidly,
getting as low as 10 stone. I WAS JUST ABOUT AS BAD BODILY
AND MENTALLY AS IT WAS POSSIBLE TO BE.
I had medical advice, but derived no benefit from the medicine
prescribed, and began to lose hope of ever being my old self again.
Hearing of the wonders of Clements Tonic in similar cases, I
purchased some, and after taking it for a time I began to notice a beneficial
result. My appetite improved, and the nerves, becoming soothed, allowed
me to have my natural sleep. I BEGAN TO LOOK SO WELL
THAT PEOPLE SEEING ME ASKED WHAT MEDICINE I
WAS TAKING. I kept on with the Tonic till I fully recovered, and
I am now in the best of health, weighing 12st. 41b. I HAVE CLEM-
ENTS TONIC TO THANK FOR MY CURE. I have great
faith in it.
Living in Hunter s Hill all my life, and being well known, this
statement can be vouched for, as nearly everyone knew how bad in health
1 was. You may use this as you think fit.”

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hurrying Feet.
Where are you going to, hurrying feet,’
Mistily seen through the prisms above,
Roof to my chamber and floor to the street.
Lighting the dull, dismal room that I love V
Here I have labored and plotted and
dreamed—
Fool that I was to have labored at all!
Always I’ll hear that cold laugh, that she
deemed
Suitable answer, last night at the ball.
Is it some vain, foolish hope that has stirr’d
you,
Making you hasten with heart on.the beat?
Hasten to tell her, and when she has heard
you—
How she will laugh at you, hurrying feet!
Into the country or down to the ocean,
P’r’aps you are hurrying, there to be free;
Loving the long, winding road with devotion,
Rivalled alone by your love for the sea.
If in the ships that go out with the tide,
Out where the sea and the sky seem to
meet,
You should be seeking your sorrow to hide —
Wait for me! Wait for me! Hurrying
feet!
VICTORIA.
SPASM.
An Idyll of Bulls and Wings.
The bull the simple farmer keeps
Together with his ducks and hens,
The tilted tails of chirping wrens,
The sun that through green branches
creeps—
All these, if time should suit, are rife
With influence on human life.
Sages have noticed that the swine
Who love in fatted ease to lie,
Close sheltered in their straw-set sty.
May wield an influence divine.
The subjects of my truthful pen
Are one large bull and one small wren.
The little bird was crowned with blue,
The summer airs were warm and pale,
Three inches of a flirting tail
Fanned the soft leaves that greenly grew
Above the simple man and maid
Who spoke of love within the shade.
It happened that I was the man,
And she the girl' whose hair was such
It waved and glistened at the touch
Of little lights that leaped and ran
From the blown branches of the tree
To scatter gold on her and me.
Her eyes were quite as black as night,
Her lips were quite as red as sin,
Her waist was barbed with one sharp pin
That interrupted my delight
To tear some inches of my wrist
That first sweet moment that we kist.
Another barb was at her neck,
A simple jewel set with red,
Placed as the guardian of her head
To hold that daring wight in check
Whose boldness might aspire to win
The laughing dimple on her chin.
I do not think that girls should wear
More spines than deck the porcupine:
Yet this dear maid whose lips were mine
Was such a tonic to dull care,
Death adders’ bites had scarce restrained
My rapture till her mouth was gained.
Whether the blue wren called the bull
Or fate appointed what occurred,
I do not know. The bushes stirred.
And all the summer air was full
Of horns and eyes and lifted tail,
And screams, and one sweet girl grown pale.
The bull was somewhat out of breath,
The fence was half a mile away;
But neither of us cared to stay
To meet the kind of sudden death
That, judging by his noise and fuss,
The creature wished to hand to us.
The girl was quickest up the tree —
Her screams appeared to lend her wings ;
By various leaps and bounds and springs
She scrambled up ahead of me,
And perched upon a little bough
Above the uncle of the cow.
I followed her in eager haste
Because the girl was very dear,
And that bull’s horns were all so near
That, when I clasped her fluttered waist.
I breathed a sigh of great relief
To miss that mass of angry beef.
The girl was very much annoyed.
Her frock was torn beyond repair,
And portions of her blackest hair,
Which I had lately much enjoyed.
Were fallen in a glossy coil
Upon the bull-encumbered soil.
We seemed and felt and looked absurd.
Perched in the whiskers of a tree.
That tearful damsel clung to me
The while a small blue-headed bird.
With daring flight and courage cool,
Fluttered between us and the bull.
The view we had was excellent.
When sunset filled the skies with flame
The long, brown shadows, sleek and tame
A charm to all the landscape lent:
And when the stars began to peep
The purple skies were soft and deep.
The bull decided to remain.
And that is why. when days are bright
And maiden’s lips and eyes delight,
I always take an aeroplane;
And. soaring far from beef and men.
Flit through the sunshine like a wren.
When clouds are soft and young and few,
And skies are empty, wide and fair,
I love the hornless depths of air
And happy sweeps of bull-less blue,
For roosting on that midnight bough
.Taught, me to hate, the .race .of Cow. .
N.S.W.

Christmas Number, post paid.

Six Shillings and Sixpence Sterling you can have
The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week
for a year.
Cheques, drafts, etc., which should he crossed, and

How to Address letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) hy taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case he addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone hy name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused hy addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of
MS.

THURSDAY, MAY 7, 1914.
THE LOST CITY THAT WAS NEVER
FOUND.
Last week the Australian capital city of
Canberra got one more burst of public appro-
bation when the papers mentioned that a
second outbreak of diphtheria had occurred
at the Duntroon Military College. It was
so bad that out of 120 cadets no less than
40 were under suspicion on the day when
the news was published. Diphtheria is one
of the diseases which arise through bad
water and insanitary circumstances, and its
recurrence at the unbuilt Federal city, de-
spite abundance of fresh air and a complete
lack of overcrowding, isn’t the sort of pub-
licity that the house-and-land shark craves
after. The unconstructed city of ’Berra has
no luck worth mentioning. It is shown by
the meteorological records that if the whole
coast-line and the whole table-land behind
the coast-line are searched from the Gulf
of Carpentaria round to Melbourne, Can-
berra is the centre of the driest area. It
lies in a sort of depression, and the rain
clouds are deviated by the configuration of
the ground. Queanbeyan to the east, Yass
to the west, and Cooma to the south are all
places with a dismal narrative of scant
moisture. The news that comes from these
localities is mostly depressing. It is largely
to the effect that the Cotter has become a
chain of waterholes, that the Molonglo has
ceased running, that the Murrumbidgee is
very low (a recent report was to the effect
that it almost promised to cease running),
that water is scarce at Yass, that it is
scarcer at Queanbeyan, that there is diph-
theria at Duntroon Military College, and
that the Government employees have struck
by reason of lack of drinkable material and
the abundance of dead rabbits in what drink-
able material there is. Even the handful of
men who were employed doing a few pre-
liminary jobs about the unborn Babylon
declared that the water supply wasn’t large
enough for them, and that the defunct
bunny was “over the odds.”
The Canberra capital began with a sort
of moral curse upon it. It is the City of
Lies and Dirt. It had its genesis in the
act of treachery by which the N.S.W. Legis-
lature, at the instance of “Jawbone” Nield,
and with the countenance of George lleid,
broke its agreement with the other States,
and annulled the vote by which N.S.W.
accepted the first Federal Constitution. Out
of that unclean deed came the concession
that every State except N.S.W. was to be
disqualified as a possible site for the Aus-
tralian capital. After that, the representa-
tives of the other States lost interest in
the capital site question, and the choice was
left, in a great measure, to the N.S.W. re-
presentatives. And they, being almost en-
tirely Sydney men, and dominated by the
Sydney daily papers and the Sydney Con-
gestion interest, presently settled down to
one steady purpose. The metropolis was to
be the sort of place that couldn’t possibly
rival Sydney. At the same time it was
to be as near that city as the Constitution
allowed, so that the bottle-neck place could
secure its trade—what there might be of it.
The Dryberra capital was allowed a scrap
of land for a so-called port, the said scrap
stopping at high-water mark so that nothing
bigger than a canoe or coracle could possibly
get. near it. It was allowed a very narrow
right-of-way so that a very narrow line
might be built to connect the capital which
had the minimum of rain with the port that
ended on dry land. Even the Sydney Tele-
graph, which was the most furious advo-
cate of the ’Berra misery, tacitly admitted
that it recommended the site because of its
badness. The political centre of Australia,
it said, would never be more than a large
country town. Hughes, the most prominent
of the N.S.W. Laborites, and a man whose
interests are all in Sydney, was so anxious
on behalf of Canberra, the place that was to
be a mere mean suburb of the haunts of his
own Wharf-Laborers’ Union, that he even
turned dog on the sacred principle of the
referendum when it was proposed that it
should be applied in this particular instance.
His views for the moment about the mental
incapacity of the common herd were as
arrogant and unpublishable as those of (say)
Bruce Smith.
* * * *
All this petty meanness and parish-minded
jealousy constituted a bad beginning, and
the progress hasn’t been much better than
the commencement. From the day when
a N.S.W. Government, with the mind of a
police-court law shark, and the manners of
a bushranger, refused to go on with the
Federal movement which it had inaugurated
unless it was bribed with the possession of
the Federal capital, to the time when the
Canberra workmen struck on account of too
much dead rabbit in the water and the date
when the second diphtheria outbreak hap-
pened at Duntroon College, Canberra failed
to publish itself in a promising fashion.
Even the Sydney papers, which once clam-
ored that it was a paradise, hardly mention
it these days. Now and then, however,
they wake up and blame Prime Minister
Cook for not finding more money and hurry-
ing up the work of construction, and in that
occasional shindy lies their own condemna-
tion. The place is a pauper and a bad
debt. Money has to be found for it; it
doesn’t find money for itself. The one city
in the world, and in all history, which is
the political hub of a whole continent, and
which, in addition, represents the biggest ex-
periment up to date in the public ownership
of city lands, shouldn’t require to have all
its money found for it. It should be an
asset, not a liability. It should, with the
Federal prestige that it has and the natural
advantages that it ought to have, be a big
source of income. The Government ought
to be rushed by people desirous to plank
down £lO,OOO deposit on a 50-years’ lease of
a site whereon they could erect a smokeless
boot factory or something of the sort, worked
by the water-power that Canberra hasn’t
got. The official doormat ought to be worn
out by folk who desire to pay big rents for
wharf-sites, also sites for shops, houses,
warehouses, theatres and everything else im-
aginable. Unhappily the Treasury doesn’t
seem to be rilshed with a single copper.
When the Lord presented Canaan to the
people of Israel it is never implied that He
gave Israel a liability or an unpaid bill or
anything of the sort. Canaan is always
spoken of as a glorious benefit. And the
capital of a continent should be worth more
in cash than Canaan ever was, unless the
choice is something exceptional in the way
of badness. The spectacle of Moses en-
gaged in a bitter struggle to pay for the
Promised Land, instead of the Promised
Land paying for Moses, has an element of
humor about it.
Prime Minister Cook was an enthusiastic
Canberra-ite in the days when he didn’t know
any better. Now he is a tired atom trying
to carry a dead horse, and his applause has
greatly diminished. He has a proud insti-
tution on his hands that eats up pounds and
doesn’t return even pence. He knows per-
fectly well that his followers don’t want to
follow him to Canberra. The politician has
the same love as any other man for lights,
and theatres, and bustle, and hotels, and bar-
maids. and tree-lined boulevards, and the
other joys of existence. He doesn’t crave to
be marooned at a country town on a small
branch railway. He doesn’t yearn to vote
large sums in order to hurry up the time
when he will be dragged from the fleshpots
of Melbourne and sent to the bears and the
gum-trees. Meanwhile Sydney, in whose
supposed interests the Australian metropolis
was devised, hasn’t collected any dividends
up to date. If Canberra is the failure it
promises to be, its trade will do the big
Cornstalk town no good. If by any impos-
sible chance, it is a success, its traffic will
only make the railway congestion worse, and
the suburban tram more belated, and the
price of meat Id. per lb. more owing to the
further delay of the bullock era route.
The Bulletin had (and still has) visions
of a real Australian metropolis, of which the
people would be sole ground landlords for
ever. It was to be bigger than Sydney or
Melbourne —-which would mean that the un-
improved ground values would bring in over
£2,000,000 a year, and N.S.W., instead of
getting a dead rabbit and an unpaid bill,
would receive nearly £1.000,000 a year as its
share of the unearned increment. The only
way such a city could be raised would be by
making it a great manufacturing centre with
a great independent port alongside. The
proposed site at Dalgety. with Twofold Bay
as its outlet, seemed to fill the bill as nearly
as possible. The water-power of the Snowy
River offers means for the cheapest manufac-
turing in Australia. Twofold Bay, as a
port, is capable of wiping the floor with
Melbourne any day, or even twice a day, and
it might easily, with sufficient and reasonable
opportunity, shift the trade centre of that
part of Australia from Melbourne into
N.S.W. territory. But by reason of the
crazy jealousy of certain Sydney papers and
politicians, and in particular the folly of an
amateur colonel who possessed no business
capacity, the chance was thrown away. In-
stead of another City of the Violet Crown
there is a sort of skeleton hamlet on the
road to nowhere, and another epidemic of
diphtheria.
TALL MEAT IN SYDNEY.
Mention was made last week that the price
of flesh food or gory victuals, including
tripe, kidneys and pigs’ feet, had risen to a
higher status in Sydney than at any time in
the previous 10 years, and there was much
investigation and recrimination in conse-
quence. A general tendency was to take the
line of least resistance, and to blame the
capitalists and the banks and the Meat Trust.
That is an easy track of argument, like
blaming “Home,” or the Devil, or the lack of
nigger labor, or the malignity of somebody's
mother-in-law. There are points which
call for consideration, apart from the
simple highway of argument, which con-
sists in laying the responsibility on
someone else. To begin with, Sydney
is bigger than it ever was before, and re-
quires more meat. The long tangled railways
that lead to N.S.W.’s one big city are longer
than they ever were beforehand meat has to
be dragged over greater distances in order to
ensure a supply. The losses by death or
deterioration of bullock on the road have
reached a climax, for the amount of traffic
that is crowded on the narrow protracted
ways has increased beyond all previous re-
cords, and the delays have also increased.
The bull that survives has to pay for the cow
that is trodden to death in the trucks on the
interminable pilgrimage, and the more cow
is trodden on the higher is the cost of the bull
that lives. The wages of the pastoralists’
employee have gone up a little, but not much.
The railway men’s wages have gone up a
little. The butcher’s rent has gone up
mightily. The wages of his employees have
gone up somewhat. The taxes have risen
like a sky-rocket, because it is necessary to
pay interest on the awful permanent and re-
productive debt, which seems to grow more
permanent and less reproductive every time
it is considered. The railway charges have
been increased, also by reason of the perma-
nent and reproductive debt. The longer the
haul, and the slower the transit, and the
more death and depreciation of meat there is
on the road, the bigger is the freight bill.
And, by some streak of ill-luck, just when the
demand is greatest, and the congestion is
worst, and the delays are longest, and the
freight bill is highest, and the late lamented
cow that is thrown out of the truck by the
wayride is most frequent, the total quantity
of stock in N.S.W. is diminishing. The pub-
lished records are available only up to the end
of 1912, for the counting of cows and the
computation of sheep is a lengthy business.
But in 1912 they showed a tremendous de-
cline in cattle, sheep and pigs. There was no
great expansion of exports under the influ-
ence of any Meat Trust or other institution
to account for this falling off. Apparently
the struggle to get the animal to Sydney had
just made the grower too tired or heart-
broken to keep up the fight.
* * * *
To quote a single instance: Sydney insists
on being the sole outlet for N.S.W.’s wool ex-
port. For the two first months of 1913 the
export of wool was 54.000,0001 b. During the
two first months of 1914 it was 70,000,0001 b.
(Wool doesn’t die in the trucks when the
train is doing an average of two miles an
hour on a 450-mile journey.) Because
there was, for each of the first two months
of 1914, 8,000,0001 b. more wool blocking
the long railway to the one station in the
one city the meat trains travelled more
slowly, were more frequently blocked, cost
more in transit, and left more perished food
behind them. Naturally the struggle for the
animal that survives after climbing over that
extra 8,000,0001 b. of wool was intensified,
and the price rose. At the same time the in-
clination to grow meat for the city, or to
send meat to the city, didn’t rise. Meat
Trusts are bad, but they aren’t the whole
trouble, or even half the beginning of the
trouble, in N.S.W. A Meat Trust which pro-
poses to monopolise local supplies, and do a
big export trade, and grievously loot the
meat-hungry folks of Europe, must needs
have an outlet for its goods. If Sydney is
the only outlet, and the bottle-neck can’t
find sufficient accommodation for local trade,
then the Trust is just as badly crippled as
anybody else. Considering the lack of any
boom in meat exports from N.S.W. it may
be inferred that the Meat Trust hasn’t got
fairly to work in that State. Possibly it
started and found circumstances too much for
it. At all events there are sufficient explana-
tions for the high price of meat in Sydney,
based on long-standing local evils arising out
of long-standing local selfishness, muddle and
mismanagement. The U.S. Meat Trust is
almost certainly operating in Queensland, but
then Queensland has a multitude of ports at
which a steak and chop monopoly can find a
means of exit. It can hardly do that in N.S.M .
Both it and its ill-gotten cow would be trod-
den to death in the Redfern tunnel.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Man of Faith.
There must surely be an astonishing
capacity for ignorance or for plain lying
about the professional advocates of peace.
As a case in point, witness this extract
from a letter in Sydney Telegraph, dated
April 29, and signed “Stanley F. Allen,
Hon. Sec., Australian Freedom League, Syd-
ney Council”: —
If our young nation chooses to act in a fair,
upright, honorable way towards all other Powers
there will be no need for such a system as we (the
first of all Anglo-Saxon people) have adopted.
Apart from the shameless historical lie (see-
ing that Britain had compulsory military
service for anything between 1090 and 2000
years within the times we know of, the lie is
pretty shameless), this is about the worst blas-
phemy ever published against Jesus Christ.
For He, the greatest teacher and the most per-
fect gentleman that ever lived, came to
grievous trouble and suffering on earth. If
“Stanley F. Allen, Hon. Sec.,” implies
that He met these troubles because He
wasn’t “fair, upright and honorable” in His
dealings, then that person (Stanley F.
Allen to wit) is taking, in his lack of
enlightenment, liberties such as many polite
and learned atheists wouldn’t care to be
guilty of. Incidentally it might be a great
idea if all advocates of peace and non-resist-
ance were compelled to live in houses
painted pure white. The color would sig-
nify that the inhabitant is peaceful and
non-resistant; that he is,.fpir, upright and
honorable in all his dealings ! with other
Powers, including night-prowlers, explorers
of ash-bins, dog stealers, stray cats, bottle-o
persons, burglars, forgers and the like; that
no compulsory (or compulsorily-paid, which
is the same thing) police force will be called
in to defend him; that his front door and
his safe and his daughter’s bedroom are
always open ; and that if he is robbed or
sandbagged it will be entirely because of
his own lack of fairness, uprightness and
honor —the robbers and sandbaggers being
beyond suspicion—therefore he will have no
complaint to make. Therein lies the whole
story of the opposition to compulsory defence
(or compulsory payment for defence, which
is the same thing). The difference between
England and Australia lies in this fact:
When a country has nearly 700 people to the
square mile, a sufficiency of voluntary de-
fenders (paid by compulsory taxation) may
be found amid the population, but when it
has less than two persons to the square mile
the whole population is required for defence
purposes. But the compulsory element is
there in either case.
—
The Bruce Smith Land.
The chief tragedy of Mexico is that the
Mexicans don’t own it. It is the world’s
most horrible example of' a country from
which foreign capital has refused to be
scared. The United States, Britain and
France own most of the railways, the
national bonds and the industrial enter-
prises. Just twice as much of these assets
as the inhabitants do, to be explicit. The
Mexicans look strong in ranches and farms,
but the majority of these are heavily mort-
gaged to foreigners. The rubber industry is
owned abroad. France is chief proprietor of
the mills and factories, and Britain and the
United States have legal possession of the
oil wells. That is one of the reasons yyhy
war is chronic in the vast republic. It re-
quires no capital to wage guerilla warfare,
and there is a gratifying possibility of loot
about the procedure, and an off-chance of
putting a stroke across the loathly foreigner.
Mexico is a naturally wealthy country, but
until its people get hold of the mainsprings
of its resources it is not in human nature
that they should give themselves up to steady
and continuous work. If ever they get an
honest leader, who will not only lead, as
Diaz did, but who (as Diaz didn’t) will
ruthlessly discourage the foreign capitalist,
Mexico will have a chance of becoming peace-
ful, prosperous and progressive. It has no
chance now. It represents, in the most ad-
vanced form, three things that the old Aus-
tralian Tory party used to crave for: (1)
The importation of foreign capital to de-
velop the great natural resources of the coun-
try; (2) abundance of cheap colored labor
for the benefit of a small, white population :
(3) big estates and great “captains of rural
industry.” When you get these things toge-
ther—and in no other country are they com-
bined on so large a scale —and when you get
them in a country where the mort-
gaged white population has to fend for
itself, and hasn’t, like the white fragment
in India, an empire behind it, that is Mexico.
During its alleged republican days it had
two emperors, both executed; a “Pastry
War” with France because of the alleged
stealing of tarts from the shop of a French
baker in Mexico city; another French war;
a British war; several Spanish wars: a
United States war; a President with a
wooden leg; about 250 revolutions in 60
years; many Presidents that only lasted a
few days; one President that lasted 12
minutes; quite a lot of Presidents who died
by violence : and a whirl and blaze of hap-
penings. These stirring events all turned on
two points: (1) The mixed races couldn t
agree; (2) the cheap colored labor was in a
state of chronic anger; (3) the great mass
of the people, seeing that the land was
owned by a few big owners (many of them
foreign), and the mines, railways, banks,
insurance offices, and public debt by outside
capitalists who had come to develop the great
natural resources of the country, didn t care
a hang.
The Status of the N.S.W. Labor Confer-
ence; to which is Added the Parable
of the Big Flea.
There is an ancient distich which runs as
follows: —
Big fleas have little fleas upon their backs to bite
And little fleas have lesser fleas ; and so ad infinitum
The axiom which is involved in the aboi e
challenged by the N.S.W. Worker


(23/4/T4), and the Labor organ is guilty
of other wild statements. According to the
Worker, which is annoyed at Premier
Holman’s attempts to discipline Speaker
Meagher in connection with the Legislative
Council trouble: —
The Labor movement has no leader. It represents
a phase of evolution infinitely in advance of the days
when the workers had to be “led.”
They have no use for leaders. In conference
assembled they formulate their "policies and decide
their tactics. In mutual associations they select
their candidates and conduct their campaigns.
Also: —
Premier Holman is Labor’s servant, and his Gov-
ernment is Labor’s servant, and if they have not
sufficient humility to glory in rendering service to
Labor; if, in an excess of self-esteem, they seek to
exercise the power of masters over Labor; then the
sooner the situation is defined the better for all
concerned.
The Labor party, speaking through the mouth of
its High Court, the annual conference, has decided
that before persons are appointed to the Legislative
Council by the. Labor Government : they must be
approved of by the executive of the movement.
Etc., etc.
There is no doubt that the Labor Confer-
ence stands for much that is best and most
democratic in the Labor movement. (In-
cidentally it stands for much that is utterly
visionary and damphoolisli.) Still, take it
for all in all, and with its palpable faults
set against its manifold virtues, it voices
the opinion of the best sort of Australian
Labor voter. But to suggest, as the
Worker does, that it speaks for the whole
of the Labor voters of N. S. Wales, or for
anything like that number, or even, for that
matter, for a majority of them, is, unfor-
tunately, manifest rot and idiocy. The pity of
it is that Conference in nowise stands for the
Labor vote in N. S. Wales. The best that
can be said of it truthfully is that it repre-
sents the views of a big majority of the
Leagues. A huge Labor vote, which is out-
side the Leagues, as well as a sizeable
minority of the League vote, is resolutely
hostile to the opinion of Conference —a cir-
cumstance which is demonstrated afresh at
every election. The assertion that the voice
of Conference is the voice of Labor is
neither more nor less futile than the con-
tention that “Labor needs no leaders.” The
thing has a line, large sound; but it is com-
pletely meaningless. No enterprise on God’s
green earth ever proceeded a yard without a
leader. Sometimes a cause gets a capable
leader, such as Garibaldi, or Luther or
John Wilkes, and then it goes ahead like a
bush lire.. Occasionally a cause is afflicted
with a weak or shifty leader like McGowen
or Holman, and then it halts, and unless
the opposing leaders are very, very feeble it
falls dead. But always there must be some-
one running the show. For example, the
N. S. Wales Labor Conference has itself a
President or Leader —just as the Sydney
Worker has a Manager and Editor.. The
Conference consists of representatives of
the different State Leagues. Each of
the Leagues has its President or Ruler
or Chief or Dux or Boss. Each league
has various branches, and each branch
has its President or Mayor or Head
or Panjandrum. Many of the Leagues have
what may be called natural leaders —force-
ful characters to whose judgment they
defer with almost slavish docility. There
is no more uproariously independent lot . of
toilers, for example, than the waterside
workers. Yet at a word or two from that
small, deaf, dyspeptic, W. M. Hughes, they
often become as lambs. The leadership
question is a fundamental principle of
human nature that no amount of newspaper
skite can abolish. The Big Plea is an im-
perishable proposition; and the aspiration
of the Smaller Fleas to wipe out the larger
insect and run things on their own, while
easily understandable, can never be more
than a beautiful dream. The only move-
ment in which there are no leaders —any
doctor with experience in. mental diseases
will endorse this —is a riot or upheaval
in a lunatic asylum. Lunatics lose the
power of order and discipline and combina-
tion. It is about the. first instinct that, goes
when the mind begins to fall to pieces.
Therefore an absurdly small number of
weedy, ill-paid and. unarmed warders, be-
cause of their organisation, can jump on an
absurdly large number of robust lunatics.
Even Anarchists, who profess to want to
destroy all law and order, have leaders —
boss anarchists or boss destroyers or chief
abolishers.

Australia’s Bitterest Haters.
Australia is afflicted with an unpleasant
type of citizen that exists hardly anywhere
else in the world. He finds this country good
enough to live in. Sometimes he is able
to make money in it —though more often the
bulk of his income is remitted to him from
abroad on condition that he stays here. Oc-
casionally he is a native. Btit always, he
hates the Commonwealth and .everything as-
sociated with it, barring possibly its money
and its alcohol, with a loathing that he
would scorn to conceal. One of these unde-
sirables, ah imported man named C. E.
Jagomb, wrote an embittered book about
Australia the other day, and now we have
A. W. Pearse, of the Pastoral Review.
The burden of Pearse’s ( dreadful work
is the estimableness of the nigger, by
comparison with the average White Aus-
tralian. In his view, “the Pacific Islands
laborer is far more preferable to live and
work with than his white brother in Aus-
tralasia.” So are Chows. If his book ex-
presses Pearse’s true sentiments he would
marry his female relatives to coolies or Asia-
tics rather than to the -.sort of Caucasians
who, for good or for ill, haVe made Australia
what it is to-day. He hasn’t travelled enough
or read enough, seemingly, th comprehend
the significance of a nigger problem, and so
he is a Black Australian to the backbone.
The only hope he can see for the Territory
is coolie labor. Also, if he had his way,
our wharf-lumpers would be African negroes ;
our seamen Asiatics of divers breeds; and
the rest of our workers tamed savages of
one sort or another. The pastoralist would
thus be able to do better for himself in a
financial sense, in the opinion of Pearse.
True the race would presently become half-
caste \or. mulatto or something; and descend
automatically to the present status of Mexi-
co, Portugal and every other piebald nation.
But in Pearse’s view, as expressed in his
book, that would be a trifle. Australia
strikes him as a rotten hole anyhow. The
more he saw of foreign parts the more this
aospel of the immemorial remittance!* was
borne in on him. He would be glad, one
gathers, to get away from the loathsome
place and settle down happily in Ivorea or
Senegambia or Port Said or the Southern
States of America, where they burn one live
nigger every week-day and two on Sundays.
But he can’t. This is the only place where
he can earn what represents to him a living
wage: and so he comes back here and takes
it out of the hide of the country which pro-
vides him with a livelihood. On the same
day that the writer came across I eakse s
book the Interstate Commission was en-
gaged with what one may term a Commercial
or Trade. Peakse. Just as it is Pearse s
job to misrepresent, by any means fair or
foul, the cherished institutions of the country
where he elects to earn his living, so it is
the mission of G. A. Teale, of Melbourne, to
heap mud on its manufactures. Teale ap-
pears to have the same fatuous belief in his
slanders that is suggested in the furious
outpourings of Pearse. Teale, in support
of his view that Australian-made goods—the
work, that is to say, not of cheap niggers but
of white men working under decent condi-
tions —must necessarily be rotten, _ assured
the Commission, “that if Australian-made
three-ply veneer was soaked in water, the
veneer would come to pieces.” The. Com-
mission decided to test the truth of this, wild
allegation. As a result, Chief Commissioner
Piodington was impelled to speak as
follows: —
We have carried out the experiment as suggested
yesterday. All the samples of three-ply veneer sub-
mitted to the Commission have been completely im-
mersed in water for 18 hours, and then exposed to
the sun. As a result, the Australian-made three-ply
veneer, specially manufactured for butter-boxes, has
completely stood the test. On ervery side these veneers
are as good as ever before. The other veneers sub-
mitted by Mr. Stukrock (the local manufacturer)
have fared variously ; but, on the whole, may be said
to have completely stood the test. With regard to the
imported veneers, only two very small pieces can be
said to have stood the test in any way whatever. The
others have completely broken down under it.
According to the Melbourne dailies the Chief
Commissioner wound up by “expressing the
strongest condemnation” of Teale, he
having obviously entered into an organised attempt
to disparage an Australian manufacture. The result
of the experiments carried out by the Commission
would not allow them to put any reliance whatever
upon evidence that was evidently part of an organised
campaign of disparagement of Australian manufac-
tures.
The moral difference between the organised
attempt of Teale to disparage Australian
manufactures, and the organised attempt of
Pearse to disparage Australia is indiscern-
ible to the naked eye. Teale slande" j in
the interests of the importers; Pearse acts
as a sort of heeler for the cheap labor-seeking
pastoralist. Neither would be endured in
a starkly patriotic country like Germany.
There are times when this paper wonders
whether Australia doesn’t carry too far its
bland tolerance of the people who take away
its character in the intervals of accepting its
benefits.

The Bucolic Person’s Latest Grope
after a Fair Deal.
The formation of a Country Party in the
ranks of N. S. Wales Liberalism was one
of those things that had to happen before
long. The Holman crowd is sufficiently
Sydney-led, but the Liberals are in this par-
ticular respect the absolute and dizzy limit.
The explanation may be found by a casual
glance at the personnel of the Liberal execu-
tive —the body which lives in the classic old
Pitt-street cellar or excavation, or what-
ever it is, and which corresponds to the
Laborites’ caucus. Four-fifths of the mem-
bers are city or suburban property-holders,
of a more or less extensive sort, and most of
the remaining fifth consists of property-hold-
ers’ agents or heelers. What earthly pro-
spect of a fair deal has the Country from
such as these? The essence of all fiery poli-
tical enthusiasm is self-interest. Sometimes
it is a national matter, and as an outcome
of it an army and a navy are evolved. More
often it is a parish affair of higher or lower
wages, or a railway deviation. The main
immediate interest of every Sydney landlord
is to bloat Sydney to bursting point. What
may happen to property values later, as a
result of this unnatural process, is of scant
interest to the advocate of bloat; since he
expects, before the slump or other trouble
has arrived, to have realised at a big profit.
The idea of opening up to trade such price-
less harbors as Port Stephens and Jervis
Bay fills the type of citizen who controls the
Liberal movement with a kind of weary
gloom. He is unable, for the life of him, to
see the necessity for anything of the sort.
He is prepared to supply a dozen reasons or
more why the deed should not be done. The
scheme represents, to his mind, an interfer-
ence with the established order; something
equivalent to a proposal to sub-edit the
Lord’s Prayer. Any township that ever
eventuates on either of these provincial
harbors must begin by being an insignificant
place, and hence, he argues, it must remain
for ever small and paltry and miserable.
That is to say, whatever money is spent on
linking up Port Stephens and Jervis Bay
with the main railways will be wasted
utterly. Far, far better to outlay the bor-
rowed or other cash in Sydney. The country
dweller has grown utterly sick of these spe-
cious contentions. In his opinion they repre-
sent the last word in dull, mule-headed sel-
fishness. His wheat rots and his stock per-
ish in the attempt to force their way into
a city that is not equipped to receive them.
When he reflects how different things would
be if there were an outlet for his perishable
goods at the nearest big port, he seethes
with impotent annoyance. The opening up
of the great starved (ports of N. S. Wales is
one of the most obviously needed of local re-
forms, and it is only shelved persistently
because the vested interests of Sydney are
so powerful that neither party is able to
take a firm stand in opposition to them.
Failing an Elective Ministry, composed of
independent legislators who have no organi-
sation of earnest Sydneyites to answer to,
the formation of an effective Country Party
represents the only way, so far as the
harassed country resident is concerned. And
salvation isn’t a certainty even by means of a
Parliamentary country section. There is
the possibility of some of them proving
WADE-ites first and decentralisationists a long
way afterwards. In fact, The Bulletin
arises from a consideration of the names of
the members of the newly-formed organisa-
tion with a dejected impression that that is
just the sort of country representative the
majority of them are.
Carson the Paranoiac.
Max Adams has an opinion :
Everybody knows nowadays what paranoia is. The
term has been made colloquial by insanity pleas from
the case of Thaw to that of a certain Sydney police-
man. What about Sir Edward Carson, King of the
Smaller Scrap of Ulster, as a paranoiac ? The speeches
of the rebel barrister with the heavy jowl show him
more and more exalted in the belief that he is a
Heaven-sent instrument to scourge the Devil in the
shape of the Nationalist and Liberal parties.
Hark to some of the things which King Carson
has been saving in the past year or two. Already he
has visions of himself as a conquering Napoleon in
the field of battle. “I like to get nearer the enemy.
I like to see the men are preparing for what 1 call the
Great Day.” Here he is affected by a reflexion of the
national paranoia which Germans have caught from
their Emperor. That speech (made at Carney Hill, in
August, 1913) came , after a comic-opera series of
manoeuvres by part of his “army.” At other times
he has proved equally well that military megalomania
has got hold of him. The belief that Belfast intrigu-
ing has split the British Army in two is firmly rooted
in his mind; and pothing could be more like a
paranoiac delusion than this faith in a figment sup-
ported by so little evidence.
Carson is a trained lawyer, and a good one ; but
here his ego has so deceived him that he has become
incapable of estimating probabilities. “Action on the
part of the British Government will, I believe, smash
the Army into pieces, because it will divide the
Army.” (Speech at Ballymena, July, 1913.) “A day
never passes on which I do not get, on a really low
average half a dozen letters from British officers ask-
ing to be enrolled.” (Speech at Craigarvon, July,
1913.) “I tell the Government that we have pledges
and promises from some of the greatest generals in
the army.” (Speech at Antrim, Sentember. 1913.)
Quite early the Scrap-of-Ulster King had begun to
regard himself as the splendid rebel. In June, 1912,
he announced in England: “I intend, when I go over
there to break every law that is possible.” (Even hen-
stealing and pocket-picking were on his programme.)
A little later the inauguration of the “Solemn Cove-
nant” took place, with a grandiose ceremony attended
by armies—of soldiers? No; of reporters and photo-
graphers. They are the paraphernalia of paranoiacs
nowadays. The Covenant was attested under electric
light of 39,000 candle-power, for the sake of the mov-
ing pictures. The blaze of publicity sent King Carson
soaring thereafter into the first person singular. He
began to think of himself as the Cassar of a new
nation. “The whole of this matter is to me one of
the gravest responsibilities I have ever had in my life.
I am no thoughtless lad, trying to inflame bigoted
passions.” No; he was a GoD-given leader of great
events. “My own people in the North of Ireland,” he
called them with magniloquent proprietorship in the
Ulster Guardian, in December, 1912. Religious
ecstasy was soon added to the other stigmata of para-
noia. “There is the Covenant. There is the copy
I signed. I have never let it for one moment out of
my possession. I have read it many times, and I have
prayed that I might live up to it.” (Speech at Bel-
fast, May, 1913.) Finally the Covenant becomes a
thing that is entirely’his own. It is he, Carson, in
his own person who has made this league with
God Almighty. “I know nothing about legality or
illegality. All I think of is my Covenant. My Cove-
nant to me is the text and foundation of what is
legality and what is illegality.” (Speech at Craig-
arvon, July, 1913.) A month later in Belfast the
frenzied Captain Craig told the people that “Sir
Edward Carson had been sent from Heaven.” King
Carson was present. He himself by now seems to be-
lieve or almost believe in his Divine origin.
When the drunken bruiser in Masefield’s poem ran
round the place shrieking: “I’m fire of Hell come up
this minute to burn this town and all that’s in it,”
was he any more preposterous than King Carson?
Two doctors have sent men into asylums for opinions
of their own destiny far less extravagant than those
of the Monarch of the Covenant of the back-streets of
Belfast. If Sir Edward Carson’s liberty depended on
the diagnosis of his condition by a couple of Chis-
holm Rosses, I wouldn’t like to guarantee too big an
amount of insurance.

A Railway Complication.
By an old railway man: —
When Victorian Railway Commissioner Fitzpatrick
went abroad last year he seems to have made an honest
attempt to find improved safety appliances that would
make repetitions of Sunshine and Richmond disasters
impossible. Neither England nor the United States
have given the “automatic stop” much consideration.
In fact the Victorian experts reported that in England
it has been tried only in one or two places ; but where
in use on the lines of the Great Western Company 7
it has proved very successful. The European conti-
nental railway systems are far ahead of both British
and American railway companies in this respect.
But the Commissioner made last Wednesday week
the pleasing announcement that some “auto-stops” are
to be installed on the Victorian system for experi-
mental purposes, and if they prove successful and re-
liable they will be installed generally. To the average
person whose life will be in the keeping of these
automatic appliances it is all so much Greek. In the
beginning of railway signalling there was a great
struggle between the positive and the negative sys-
tems. Which, stated baldly, is so much more Greek.
But it is simple enough. Under the positive system
all lines are open unless the signals are at danger.
That is to say that the normal position of any
signal is “Clear Line—Go Ahead!” And the duty of
the signalmen under that system is to close a line
when it is occupied by a train. Under the negative
system, on the other hand, all lines are closed unless
the signals are at “Clear Line”; and the duty of
the signalman is to open the section of line if re-
quired only when that portion of line does not con-
tain a train. The normal position of the signals
is at “Stop—Danger.”
As a safety factor the system under which a num-
ber of manipulations of signalling instruments has
to be made to admit a train on to a section that is
clear should triumph over one in which a number of
manipulations are necessary to keep the same train
out of a section already occupied by another train.
And as it was with Block Signalling in the beginning
so it is now with Automatic Stops. There are many
systems of automatic stops; but the greater proportion
of them are positive—so that if they fail to act the
train dashes on in fancied security to utter smash.
The negative systems are few, and they are handi-
capped by being about 15 times as expensive to
maintain and three times as dear to install.
The best auto-stop system this writer wots of is
an Australian invention, which is now being ex-
tensively adopted on the continent of Europe. It
had nothing to do with the ordinary Block system.
An electrical contrivance in the engine itself keeps
the brakes off the wheels. Immediately the engine
comes within half a mile of one in front the electric
current is diverted, and, after the sounding of
a warning gong and the showing of a
red light, steam is automatically cut off, the brakes are
jammed on hard and the train is brought to a sudden
stop, to be automatically released immediately the
other train moves out of the circuit. The only
thing against this system is the cost of installation
and maintenance. It is expensive, and some ex-
perts fear it is super-sensitive and liable to hold up
trains in wet weather or when a rush of storm-water
has swept rubbish across the line or disturbed the
surface ballast —which, come to think of it, is
hardlv a defect.
It is pleasing to hear that the Commissioner is making
an effort to make the dangerous Victorian lines safer;
but it would be foolish to get wildly enthusiastic
over the announcement till it is known whether the
proposed automatic stops are worked on the positive
or the negative systems.

“0.K.” : The selection of Victorian candidates
for the Senate has got the Liberal leagues
and parties into another wrangle. Sam
Mauger, the hatter, has withdrawn his
name from the list of candidates on the
ground that the shemale section of the
caucus would not give him a fair chance to
be chosen. When the result of the poll is
announced some of the rejected ones will
certainly kick. Probably candidate Packer,
of the “Independent Workers’ Union” will
refuse to be snuffed out by a caucus vote, and
truly Packer will have cause for complaint.
A party that denounces preference to union-
ists as a crime against Liberalism ought to
pick Packer for its standard bearer. Didn’t
he advocate independence for his motley
gathering of “unionists” before preference to
trades unionists was ever talked about?
Doesn’t he believe in organised opposition
to organisation? Packer is the proof spirit,
the distilled essence, of Liberalism. If the
Liberal political unions refuse to select him
as a suitable person for the Senate he ought
to assert his independence as a worker and go
alone.
Portland (Vic.) had a gala day last week
to celebrate the beginning of its outer-harbor
works and of the Victorian section of railway
line which will connect that long-suppressed
district with S.A. Announcements of the
gala arrangements were a lovely satire on
the policy which a long succession of Mel-
bournian Governments has practised against
Portland. The capabilities of its harbor
could be taken for granted from the fact that
“H.M.A.S. Melbourne left Williamstown for
Portland to take uart in the celebration.”
But the awful inefficiency of its railway ser-
vice was admitted by the statement that Pre-
mier Watt and a political picnic party,
having left Melbourne at 11 p.m. on Wednes-
day, by special train, to attend the celebra-
tions at Portland on the following day, would
“leave on the return journey to Melbourne
shortly after midnight, and reach the city on
Friday morning.” And this Portland, so
easily approached by a warship yet so hard
to get to and fro by rail, is the place where
the Hentys landed 80 years ago, come next
November. If Watt had any poetic ten-
dencies he might have written an elegy among
the ruins of Portland’s once-prosperous gaol.
The Vote of Censure.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Len’s a pipe er baccer, Bill.
Siddown over there.
Bloomin’ contrac’s been ’ung up again.
Aw, there’s no use worryin’ ;
Whips er time to spare;
Blokes will ’ave their quarrils now an’ then.
’Ere’s them flamin’ managers
Scrappin’ like the dooce.
Playin’ at the same ole silly game.
Chuck yer pick an’ shovel down,
Wot’s the bloomin’ use?
We kin dror our wages orl the same!
Toil to do? Er course ther’ is—
’Eaps er solid work;
’Nough to keep us goin’ overtime.
But when bosses chucks it in
An’ begins to strike,
Well, fer me an’ you it ain’t no crime.
Ther’s that darn ole Tariff Wall
Ain’t been mended yet,
Said they’d ’tend to that long years ago.
Aw, don’t bust yer boiler, Bill !
You’ve no call to fret;
Pay-day’s cornin’ all the same, yeh know.
Yus, I’m pretty sick of it,
’Angin’ ’round the job,
While they squabbles all the ’ole _ day long.
’Ere, when one bloke does a bit,
’Nother silly yob
Comes an’ pulls it down, becos it’s wrong.
Then they starts to mix it up,
’Ell fer leather. Biff!
Yow! An’ goes an’ wastes a month in talk.
Only fer the pay I’d be
Orf in ’alf a jiff.
Struth! I’d pick me dunnage up an’ walk !
It’s the flamin’ system, Bill,
Got us in its grip:
Me, an’ you an’ orl the blessed lot.
If they don’t soon alter it,
Take my dead sure tip,
Things on this ’ere job ’ull go to pot.
Orl lars’ shift they forks a treat,
Gittin’ nithin’ done;
’Ere, this shift they starts to fork some more.
Jist sit back in comfort, Bill/
Stay an’ watch the fun.
Ther’s a bit er wages still to dror.
Dunno wot ’e’s thinkin’ of,
Bloke ’oo owns these works.
If ’twas me I wouldn’t wait fer munce.
Orl this brawlin’ crowd ’ud git
Swift an’ suddin jerks.
Out into the cold, ’ard world at once.
I’d not stand this sorter thing,
Not fer arf a day :
Runnin’ contracks at a dead sure loss.

MAMMA, DADDY AND CHILDREN ALL
LOVE “CALIFORNIA SYRUP OF FIGS.
Harmless “fruit laxative” cleanses the
stomach, liver and bowels without
any griping.
A delicious cure for constipation, bilious-
ness, sick headache, disordered stomach, indi-
gestion, coated tongue, sallowness—take
“California Syrup of Figs.” For the cause
of all this distress lies in a torpid liver and
sluggish bowels.
A tablespoonful tonight means all com
stipation poison, waste, matter, fermenting
food and bile gently moved out of your sys-
tem by morning without griping. Please
don’t think of “California Syrup of Figs” as
a physic. Don’t think you are drugging
yourself or your children. This delicious
fruit laxative cannot cause injury. Even a
delicate child can take it as safely as a robust
man. It is the most harmless, effective
stomach, liver and bowel regulator and tonic
ever devised.
Ask your chimist for a bottle of “Cali-
fornia Syrup of Figs,” which has full direc-
tions for babies, children of all ages and for
grown-ups plainly printed on the bottle.
Beware of counterfeits sold here. Get the
genuine, made by “California Fig Syrup
Company.” Refuse any other kind with con-
tempt. “California Syrup of Figs” is sold
by all leading chemists, 1/I| and 1/9-
Liberalism made a criminal mess of its pre-
election of the six Victorian Senate candi-
dates. As a preliminary, the ballot was
bungled, the vote being taken by each of the
three component leagues separately, as the
central body declared it hadn’t enough funds
to carry out the job. Then the undertaking
that no ‘‘ticket” was to be issued was freely
broken. The Argus next printed its own
little list—the sole recommendation of the
favored ones being their hopeless Toryism.
Mauger, seeing how the land lay, slung in
his alley. The ballot closed on May 2, and
of course the selected candidates will claim
to go on the ticket, and the unsuccessful ones
will say the interference damaged their
chances, and Mauger promises to run on his
own, anyhow. So, what with one thing and
another, matters are about as orderly as a
midnight row of black cats on a stranger’s
roof. By way of a sporting event this paper
gives its vote for Samuel Mauger. He
is a manufacturer, an employer of Australian
labor, a fairly good Australian and a good
Protectionist. Ilis brand of piety may be
an offence and his name a misfortune, but
the reason why Samuel M. doesn’t get on
with the crowd he belongs to is that he is
immeasurably superior to most of the gang.
“Sea Lawyer” writes:—
Sydney’s Marine Court, with the aid of an army of
legal talent, has been investigating a Port Jackson
bump. Two steam-lighters, towing punts along Darl-
ing Harbor, collided violently, with the result that
the air became full of profanity and the harbor
swollen with sunken punts. After weighing the evi-
dence the Court pitched on one of the lightermen as
the offending party. This luckless wight, who held
a master’s ticket, was severely lectured by the Chair-
man of-the Court, and his certificate was ordered to
be hung up for three months. This action, according
to Judge Murray, was intended to serve as a warning
and a deterrent. Still, as the law does not require
those in charge of steam lighters to hold a master’s
certificate, I don’t observe where the deterrent pro-
poses to obtrude itself. The lighterman in this case
need only hand in for three months the document
which he happened to have, and go on, in the mean-
time, galloping his recent lighter (provided the
owner is satisfied), or another lighter, or his old
punt, or a fresh punt, up and down the Port Jack-
son course. I don't know whether the Marine Court
was aware of that. Probably not. There’s a whole
pile of things that same old Court isn’t aware of.
“The Heathen”: During the evenings
of last week, the Anglican clergy of
Melbourne had the Scriptures explained
to them, in the dimly-lit St. Paul’s
Cathedral, by Headlam, the imported
Moorhouse lecturer. The surroundings, as
described by the uneasy daily paper reporter,
were reverently dramatic : :
The patter of feet is heard, those assembled rise,
and Dr. A. C. Headlam ascends the pulpit. The
lights are lowered, notebooks come out, and, in the
semi-darkness, clergymen and other students endeavor
to put on paper the passing points. Embryo sermons
are there by the score. Standing practically motion-
less, cloaked by his academic gown with its scarlet
divinity hood, Dr. Headlam details the Christian case
for miracles, and combats adverse criticism. He is '
no orator. The voice has little gradation of tone,
save that the leading Christian arguments are stated
in a kind of triumphant chant. Subtle sentences
lose themselves half-way down the nave, and the
sound travels only to the shadows near the roof.
And, of course, the shadows don’t contradict
him. If there is anything calculated to give
a plain person noises in the head and specks
before the eyes it is an involved theological
discourse in the usual dim light. I have a
vivid recollection of one such performance
in the private chapel of a college. The
audience was a group of devout laymen (I
was a mere intruder), and the lecturer an
aged cleric who sat huddled at the
foot of the altar, and never once looked his
listeners in the eye. His method of concen-
trating his thoughts was to let his head
101 l on his chest and shake it occasionally
at his feet. He had the appearance of a
man who had been hung. After a quarter
of an hour’s darkness and mumbling and
stone-cold draughts. I felt quite dead. And
although I listened with both ears and all
my intelligence, I couldn’t make plain com-
mon sense of him from any angle. I have an
idea that he defended in some tortuous way
the ancient, art of lying ; but I won’t swear
to it. I think t I would have laughed at that
holy professor if the lights had been turned
up. A dim light and a cold, dank atmos-
phere are absolutely essential to all such
seances.
The Oldest Inhabitant:—
A recent paragraph in a Sydney newspaper recalls
an old-time tragedy, the author of which was never
discovered. The paragraph related to a legal action
regarding the will of Mary Hoadley, who owned
property in King-street, at the rear of the Australian
Bank of Commerce, three houses known as “Hoad-
ley’s Buildings, 1842,” as we used to read on the
parapet. Mrs. Hoadley was murdered in her bed
early one Sunday morning in 1845, the witness to
the tragedy being an adopted daughter (who became
Mrs. Robert Fitzstubbs, and who died but recently
at Mosrnan). A man named Cadman was tried for
the murder, but acquitted. The old lady, the widow
of a butcher, who had disappeared from Sydney some
years befqre, had made her will in favor of the
adopted daughter, but had not signed it. Solicitor
James Norton, who had prepared the document, satis-
fied the Court of Mrs. Hoadley’s intentions, and, as
she had neither kith nor kin in Sydney, and the
butcher husband was still in a state of vanishment,
the adopted girl obtained the property. Miss Maud
Fitzstubbs (Mrs. .Woods) and Mrs. Bladen, widow cf
the late Public Librarian, are daughters of Mrs. Fitz-
stubbs. I’he Australian Bank of Commerce recently
erected a fine structure on the site of Hoadley’s
Buildings.
