Gilbert and Sullivan’s “Mikado” is the
rich inspirational uncle of “The Mayor of
Tokio,” now flourishing at the Adelphi (Syd-
ney). But as Gilbert and Sullivan will
never collaborate furiously again, and are
big enough to be considered Old Masters, and
as imitation is the sincerest form of wor-
ship, no hard words needs be said about
librettist Richard Carle and composer Wil-
liam F. Peters. Indeed, Carle might even
be rebuked gently for not getting a little
closer to Gilbert —say as close as Peters has
got to Sullivan. “The Mayor” is one of
the brightest light musical shows Sydney has
been offered during the last year or so. The
story is one of those narrative clothes-lines
that anything can be pegged on to; but it
never breaks off utterly and trails in the mud
of dullness, and the songs and dances that
flap in the wind are worth their places.
Then the co., though it includes some weak
members, has a few whose work is excellent
and occasionally brilliant. Miss Grace
Ellsworth, as Madame Stitch, the wild, wail-
ing wardrobe woman and ex-ballet dancer
of an American co. travelling in Japan,
proves herself a genuine comedienne with a
sense of humor of her own. She is remin-
iscent of a woman character in “Mrs. Wiggs
of the Cabbage Patch” —that is to say, the
Cabbage Patch female is the only character
who is in the same weird class. Mr. Car-
rick Major, as Marcus Kidder, impresario
and manager of the co., is a genial comedian
who doesn’t work too hard to make his
points ; also he possesses .a fine baritone voice
which he uses cleverly. His easy, confident
manner is most noticeable when he removes
his battered comedy belltopper, ties a red
handkerchief round his head brigand-wise,
and then sings a difficult and serious ballad
with considerable success. Bobby Woolsey,
as Rusty, the song-book boy, supplies a
character sketch of a familiar American
type, and works well with Eva Olivetti. She
is Birdie Talcum, a bitter soubrette who
attributes the failure of the co. to the fact
that she wasn’t allowed to play Juliet. Amy
Murphy, as Oloto San. daughter of the
Mayor, has the most picturesque woman’s
part, and does a deal of duetting with tenor
George H. Bogues. George looks less like
a love-making tenor than any stage singer

“Sealed Orders,” the J. C. Williamson
.Ltd, bellowdrama, is still raging at Sydney
Royal, and if the supers’ clothes and uni-
forms are not as correct as they might be,
everybody concerned tries to conceal the
deficiency by shouting at the top of his or
her voice. (Almost anything can be con-
cealed by making a noise. There was once a
hostess who, through the neglect of a house-
maid, found her drawing-room encumbered
with the dead bodies of three poisoned lovers
on her day at home. Just as the first batch
of guests was admitted, she threw up her
hands and screamed at the top of her voice.
The function passed off quite successfully,
and, if the guests noticed anything amiss,
they were too well-bred to mention it.)
* * * *
There are two points of interest this week
at the Palace (Sydney). One is the revi-
val of “The Colleen Bawn” and the other is
the replacement of Allen Doone, as Myles
Na-Coppaleen, by Harry Roberts. Doone has
a bad throat and needs a rest. The Bulletin
doesn’t know a more hard-working showman.
Dion Boucicault’s old drama has been re-
ceiving the welcome hand, and might have
been with advantage put on earlier in the
season, when Doone was well. It suffers
with Doone audiences from lack of Doone.
Roberts approaches his work at a right
angle to Doone, as it were. In the beginning
he was nervous, as he always is when start-
ing a season; but no amount of confidence
would have placed him on the right side of
a Doone audience. As a matter ,of cold fact,
no understudy of Doone could hope for howls
of applause in the Palace. The pest piece of
acting in the old-timer is that of'Tom Buck-
ley, as Father Tom. An appealing player at
any time, he is especially recommendable as
the parish priest in this piece. Edna Keeley
also earns this paper’s approbation (for a
change), and; no harsh remarks are passed
over the footlights to anyone in the company.
The programme at the National Amphi-
theatre (Sydney) is no worse than it was
last week ; and last week it was one of the
best The Bulletin has been introduced to
in the hall. With that compliment the
paper removes its lid and bows to the man-
agement.
These are not jimjams. They're Lauder's
Scotch walking-sticks.

A similar roll-up is billed for to-morrow

* * * *
There are several good points about the
Six Camerons at Sydney Tivoli, but the
best are (1) they don’t sing ragtime; (2)
their turn lasts only 15 minutes or so, when
there’s nothing to prevent it lasting 30;
and (3) there are only six of them, whereas
there might easily be ten or a dozen. There
are a few other good points, but those are
the main ones. The Shentons, a trio of
rather good step dancers ; Ed. E. Ford, “the
Australian sundowner” ; Fields, the silent
humorist; the Elliott Savonas and Sam
Stern are the props of the remainder of the
show.
At the rear end of this week Wirths’ Cir-:
cus will put away its tent, fold up the hippo-
potamus, stow away the lions with a few
moth balls, bank the cash and move on. For the
benefit of the various people who have just
come out of gaol or for those who have seen
the show five or six times and want to see it
again, it might be mentioned that, till the
date above hinted, it will continue at Prince
Alfred Park, Sydney.

If all the admirers of Barry Lupino, Marie
Eaton and Edwin Brett, whom the manage-
ment of Her Majesty’s, Sydney, was re-
gretfully obliged, during last week, to turn
away from the doors, cash in hand and seeth-
ing with disappointment and annoyance—
if all these people, we say, were placed on
the ground in a straight line, head to heel,
they would stretch from the front step of
the pub two doors from The Bulletin office
to the police station at Oodnadatta (S.A.).
If they were heaped one on top of the other,
in Hyde Park, or the Domain, or in the
middle of the Never Never Desert, or in one
Pf our other open spaces—however, to put
it shortly, the pantomime is going stronv. The
writer has said this before, and he hereby
asserts it again, and anyone who gives him
the lie must take the consequences. He can
have them whenever he likes, but should he
be found taking anything else that doesn’t
belong to him there will be trouble.

The revival for one week of “The Fortune
Hunter” at the Criterion affords Sydney
another chance to witness a pleasant comedy,
one of the best produced during Fred Niblo's
long stay in Australia. Niblo is one of the
finest and most finished actors we have had
here. His “business” in this play is peren-
nially delightful. Josephine Cohan, as the
heroine who knows how to scrub, again fails
to make herself quite audible, and thus the
audience misses much of her dainty charm.
Plumpton Wilson takes for the first time
the character part of old Sam Graham; and
his work is solid and sincere. Another
change in the cast, and a strengthening one,
was Sydney Stirling as the promoter. The
rain was, as usual, quite wet.

Good acting is the great feature of the
present Williamson opera season at Ade-
laide Royal. That of the three women in
the first act of “The Chocolate Soldier,”
Florence Young, Celia Ghiloni and Dorothy
Brunton, was striking; so was that of the
new recruit, Robert Whyte, in various parts,
ranging from an angry general to Peter
Doodv. A co. that can quietly drop Frank
Greene and Miss Young, as this one did for
“The Arcadians,” proclaims its strength.
Dorothy Brunton, who should have a big
future, definitely took a leading place as
Sombra; but she gets one black mark for
playing the obviously comic and middle-aged
charlady in “The Balkan Princess” as a
“straigth” part—in fact as her own charming
self. Reginald Roberts is just suited by
the modern convention which allows singing
lovers to have of humor too. At the
King’s are various clever Brennan-Fuller
turns, mostly American ; the Valerio people
on a wire; Fanny Rice taking a wide range
of characters in her own jolly way: Crotton
and Ivremka,. Leslie and Sol Bern fun-mak-
ing. The brightest turn in the variety line,
however, is. that of Vallecita at the Tivoli,
the prevailing uncertainty as to which of
her leopards will bite her, and when, giving
it a sort of gambling interest. The gilded
Athlqnes are now showing their good form
or forms at. this house. The Orpheus So-
ciety has given a brilliant evening at the
Exhibition, in support of a Carnival for the
blind, that is to rage for a fortnight; and
at the Town Hall Waller’s Butterflies, under
Wondergraph ‘‘direction,” have .a steady and
profitable public for anything they care to
put up.

Still another of Victoria’s big alluvial gold pro-
ducers has struck its doom. This is the great
Southern Mine at Rutherglen, and on the 13th and
14th of May next the loud voice of the auctioneer
will be heard to proclaim that the whole of the goods
and chattels of the company (now in liquidation)
will be sold, or knocked out, or down, or some-
thing without mercy. The plant is one of Vic.’s
biggest efforts and cost a fabulous sum to install.
Fortunately the claim was no duffer—something like
£400,000 in divs. came to light. The plant con-
sists of a full rig-out in every detail necessary for
up-to-date alluvial mining. The power used was
exceedingly heavy, and was produced by both steam
and electricity. Application to Somer and Dabb,
Maldon, Victoria, will bring forth a detailed cata-
logue of 500 lots.*

Alexander Watson’s stay at the Athen-
aeum Hall (Melbourne) must have been pr -
longed under pressure from General Desire
or Carlyle Smythe. Anyway, the versatile
recitalist is still there, drawing treasures
of literature from his caverns of memory and
entertaining his admirers for 90 minutes aDd
more at a stretch, without pausing for a
drink. “The Everlasting Mercy” (three
times did the Mercy last for ever) is the
only programme that Watson has repeated,
thus far, but to-night (Thursday) he gives
“Richard II.” once again.

Harry Lauder’s month at Melbourne Prin-
cess’s ends on Friday night, when the audi-
ence’s final chorus to “A Wee Deocli and
Doris” should be the sweetest and neatest
noise of its kind that ever happened under
local vaudeville conditions. It is always
guid to see and hear the braw, bandy-legged
apostle of insobriety lure a mixed gathering
of citizens into song, but on Friday nicht it
will be truly gran’. Lauder, ye ken, is a
magnetic musical comedian, who, being a
verra reespectable mon himsel’, appeals most
forcibly to the least disrespectable sections
of the community. Therefore his finest hum-
orous effect is in the fact that his spontane-
ous choristers infuse a tone of sanctity into
“A Wee Deoch,” etc., as though it were an
intemperance'hymn. On Saturday and Mon-
day the co. appears at Ballarat, with Ben-
digo, Geelong and Horsham in prospective.
Drama resumes its sway at the Princess’s
on Saturday, and a new American company
will come to light. E. J. Carroll (theatre
arrangements with Geo. Willoughby, Ltd.)
takes great pleasure in announcing the first
appearance in Australia of “The Rosary.” His
pleasure in the announcement is due to the
• a^oresa id having been an easy winner
in America wherever it ran. It carries what
the advt. describes as “a virile religious senti-
ment” warranted not to give offence.

cut. off. Also the gangway would be blocked
as it never was blocked before, and a grue-
some tango would result.

SYDNEY ADELPHI.
The Mayor assents to the marriage of his
daughter with the Sporting Tenor.
“What Happened to Mary” is eventuating
for the last times at Melbourne King’s. Mary
goes on Friday. She is the most guileless
girl on record, so, by way of restoring public
opinion to its normal balance, Anderson will
revive “The Worst Woman in London” on
Saturday. In the Bert Bailey productions
virtuous womanhood is apt to run to ex-
tremes, and the reputation of the theatre
demanded that a sincerely vicious heroine
should get _ another look in. “The Worst
Woman” will be assisted on this occasion by
a number of vaudeville items, and her wick-
edness will be, if possible, more pronounced
than before.
The Ada Reeve season at Melbourne
livoli got further stimulus on Saturday
from the Two Bobs, who unblushingly claim
to have been the pioneers of ragtime in Eng-
land. Other members of the co. are the
same as before. It is gratefully remarked of
Ada Reeve that she has no ragtime ditties in
her repertory. She talks, sings, acts con-
siderably, dances a little, and does it all
without changing her frock. But she rags
not.
* * *
The chief attraction at Spencer’s, Mel-
bourne, last week was “The Curse of War,”
a rather unconvincing view of a modern
scrap with aeroplanes supplying the bulk of
the unpleasantness. “The Outlaw” proved
to be a mere retailer in the same line of
business. His efforts were greeted with
hushed cheers, as also were the activities of
“The Pote Lariat of ‘X’ Ranch,” a film
which showed how the hardest boozer of his
district suffered from a rush of poetry to
the head.
** * *
“Jean Sibi” :
J. J. Ililder has been showing a bunch of his pic-
tures at the Melbourne Centreway. A gifted artist
with a highly educated color sense, the Sydneysider
contrives to knock the bottom out of the superstition
that common, lowly toil is too ugly for pictorial
presentation. One has only to view his set of
observations in a clay pit to realise that plain Bill
with his barrow and his bowyangs may be made a
thing of perfect beauty. “A Little Landscape,”
“Suburban Landscape,” and “A Country Post Office”
are notable for their color and workmanship, and
the serene beauty of “The Plough” is undeniable;
but, generally speaking, every effort in the show de-
mands a bouquet. Judged on this display, Hilder
stands eminently above the ruck of local contempo-
rary workers in his medium.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Nietzsche”: Masefield’s “The Everlasting Mercy,”
which Alexander Watson recited at the Melbourne
Athenaeum lately, indicates the amount of life that
a person of drive and initiative can pack into 30
hours. The hero’s exploits during that period in-
clude a prizefight, an affaire de lust, three drunks,
two fits of dangerous insanity, in one of which he
assaults an inoffensive town with a fire-hose, and a
conversion. It is towards the fag end of the third
drunk, the hour being 11.30 p.m., and the scene a pub
parlor, that he generates the inner light. After-
wards he wanders about in the night and broods
darkly, as exiled drunks will do, and the curtain
falls. The closeness of the relationship between
religious inspiration and dingbats is well illustrated
in this poem. The fact that Watson wasn’t arrested
for “language” also illustrates the circumstance that
this offence is merely a matter of location. Also,
the show illustrates a number of other things which
I can’t call to mind at the moment.
“0.K.”: After hearing Walter Kirby sing at one or
other of the Saturday Pop Concerts at Melbourne
Auditorium, a lot of people have been wondering
why that strangely non-typical Australian doesn’t
return to Britain and earn 10 times as much money
as he can hope to pick up in his native land. Per-
sonality counts for much on an Australian concert-
platform, and Walter's personality seems to have got
on the nerves of his countrymen and women. He was
something of a prophet in his own Melbourne ere he
went to Europe a few years ago. The women, any-
how, adored his voice, and made a pet of the young
man. When he returned, with the same sweet voice
and a vastly-improved method of using it, there was
no rush to hear him. The bit of reputation he had
made in London didn’t help him at all. People
objected to his effeminate airs and graces off the
platform rather than on it. His name was no draw,
and for a long time he rarely appeared at a public
hall or theatre except as one of the audience. And
then people casually remarked that they thought he
had gone back to England. He was singing only at
private houses. On Good Friday he ran a highly
unappreciated sacred concert at the King’s Theatre

Malcolm Tearle saves a woman's name by
(earing up an incriminating letter under the sea.
before an audience who would have preferred nigger
minstrelsy. But his voice and his art were beautiful.
Then the Taits engaged him for their Pops, and a
more musical public is now reluctantly consenting
to be charmed by his performance. Still, there is a
general disinclination to “rave” over Walter Kirby.
Any visiting tenor, with a hard, old voice and a
shoutful top note, may be raved over for his singing
and for himself —but not Kirby. He ought to be
singing these times to a world that would know him
only as a supremely sweet warbler with a much better
stage appearance than the average Italian tenor. The
quality of his voice is rich, the production is effort-
less, and the notes are as smooth as glass. The sym-
pathy in his rendering of the music and words is
feminine as compared with, say, John McCormack,
but, then, Mac is quite a rough vocalist as compared
with Kirby.
E. J. Carroll (of Queensland and New South
Wales) has how in Australia two of his long string of
shows for 1914. Le Koy, Talma and Bqsco, the
world-famous illusionists, commenced their sea-
son at Perth on Saturday, and Harrington Reynolds
will produce “The Rosary” in the Princess’s, Mel-
bourne next Saturday. The latter co. arrived
from U.S. by the Ventura on Monday, and went on
by rail the same evening. “Just time for a whisky
and a so-long,” as Reynolds complained. Other
Carroll shows in the immediate distance are “Bunty
Pulls the Strings,” Cosmo Hamilton’s “Blindness of
Virtue,” and a dog and pony circus.
An interesting experiment in the show business in
New South Wales is the Country Amusements, Ltd.
At least, it will have that name when its formation
is complete. Up to now the country towns have had
to take any old thing in the way of entertainment
that came along, and be thankful, even if it was the
worst show on earth. Generally it was. Reynolds
Denniston (of the late Plimmer-Denniston combina-
tion) and one or two others have an idea now that
the time is ripe for giving 23 of what might be
termed No. 1 towns high-class plays that have
attracted the metropolis. The selected towns are
Gunnedah, Narrabri, Moree, Inverell, Glen Innes,
Armidale, Tamworth, Singleton, Muswellbrook, Bath-
urst, Orange, Forbes, Parkes, Goulburn, Cowra, Young,
Murrumburrah, Wagga, Cootamundra, Dubbo, Wel-
lington, Yass and Alburv. In each of these one perform-
ance will be given on the same date every month by a
good co. The shares are to be held in the various
towns—lso in each and no person may hold more
than 15. As nearly as possible, every play that is
successfully produced in Sydney will be taken on
circuit by Country Amusements Ltd. It looks an
alluring proposition for 23 communities that have
been more or less show-starved up to the present.
Mr. Denniston would have commenced a preliminary
tour of investigation last week, but postponed it
owing to being specially engaged by the J. C.
Williamson firm to appear in the drama, “Sealed
Orders.”
From the agony column of London Times: “I
have the best drama, full of comedy, written since
‘The Silver King.’ I want it read by somebody with
money, enterprise and intelligence. The Tin Gods
of the stage are unapproachable, save through their
underlings, who have sawdust where brains should be.
There is £lOO,OOO in this drama. (Signed) A. G.
Hales, war correspondent and novelist.” The signa-
ture might have been that of any other than the
unquenchable “Smiler.” The humanity of the docu-
ment lies in the fact that it expresses in the fewest
possible words what every dramatist on earth thinks
about his own particular Bellow.
“Melb ”: At a picture show I have just seen the
finest bathos ever imported. It is called “Faith in
the Flag.” First there is one of those New York
schools where immigrants of divers orders, either
sex, and every age and every language, are taught
the New York variety of English. On July i the
schoolmistress gives each a little U.S. flag on a
stick, and says. “Remember that no harm can come
to anyone who is under this flag.” Carita, a poor
Italian widow, gets one. She lives in a squalid
place called Hennessy’s-lano. with her little son and
her mother. The child is taken very ill. The
Italian doctor holds out no hope. She lays the
flag upon the child, but, fearing there -may not be
enough of it, goes out to buy a big one. Her money
is insufficient, and she is in despair. O’Sullivan,
a retired army surgeon, greatly values a huge old
banner which was with his regiment in the civil
war, at Chattanooga or Chickahotniny. His wife
washes it, and hangs it over a rosebush in the gar-
den. Carita runs off with it, but a newsboy sees
her, and follows her home. Then he goes to O’Sul-
livan, who is on the track directly. He wants to
recover the standard that flapped at the battle of
Chickabiddy . Lick. He pushes open the door of
Carita’s home, and sees the flag spread over the boy,
while the two women kneel by the bedside. Carita
cries “Mother! I believe this is a doctor. No harm
can come to anyone under the flag.” And the doctor
cures the boy. After that there is much “Hail
Columbia,” and pork and beans, and spreadeagle,
and some Bourbon whisky, and a corncob is eaten
all round, and addresses are delivered by Elijah I’o-
gram, and Jefferson Brick, and Colonel Starbottle,
and. Jack Hamlin, and the Outcasts of Poker Flat.
Finally there are three cheers •and a cocktail.
“Harrison O.”: Melbourne Repertory Theatre has
doubtless done something to assist the local play-
wright, but it might have done a lot more. There
would seem to be little justification for the present
system of producing foreign plays in the Theatre
Royal, while relegating local efforts to the
barn-like St. Peter’s: Director McMahon says
that he gets a different class of audience for
the Australian drama, but there is no reason
for supposing the class to be less numerous.
Full audiences have been attracted to Aus-
tralian-drama nights at the Athenaeum and St.
Patrick’s, which was not always the case when
imported highbrows held the stage at these
places. The foreigner has now been given the
advantage of a first-rate theatre, while the
local writer has to be content with a pocket-
handkerchief stage in a holy ball. If he had
been tried in one of the regular theatres, and
the audiences had been found wanting, this
treatment might be justified, but as it is it
looks as if the Repertory management is merely
using the Australian writer as a sop to the
local patriot, whose subscription the. theatre
wants. In the matter of casting, too, the
home-grown author is apt to receive less than
justice. This was the case with Alfred
Buchanan, whose “Tmagc Breaker” was put on
the other night. It is true that the manage-
ment is not altogether to blame for this, as
the Repertory ranks include a weird type of
amateur mummer who refuses to squander his
or her histrionic talents on anything- that does not
bear the merit certificate: “Made Outside Australia.”
One way and another, the indigenous dramatist is in
need of heaps of enthusiasm to sustain him.
The following letter—or, maybe, it is an ultima-
tum —was received at this office during the week: —
Editor Bulletin.
Dear Sir, —I am going to be an actress how do I
get there ?
Yrs. truly, ANXIOUS.
“Anxious” (she doesn’t supply any name or address)
is so dead sure that she’s going to be an actress that
it seems a blank waste of time on her part to ask a
completely uninterested third party how she’s going
to “get here.” (The reader will notice that, with a
splendid feminine disregard for details, she neglects
to specify where she wants to get—or where she
intends to get unless stopped by the police.) The
editor of The Bulletin passes on the warning to
theatrical managers everywhere. At any moment
“Anxious” may launch herself from the void at
whatever stage she is aiming at, and start being
some sort of actress. It is depressing to dwell on,
but it can’t be helped. If any person finds a totally
unexuected actress anywhere during the next week or
so, she will most probably be “Anxious.” But, then,
nearly all actresses are Anxious. Those who are not
Anxious are Dashed Worried.
(Continued on page 86.)
“Adelaide”: A Social-reform Bureau, so-
called, has had the nerve to ask Adelaide
City Council to decree “that no persons be
allowed to remain in the parks around the
city after 10 p.m. in summer and nine in
winter.” The letter was merely “received.”
The contract is too big. To say nothing of
squares and gardens, Adelaide’s park lands
are from a quarter to a third of a mile wide,
ringing the city with a belt perhaps eight
miles long! But the important point is the
open suggestion that the thickly-scattered
thousands haunting park or river-bank on
warm evenings are there for any improper
purpose. To say, as one city councillor did,
that they are after “a breath of fresh air ”
is to blink the question. Their purpose is,
in the vast majority of cases, frankly flirta-
tious, and why not? Nothing is alleged
against the young people who do their court-
ing in dimly-lighted drawing-room or conser-
vatory, and a democratic country ought to re-
sent the labelling of every girl as immoral
whose parents don’t possess those apart-
ments, and whose only chance of privacy is
the open air.
THE PROFESSOR AT THE PANTOMIME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
And he remarks on our fare: “During
the whole of the year there is produced in the Aus-
tralian theatres those pantomimes which are only
given in England from Christmas to Easter.” It
seems to surprise him that people “in the prime of
life” should be content with this sort of thing.
—News item.
The wandering Professor turned him round within
his stall,
From which he watched the pantomime of “Adam;
or, The Fall” ;
The ballet of “Temptation” was the scene then
being staged,
And as he viewed that leggy void the good man was
enraged.
“Tut-tut!” he said. “Oh, pish!” he said. “Of
course, they’ll have their fling;
But fancy grown-up folk content with this-sbrt-of-
thing!”
The ballet was succeeded by the comic Angel-Coon,
Who put salt on the Serpent’s tail and sang about
the moon;
And, after flirting hard with Eve, the Angel-Coon
withdrew,
Asserting that: “Ah want that gal—you bet your
stars Ah do!”
What time the zithers and banjoes went “ping-a-
ponga-ping.”
“My godfathers!” groaned the weary Professor.
“Isn’t it lamentable to think that grown-up
folk can listen to this-sort-of thing!”
Next time the curtain rose upon the Valley of the
Apes,
Wherein the chorus wandered round in most attrac-
tive shapes,
Till put to flight by hideous beings falling in a
loop
From off the gorgeous scenery—the Great Euphrates
Troupe!
“Oh, sainted mother!” moaned the Prof, who watched
them sway and swing;
“This is monstrous — incredible ! —one ought to write
to the papers to protest against the utter folly
of grown-up folk who patronise this-sort-of-
thing.”
“Surf-bathing in the Rivers Five,” the next big scene
was called.
It pictured angels bathing—the Professor was
appalled! . .
“Most inartistic —oh, absurd!” he muttered, in a
pet.
“God bless my soul! Imagine angels with their
plumage wet!”
Once more the chorus wandered on—their feathers
seemed to cling,
Which fact drew from the Professor the caustic
remark that there were far too many feathers on
the small angels, but nothing to what he could
see on the hats in front of him; at the same
time, it was scandalous that people should go
to see this-sort-of-thing.
The good man’s indignation was not lessened in the
least
When in the fourth act (Scene Nineteen)) they put
on “Satan’s Feast” ;
The scene was laid in Hades, and the devils all were
seen
Arrayed in horns and tails and hoofs, with flames
shown in between.
Here Mammon and Beelzebub—the Bounding Brothers
Byng—
Brought down the house with comic gags and busi-
ness, but the Professor simply glared at them
and said: “In the name of Art, and by the god
of Dramatic Criticism, how can grown-up people
sit and watch this-sort-of-thing?”
The sternest sense of duty only kept him in his chair
Until the Anal moving scene, “Eve Cuts Her Hus-
band’s Hair” !
In this the chorus, quite disrobed—bar haloes, wings
and spears—-
Encircles Our First Parents, driven forth in leaves
and tears.
Here the Professor woke right up; some joy it
seemed to bring
To his discouraged heart, for he smiled, and was
heard to remark that, after all, though himself
no wowser, a chorus like that was almost angel-
like, and perhaps there might be some excuse
for grown-up folk who were content with this-
sort-of-thing !

SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
Hospital Saturday got it right on the
point this year and its barrackers took the
full count. With an army of 5000 female
street-cadgers, the whole foolish and insult-
ing jamboree realised only £7717, as against
£BO3O last year. Sydney Telegraph, which
has done its share of the H.S. boosting, now
has serious doubts about the whole busi-
ness. (It might easily have had the same
doubts years ago and not waited for a drop
in the takings ; still a death-bed repentance
is better than none.) The King-street
organ, in its issue of May 4, took itself
firmly by the ear, and, leaning far round its
own neck, asked itself a series of pertinent
questions. It suspects “a growing feeling
that the requirements of the hospitals and
public institutions of a great city like Syd-
ney ought not to be dependent in any degree
upon the chances of a collection from the
passers-by in the streets.” And again. "A
conviction that this is not a business-like
method of dealing with the situation is pos-
sibly felt by a considerable section of tne
public.” Thus the ’Graph, after many years
of blasphemy by every man that has had oc-
casion to use the streets, has a feeling in its
bones that the system whereby 5000 allegedly
respectable women pester and hold up
a city during a whole day for an
equivalent of 3d. or less per flying inhabi-
tant is a waste of time. Perhaps the paper
had a suspicion long ago that it was quite
futile; but. of course the safest course for
a daily paper is to ascertain first what public
opinion is and then lead it from behind like
the Duke in the Gilbert and Sullivan comic
opera.

“Hash” writes: —
One man of impoverished luck is a Maoriland
lawyer wanted for the alleged misappropriation of
some £3OOO. On February 28 last his address in
a certain one-horse burgh in Otago, failed to
find him when J. Hop arrived. He had gone for
change of air, and the sleuths had to look wise and
smile tolerantly while irate creditors made caustic
remarks. For six weeks blank silence reigned, and
then came the news that the wanderer had been
arrested at Hokitika. His first flitting carried him
by a second-class ticket to Lyttelton, where, in
sailor garb, he arranged for passage as a deck-hand
in a sailer bound for Newcastle (N.S.W.). Fate,
however, thrust forth her front foot and delayed
departure by bad weather. After two weeks the
insolvent Columbus developed anxieties, and shipped
as purser on a small coasting steamer bound for
Greymouth. With a first-class discharge in his
pocket he then sought a timber carrier for Australia,
but that front foot of Fate’s was still protruding
most unpleasantly. To avoid the water-front Gentle-
man One sought Hokitika, and played there the part
of the gentleman-at-large, which was about the worst
part he could play. Pending “his departure for
Australia on a sailing vessel for health reasons,” he
made himself very agreeable to the townspeople;
was taken by the Mayor to the races; was
invited by leading merchants to the golf
links; and finally asked to be introduced to
the police, and discussed with the sergeant
certain aspects of criminology. In a word, he ran
wild, and instead of being a humble marine officer
waiting with a glass of gin and water in a cheap
hotel till his ship was ready, he became a sort of
Public Howl. The sergeant of police was at last
driven to notice the garish visitor who was so con-
spicuous in his desire to avoid publicity, and the
usual thing happened. It almost always does.

Australia once owned a Baptist or Dip-
per parson named Spurr, who has now a
cure of souls in London. This person—so
it is cabled —has been holding forth in Bull-
opolis about the “absolute uselessness” of
our defence forces, because “Australia could
do nothing with her amateur soldiers if
China or Japan made a descent upon her.”
Australia’s young army may not amount to
very much as yet, still Baptist parson Spurr
was absolutely useless 40 years ago. And
he will be absolutely useless 40 years hence.
Also, he isn’t much use now. And if. the
Devil made a descent on him next Friday
he could probably do nothing. Australia’s
army, poor as it is, looks a great deal
stronger to-day than the Christian Church
did when it was the same age. And if par-
son Spurr considered this country to be in
such danger he shouldn’t have run away,
even if a higher salary was offering. He
should have stayed here, and died at his post
when the invasion came, and gone to
Heaven, and got a crown of gold, and a
lien on the golden streets, and a fishing
license on the Jasper Sea.
* * * *
Macleod, the chieftain of the Country Clan
in Victorian Assembly, seems likely to be
“chucked” by his Daylesford constituency at
next election. Daylesford is much perturbed
by the bursting-up of the famous
old Hepburn Spring which has
been one of the district’s chief
attractions for tourists. The
spring, though not lost, is gone
to pieces of late. The trouble
began in the underground opera-
tions of the new Hepburn
Mineral Water Co., the bottling
works whereof are at the back
of the Spring Pavilion. Macleod,
M.L.A., is considerably inter-
ested in this company. In fact,
the Hepburn folk think of
member Macleod as their worst friend, also
the rest of the electorate begins to think with
Hepburn. The local Progressive Association
is now urging the Minister of Lands to do
something or other towards rectifying the
wrongs inflicted on the good old spring, and
if he does nothing—well, the Daylesford
voters will naturally suspect him of having
had his ear bitten by their Parliamentary re-
presentative.

The Oldest Inhabitant: —
S. M. Herald’s historian is once more in a muddle.
This time he talks of a “Sir John Young” trophy in
bowls. Sir John Young, afterwards Lord Lisgar, was
Governor of N. S. Wales in the ’6o’s. John Young,
the “father of bowls,” of Sydney, was eminent as
a contractor, but never achieved knighthood. Still,
the contractor’s job is useful, and the business of
being a knight is a bewilderment to the intelligent.
Few litigants have the luck that befell
George William Turner in the Sydney
Divorce Court last week. He was asking for
peace and freedom on the grounds of his
wife’s habitual drunkenness. In the middle
of his narrative the lady herself burst into
the court, and with the prefatory “ ’Scuse
me!” that so surely indicates the portent-
ously genteel stage of inebriation in certain
females, gave him the lie direct. Later she
had to be ousted, and as she went, struggling
manfully, she yelled: “I’ll blow his brains
out. I’ll shoot him.” And in due course she
was arrested and taken to the Central on a
charge of drunkenness. Of course the peti-
tioner got his decree. It was as though the
accused in a robbery trial had suddenly made
things easy for the Crown Prosecutor by pub-
licly picking the pocket of the Judge.
* * * *
“Chasuble” speaks :—•
The Brigidine nuns have just rather startled the
public by purchasing Matthew Boom Davies’s former
mansion at Mentone, the Melbourne watering place,
where they first plunged by purchasing the fine
coffee palace. The Orders run a race. First came
the Sisters of Mercy. Started with a bit of a
shanty in Nicholson-street, Fitzroy. Now they have
a magnificent church and immense convent. The
Little Sisters of the Poor, starting also with nothing,
have their palace for the indigent at Northcote. The
vast Abbotsford Convent has 800 inmates. The
nuns captured Chalmers Presbyterian Church, East
Melbourne, where Dr. Cairns exulted in fulminating
against the Scarlet La-a-dy. They tore down his big
church and steeple and built the materials into their
convent. The Good Shepherd nuns came in a ridiculously
small way from Sydney. They bought the splendid
Fitzroy Congregational Church, tore it down, and
built St. Evins’s Hospital. Before this they built
the noble St. Vincent’s Hospital close by, and it is
just extended with what is called the Druids’ Wing
over a side street. The Sisters of St. Joseph win
sympathy as an Australian order. They are great
in school and charitable work and buildings. It
would take much space to mention the Melbourne
edifices of the nuns. Most important of all are the
great institutions, such as the Loreto, South Mel-
bourne, and the Novitiate, Ascot Vale, for traimng
day-school teachers.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thieves evidently regarded the Easter holidays as
an opportune time for their activity. Scores of rob-
beries were committed in the areas that are regu-
larly patrolled by the police. —Melbourne Age.
They pinched a bloke in Little Bourke for cursing
at a cop ;
They lumbered Leary Liz, who’d been partaking of
a drop ;
They copped a bunch two-upping; and, before that
day was spent,
They pulled a kerbstone-bookie and a beggar, blind
and bent.
They did all this, and more,
While burglars by the score
Popped in and out of likely cribs, each to his heart’s
content.
The boys who came home bawling in a joyful, beery
bray
Found John Hop waiting for ’em in a most persistent
way;
And those who smashed up crayfish on the cushions,
late at night,
Were staggered when the watchful Law fell on them
in its might.
But certain sports there were
Who never had a scare
Although they broke the Law with ease in spots
ablaze with light.
The man. who meant to meet a mate outside the
Royal Mail
Got moved on to the watch-house, where he had to
wait for bail;
The lad who lingered for his love nearby the Book
Arcade
Was pushed along, and missed her —for the Pleece
must be obeyed!
And all the time, at will,
The talent tried its skill,
At busting cribs wide open and collecting cash and
trade.
They pinched a bloke in Little Bourke, that home of
dreadful vice,
Because he spoke a sergeant in a manner far from
nice;
They caught a girl in Lonsdale-street, and stormed a
piping Chow,
And caught a loafer resting, which the Law does
not allow;
But meanwhile, as before,
Mere burglars by the score
Hopped in and cracked more cribs.... No doubt
they’re cracking others now.
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.
TUB MAD BATTEH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dr. Schultz, of Mayence, predicts an epidemic of
female baldness if women persist in the present
type of headgear.:— Cable.
Fair woman’s lost her modesty,
Say saints who ought to know;
She’s lost her head, but not her hat,
Her hair’s now doomed to go !
As maiden young or matron wedded,
She’ll go for what she wants bald-headed!
Of hips and fine, seductive curves
She’s rid herself with pains.
Her dress she’s shedding rapidly ;
Her temper, though, remains!
She may her figure little wear on,
Say I, if she’ll but keep her hair on!

“Leges”:—
The Rules Committee of the Supreme Court of
England, which includes the Lord High Chancellor,
Lord Chief Justice, Master of Rolls, President of
Divorce, Justice Pickford and sundry eminent bar-
risters and solicitors, has drafted the regulations of
a Poor Persons Legal Department. Indigent citizens
in quest of justice must fill in a form. Counsel will
then consider it, and decide whether there is a case.
If so, the proper legal men will be,briefed at Govern-
ment expense. This will not be so costly as might be
supposed. There is an enormous shoal of briefless
barristers, many hanging on for a number of years
without one brief. Most of them will be glad to get
the advt. of appearing in a case pronounced by the
Crown authorities to be good. The regulations per-
mit such aspiring men to violate etiquette by taking
briefs without fee, and so much will be saved to the
Crown. Provision is also made for putting through
divorce proceedings on the dead cheap.

“Germophil”: The destruction of the
Druid’s false beard looks to me more than a
perishing of old traditions; it is wholesale
murder. Life was contained in those whis-
kers, which descended from func-
tionary to functionary. In one
set of fringes that I wot of were
stowed away the diphtheria that
slew three children of P. G. M.
Zerubbabel Pottinger ; the tubercle
that carried away Right Worship-
ful Axebearer Hannibal Katupna
Spoopendyke; the cancer that af-
flicted Chief Swordsman Titus
Trebbins ; and I don’t know what
other of God’s creatures may not
have had their habitat there. When
the beard is burned, not only shall
we lose memories of Zerubbabel
and Hannibal and Titus, but we
shall also commit a holocaust of
living things.
“The Heathen”: I struck it
again the other evening below the
newspaper portrait of a hard-faced
old Worker for the Good Cause.
The interviewer, who seemed to be
bursting with admiration for him,
capped his praises by describing
him as “a God-fearing man.” Can’t
understand why no Christian ob-
jects to the phrase. Why should
the Almighty be everlastingly re-
presented as something to be feared,
and the Worker as a cringing
coward ? Bernard Shaw refers some-
where to the popular conception of
God as “a very vindictive and
jealous old gentleman.” Yet the
advertising evangelists, when they
are urging people to come and be
salvaged, represent Him as infi-
nitely merciful. If the religious
press still clings to the “God-fear-
ing” insult, the daily press at least
might drop it. It seems to
belong to the far-back time
when Christianity was the religion of
slaves who lived more or less in a perpetual
state of terror, and the Allseeing was sup-
posed to personally “lay for” every unlucky
sinner with various afflictions. The pro-
strate attitude of mind engendered by such
beliefs has held up such good worldly works
as the modern medical crusade against
syphilis. The “God-fearer” should be out of
date by now.
A Melbourne accountant has just received
a three years’ sentence for embezzlement, the
total- shortage being £3OOO to £4OOO. Some
30 years ago his father was a well-known
solicitor in a leading Western District (Vic.)
town. One morning he had his breakfast
as usual, said good-bye in a casual fashion,
and departed for his office a few streets away.
He was never seen again. The whole dis-
trict was stunned when it was found out that
some of the biggest adjacent squatters had
been rooked for large sums. The Melbourne
accountant was his youngest son. Shortage
is hereditary, like phthisis. In proof thereof
witness the public debt, which is handed
down from one generation to another.

“Croson’s” protest:—
It is only the other day that a Melbourne husband
was shown to have placed his too-regularly-inebriated
wife in a South Yarra “home” for six months, with
the idea that the healthful discipline of the washtub
would do her good. ' The Court elicited, simultane-
ously, that the managers of the Home believed they
were legally entitled to forcibly detain the lady on
the husband’s authority. An even more amazing
narrative has just emanated from St. liilda Court,
where Mrs. Tapscott sued her husband for mainten-
ance for herself and her four babies. Tapscott is an
alcoholic plumber. Nevertheless he successfully
stalled the wolf off the family doorstep until last
December. At this stage Beer got the
mastery. Still, he managed from time to time to
send his wife various sums. Then his mother and
sister took a hand, and incontinently shoved him
into the Inebriate Asylum at Lara for six months—
and without troubling to tell his wife either. Even
after he was committed these relatives didn’t con-
descend to inform the lady most concerned, and it
was only after he had been away five weeks that she
discovered what had happened to him, and where
he was. In the meantime, the wife had to keep
herself and four small children. The St. Hilda beaks
inspected Tapscott, and learning that he was legally
a prisoner in the custody of the Master-in-Lunacy,
adjourned the case in order that more light might be
shed on it. It is the sort of case that calls for a
lot of light. If the existing law allows two women,
with no shadow of legal control over, or legal claim
on a man, to have him secluded at Lara without the
consent or even the knowledge of those dependent on
him, it is high time that law was passed down and
out. Meanwhile it would be pleasing to learn how
many more compulsory inmates Lara possesses, and
who put them there, and why, and for how long, and
all about them.
* * * *
Years ago, before the specially-built ambu-
lance cart and its trained and uniformed
attendants became a clanging commonplace
of the Sydney traffic, the unfortunate who
happened to'be bowled over in the street was
lifted into the first available vehicle and
trotted off to the hospital. Then the ambu-
lance service was organised, and sympa-
thisers with the fallen were imploi'ed not
to transport them roughly to the doctors,
but to wait patiently for the First Aid men
and their special chariot. The idea is good;
but (1) there are not many ambulance wag-
gons; (2) what there are seem to be kept
fairly busy shifting invalids; and (3) Syd-
ney is a very crowded and awkward city
to get round or through. The result is that
the street victim has often to wait and bleed
longer than he used to in the old rougli-but-
ready days. The other night the writer
saw an old man, whose arm had been taken
off by an electric tram, waiting many preci-
ous minutes for his regulation vehicle, while
available lorries, motors and carts passed by
the place where he lay. It was one of
those desperately urgent surgical cases where
a few minutes might mean all the difference
between life and death. Two days later,
the daily paper mentioned briefly that the
old man had died. Still he went to the hos-
pital in style. #
The Oldest Inhabitant writes about an inn
and a few sundries :
Another bit of Sydney city property belonging to
the poor Cooper family comes on the market in
May—the south-east corner of Bathurst and George
streets, with a big frontage to the latter street, which
takes in seven freehold lots, including the Crystal
Palace Picture Theatre. The site is an historic bit
of old Sydney, inasmuch as that on the corner,
in many years gone by, was the Australian Brewery,
run by James Wright, and in close proximity to
Talbot Inn, from which started coaches “for the
country.” “The country” then meant Parramatta and
Windsor.
There may be no truth in the statement
that Jeremiah Strongleman (aged 80) died
of ill-usage in the Sal, Army Home at Pak-
enham (Vic.), but there is just sufficient
suspicion about the matter to create an im-
pression that there is no joy in being an
“inmate” aged 80. And the possession of a
name like Jeremiah Strongleman is about
the last straw on the back of the camel of
misfortune.
A WAIL FROM PENTRIDGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Harry Lauder went to Pentridge prison last Friday
and entertained the male convicts for two hours.
—News item.
Me name is Dicky Dolbird, and I’m a-doin’ time
For makin’ of me livin’ by committin’ of a crime:
And ’ere in peaceful Pentridge I was easy for a spell,
For the simple life in chokee used to suit me fairly
well,
When a Scotch bloke —-’Arry Lauder —wif a bloomin’
toonful voice,
Bein’ under the himpression that ’e’d make our
’earts rejoice,
Come and sings us songs of boozin’ and the ’eather
and the trees,
And the tarts that sez “Don’t tickle!” w’en you get
’em on your knees,
And of ’ow ’e felt so jolly w’en ’e brekfusted in bed.
Well, ever since he done it, I’ve been wishin’ I
was dead.
I can bear it w’en the parson talks of Paridise and
things,
An’ tells me that I’ll one day be a flappin’ snowy
wings.
For there ain’t no earthly comforts in the world he
prates abowt,
An’ he doesn’t leave me pantin’ wif me tongue a-
hanging owt.
But Lauder’s songs, Gawd ’elp me! filled me up wif
vain regret,
Me throat is parched wif longin,’ and me blanky
cheeks is wet,
For the whisky, an’ the feedin’, an’ the sorcy little
dears
That no bloke ought to think of w’en ’e’s doin’
seven years.
When McNab left off a cuddlin’ the braw lassie ’e
’ad wed,
An’ begun to squeeze a bottle—oh, I wished as I
wos dead.
* * * *
At last this ’Arry Lauder got ’em singin’ all they
knew,
Just as if we’d Dock and Dorissed, and was feelin’
“fou the noo.”
My ’eart was full to burstin’, and me brain was in
a w’irl
As I thought abowt me sentence, an’ me own dear
little girl.
The Dock was wot I stood in w’en me Doris caught
me heye
And shouted: “Buck up, Dicky! We’ll be married
by-an’-bye.”
That drinkin’ song at partin’ was the ’ardest blow
to bear,
It burst me up completely, an’ it left me in despair.
I meant to serve me sentence like a game ’un, on
me ’ead,
But I’m weepin’ like a babby, an’ I wishes I was
dead.

ferent suburbs, and, ns an outcome of
the deliberations, a decision not to ob-
serve in Into the association’s prices was
arrived at.

VICTORIAN VIEWS.


THE MILK-WHITE FLAG.
Mr. Stanley F. Allen, described as “Hon. Sec., Australian Freedom League, Sydney Counell," has written to the press stating that: "If our young nation chooses to act in
a fair, upright, honorable way towards other Powers there will be no need for such a system (compulsory military training). It is thus explained to us that if any trouble arises
we must be to blame, as nobody ever does anything wrong except ourselves.
Mr. Allen is here represented as dwelling in the white house of a blameless life. The flag is intended to imply that he is inside
and actively employed in living up to his principles.

“Siren’s” note: —
Died suddenly on May 2, W. G. Todd, a victim of
the big Sydney ferry strike of 1913. Todd was
manager of the Sydney Ferries Co. at the time, and
being a militant individual set out to smash up oppo-
sition. In his enthusiasm he misled the directors as
to the weakness of the co.’s position. An inglorious
capitulation followed after a few days’ fight Todd
felt the defeat keenly, and left the co. He wasn’t in
good health then, and his bad health never became
much better afterwards.
M.L. House of Reps, will lose a conspicu-
ous figure in June when its present Clerk
Henry Otterson, retires. He will then be
o», and will have completed 44 years in the
Parliament’s service. He joined in 1870 as
a committee clerk, and two years later was
made reader. In 1875 he became second
assistant clerk, and in 1889 first assistant.
In 1898 he became Clerk of the House, and
has held down that billet ever since.
After that silly display which Monsieur
Guillaux, the French flying man, gave at
Newcastle, when he pretended to be drop-
ping his machine on a compllny of cadets, it
is proposed that he should be known as
Giddyox.
“Nemo” :
Cabled that two of the “chamberlains of-cape and
sword ’ on duty at the Vatican during the previous
week were William Humphrey Page, Knight Com-
mander of St. Gregory, and Alexander Itawlinson,
Knight of St. Gregory. They were well known in
social circles in Australia a decade ago, more especi-
ally in Tasmania, where they settled for a time, and
where their bosom friend and fellow-Komanist, Vis-
count Gormanston, was Governor. Page had been a
Judge in India. Rawlinson was nothing in. particular.
They both had money and charming manners, and
were in consequence popular. Page lisped, whereas
Rawlinson didn’t. They and Gormanston were familiar
figures at mass, vespers, benediction and I don’t
know what else in the old cathedral at Hobart.
Rawlinson now lives in Rome. Page has settled down
in Bruges (Belgium)—his son having become a
Jesuit priest. Gormanston is dead. Which about
exhausts the human interest of the trio.
HE FRIES, Minister of
Railways, Maoriland.
Francis Bathurst Suttor, President of
the Legislative Council, celebrated his
75th • birthday on April 30. He was born at
Brucedale, in the Bathurst (N.S.W.) dis-
trict ; is a son of W. H. Suttor and grandson
of George Suttor, who was the protegd of
Joseph Banks, and the friend of Governor
Bligh. As a side line he has established
something of a record as a parliamentarian,
having been in one or other House, with a
short interval, for about 40 years. lie held
ministerial office for 12 years, and served in
seven Cabinets—Parkes (Ist), Parkes
(2nd.), Jennings, Dibbs (2nd.), Dibbs
(3rd), Lyne and See.
Cabled news of George Doughty’s death in
England was a matter of interest to many
people hereabouts, especially in Melbourne.
The knighted Doughty, M.P., married
Eugenia Stone, who formerly wrote the Mel-
bourne woman’s letter for The Bulletin.
He was a shipowner in the fish trade, and
he represented Grimsby in the House of
Commons, where he was accepted as an able
speaker on the subjects nearest to his pocket.
At the time of his marriage to Miss Stone,
some seven years ago, George Doughty was
said to be already an ancient person, but
the cable message stated that he died in his
60th year.
An old Mrs. Lauder who died in South
Melbourne the other day, was a pal of Adam
Lindsay Gordon when she was a girl. Her
father, John Bright, gave young Gordon a
billet' on his station in the Long Desert
(S.A.), and the newchum told the family
how he come to be packed off to Australia
from Cotswold, England. As a student at a
military college he was promised the mount
on a horse that had been backed for a prin-
cipal event at an amateur race meeting. The
owner of the gee gave orders that it was not
to be started, but Gordon took it from its
stable and won the race. The owner and a
policeman were waiting for him when he dis-
mounted, and the “horse stealing” incident
ended in Gordon being exported as a bad egg.
“A.H.A.”: The late Argyll was the only
duke that I ever mistook for a waiter. He
was a good waiter, too. I happened to be
invited' down to Windsor with a Maoriland
Coronation contingent, who were the guests
of the Windsor Fusiliers, or Royal Busby
Guards, or something equally swagger. There
was the usual lunch, and my party
found itself at a side table, in a mixed com-
pany of Maoriland and British officers,
titled ladies and a lord or two. My friend
and I were doing ourselves well when we
noticed that the waiter had overlooked us
with the wine. A small, insignificant, ex-
ceedingly busy waiter was doing his hurried
best to keep the glasses full. I beckoned
him and hinted at champagne. He rushed
for a new bottle and sprinted back, filled
our glasses with a practised hand.' and fled
to other thirsty visitors. When the toasts
began, I noticed that the waiter was seated
on the chairman’s left and that gorgeous
individual or personage or popinjay
began his speech with “Your Grace,
ladies and gentlemen.” Later, when the
waiter rose, he made the finest speech at the
luncheon. In fact, it was the most sensible
speech I have ever heard a waiter or a Grace
deliver.
“Jeames”: Sydney Sun headed its cabled
references to last week’s proceedings against
Crosland and Lord Alfred Douglas “Earl and
Author.” Sydney Telegraph went one
better next morning by cross-heading its
cable on the subject “Charge against a Peer.”
Also it referred to Douglas throughout as
Douglas.” The stormy son of Oscar
■Wilde’s enemy and ultimate destroyer is not
an Earl, and he is not a Peer; nor is he
Lord Douglas. The only person with a right
to the latter title is the Earl of Home. If I
referred to the Premier of N. S. Wales as
Mayor Holman there would be cries of deri-
sion. and were I to term Hugh I). Mclntosh
the Dean of Stadium I should find ridicule
heaped upon me in large quantities. Why
isn’t the same standard applied in other
respects? The absence of it casts over the
metropolitan newspapers something of the
atmosphere of the cheaper Yankee rags.
By the Court Reporter:—
It is mentioned casually in the papers that the
just deceased Duke of Argyll left no direct heirs. lie
married (amid great surprise! Louise, fourth daugh-
ter of Victoria I. Now in the gospel of Court eti-
quette it is legitimate for a British royalty to marry
any other kind of royalty, provided the latter isn’t a
colored heathen. (This etiquette isn’t quite so strenuous
as it was, but it is still fairly awful ) The husband
may be desperately poor—thus the Battenberg whom
Princess Beatrice Wedded was reported to have been
reduced to cleaning his own boots—but he ought to
come of a line which wore some species of a crown.
He may he dethroned, but if his family was once
on any kind of .a throne he is ,still more or less
eligible. Germany was cut up into about 2000
kingdoms of one sort or another till Napoleon I.
made an unholy smash of them, and the descendants
of the 2000 more-or-less kings still have the shadow
of the old glory about them. Even now there are
25 States in Germany, and the smallest potentate Of
the lot, Henry 24th of Reuss, governs 122 square
miles of country, with a population of ?3,000.
Royalties are more common than beer in Germany,
and that country supplies an inexhaustible reservoir
when a husband is wanted for the tenth
daughter or a wife for the 14th son of
a very abundant royal family. But the
Argylls, though they trace their pedigree much
further back, than the present British reigning family,
and could'bpy uo all the Battenbergs before break-
fast, royal. There was .an . old kingdom of
Argyll, but the ancestors of the .present gang weren’t
kings there. There are still • dreadful, impoverished,
no-account MacDougals earning their bread in the
cheap suburbs of,; Glasgow and Edinburgh who can
look down on the'Campbells of Argyll in the matter
of royal pedigree. So there was great excitement
when the deceased "Duke, who was then Marquis of
Lorne, - was mentioned as a husband for Princess
Louise. But, so the lsgend went at the time, the
Court; doctors had certified that Louise could never be
a mother, and that smoothed the way. The degrada-
tion of mixing royal and non-royal blood would only
last through one lifetime. The pair were married
and lived ...happily ever afterwards for all that any-
body can *'sh6w to the contrary. But, after that, the
Victorian Court resumed its old, unflinching atti-
tude, a,nd' .Princess *. Beatrice became the wife of
Battenberg., He came of a, line which had ruled oyer
a selection,’ and he was, a Mumble! crushed person
who knew his place. His place was on the hardest
chair, andithe one nearest the door. Perhaps it was
through \ye|i-iness of the hard chair and the draught
that he went away on a sudden military expedition
to Africa, where, he died.

Peacock, of t the Victorian State Ministry,
told a country audience recently that in his
youth he humped .a , gr'qcer’s basket 11 hours
a day for Bs. a'week. Which explains his
awful Laugh. Peacock’s Laugh must be his
fiendish methqd of revenging himself upon
society for the wrongs inflicted on him when
he was a grocer’s boy.
John Alfred Northmore, K.C., has been
appointed to the vacancy on the Westralian
Supreme Court Bench. He is a native of
South Australia, and served his articles and
took his LL.B. degree in Adelaide. He be-
gan practice in Westralia in 1896, and en-
tered into partnership with the late F. W.
Moorhead, who was raised to the Bench,
when paralysis had got hold of him and he
was unable to stand. There are other
things to Northmore’s credit, but this is a
busy week.

Sydney Telegraph.
MRS. VELLYGRAPH: “Now, listen to me, young
Joe; you must learn to be'ave yusself! If
I cops you playin' wiv that narsty 'Olman
kid again I'll break yer somethin ’ neck! ”
Edwin Smith, Adelaide’s grand old man
(or one of them), has had to resist almost
by force a plan to pay him the stupendous
compliment of erecting a statue while he
lives to see it. The plan originated with
the Commercial Travellers —he is president
of the Australasian Association —but would
have been carried out with enthusiasm by
the many athletic bodies he has been good
to, and public bodies of all sorts. (Just as a
side-line, he was Mayor of Adelaide five
times, and in Parliament over 30 years.)
However, he vetoed the idea, and it has been
dropped. The dailies remark approvingly
that the Exhibition, the Torrens lake, the
Victoria Square and a few other such
achievements are all the monument he
needs. Still Ed. Smith should have given
way. It is something to get out of the
rut. There was a Persian king who put
up a world’s record by being crowned before
he was born. It would be an equally re-
markable record to be the only man—above
all the only Smith —who had a statue
erected to him, against his will, before he
died.
Adelaide has at last lost the amazing
veteran, T. S. Reed, who was born three
years after Waterloo, lived a fair average
life (48 years) in England, was then sent
out to Australia with lungs “hopelessly”
affected, and put in another 48 here! He
was in daily work till the end, as secretary
to the Geographical Society of S.A., and
used no glasses. Every summer (including
the one just ended) he went on a fishing
trip. His memory was full of distin-
guished names. Among folk he had met
were Kossuth, Rowland Hill, Whewell,
Brunei, Leech, Dickens, Thackeray, Grisi,
Mario, Jenny Lind, Douglas Jerrold,
Nathaniel Hawthorne and Napoleon 111.
One of his pet claims was that, as
a link between the centuries, he could
get back to the execution of Charles
I. in three lives. His grandfather, whom
he remembered well, was born in 1730, and
there was then living a man who had seen
the king beheaded in 1649. A more prac-
tical link with the past which he treasured
was a bit of the skin of Queen Katharine
Parr, presented to him by some good-natured
ghoul who had been present at the opening of
her grave in Westminster Abbey. There .
ought to be a law against this habit of steal-
ing queens’ skins.
HUERTA’S APPEAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
President Huerta has appealed to the rebels to
unite against the American invasion. —Press item.
QUEENSLAND.
Carranza on the land,
And Sam upon the sea ;
I guess that things appear
A trifle warm for me.
So, rebels, here you have
Huerta’s manly paw ;
I 'only beg you help
To smite the Yankee jaw!
II do not beg you take
The mincing paths of peace,
JBut, merely for the nonce,
From civil strife to cease;
-Abstain for half a jiff
From shedding brothers’ blood,
Until the Yank you biff,
As surely patriots should ;
And when he’s belted home,
And finds his grab was vain—
Then Viva—for our Land,
And “At it —boys!” again!
From rolling Rio Grande
To Vera Cruz the gay,
We’ll start with much eclat
A fresh, fraternal fray;
For, though we’d joy to spank
The Gringo for his greed.
Yet, when we’ve downed the Yank
Our brother has his need.
And so I’d make it clear
To all the Aztec brood
That things like foreign war
Are but an interlude.
And so the sooner we
Drive this invader back,
The sooner shall we take
The blood-stained local track.
And by our streams that flow
With Aztec blood amain
The nations round shall know—
Our Land’s Herself Again!
PHILANDER F.

Since the Australian Labor party started to stir up
matters in connection with the C.S.R. and insist that
'there were only two classes out of whom the com-
pany was making its vast profits—the consumers and
ithe farmers—-the directors have adopted the new sys-
tem of distinguishing between the profits made in
•and out of Australia. For the past six months the
'total figures are cut up this way: Profit made in Aus-
tralia, £133.761 ; ditto in Fiji and Maoriland,
£130,000. Of course, this paper accepts the figures,
although the management might have some difficulty
in convincing, say, Justice Higgins that the division
iis anything but quite arbitrary. If they wanted to
•do it the directors could perhaps make the Australian
profit £183,761, and show as good reason for it.
For once in a way the invariably doleful directors
•admit that things are not absolutely rotten. “The
•output of sugar, both in Australia and Fiji, far ex-
ceeded that of any previous year....No change in
■prices has taken place. . . .prospects for next season
:are, on the whole, satisfactory.” However it would
be amazing butter in which the C.S.R. management
•could not find a cockroach. Though the Australian
and Fiji crops were a record the Commonwealth
imulishly refused to stuff itself with extra sweets
'in the interests of this company. Consequently as
there is still some sugar imported from Java,
•etc., the C.S.R. had to carry over some of its
'stock. And some of the Fiji bumper production
which Maoriland also inconsiderately refused to absorb
ihad to be sent to Hongkong. This year also the co.
threatens to be harassed by excessively good crops,
with the distressing result that it has been able to
•make contracts to supply Canada with some of its
•requirements. Prices realised for these over-sea ship-
ments are admitted to have been satisfactory. Never-
theless “the large production in Cuba is now tend-
ing to depress values in the world’s markets.”
For the half-year a record profit of £263,761 is
'shown. These are the net earnings. How much is
provided for the replacement of depreciating assets it
is impossible to say. Some years ago the company’s
'policy was to build up a visible depreciation fund,
but when it reached half a million the new practice
was adopted of writing down assets, which means
that the provision for replacement is now hidden.
'The capital is £3.250,000, which includes £825,000
presented to shareholders by way of bonuses during
the past 74 years. The 12) p.c. dividend therefore
means to the bulk of the shareholders something more
substantial than that. Not, of course, that the bulk
•of the shareholders get more than 12) p.c. on their
investment, for in spite of the threats of Governments
and the tears and gloomy forebodings of the managing
•Jeremiahs the market puts these shares in the most
Iheavily gilt-edged class, and reckons that a return
•of 5) p.c. on the market price is quite sufficient. The
size of the undertaking can be reckoned up from the
ifact that stocks of sugar at 31st March stood at
£1,138,941. Another aspect is presented by the fact
•that the employees’ provident fund now takes totals
£200,383. The company may know how to squeeze
'both ways, but it at least knows how to very well
treat its salaried agents who do the squeezing.
According to one yarn, told by people who
•ought to know something, the Swifts, who are put-
ting a quarter of a million into meat works in
Brisbane, are not Yankee, so far at least as their
financing is concerned. That, according to this
story, is done by the cosmopolitan Rothschilds. This
•doesn’t make things any better for one lot of Aus-
tralians—the fire and marine insurance people. It
makes things rather worse, in fact. There are no
American fire insurance offices in Australia, and with
only American money in the meat business the Aus-
tralian offices might have had a cut. Hitherto
the Australian meat cos. have been, on the whole,
good Australians in this respect, and most of their
policies have been Australian. The Rothschildren, how-
ever, have a fire and marine insurance office of their
own, trading in Australia under a well-known Eng-
lish name—which probably explains whv Australian
offices are getting quite as cold a reception as Aus-
tralian beef and mutton will have presently. Car-
cases are not the only things that will be frozen
stiff when the foreign exploiters are ready.

Under the scheme by which the affairs of the bank
were arranged after the financial riot of the early
90’s, the preference shares’ prior claims to their cut
out of capital in the event Of liquidation ceases on
the payment of 14 half-yearly dividends of 7 p.c.
There are three more to pay. Then prefs. and ordin-
aries will rank alike except that the prefs. will have
first claim to a 5 p.c. dividend. At present 7 p,c.
all round is earned with ease; the surplus profits iff
excess of the dividend were sufficient to pay another
6 p.c. The premises account is, however, very
weighty for a bank of this size, and the management
is dropping into a very good habit 'Of writing it
down out of the undivided earnings, deposits and
other liabilities total £4,439-,46&. The feash, bullion
and other liquid resources amount to £2,078,787.
That Is to Say, the bank holds 46.9 p.c. of its liabili-
ties in liquid Shapes. There have been times when it
seemed to this Ipaqmr that the Colonial was not main-
taining a sufficient proportion of cash and securities as
good as caSh. But no complaint at all can now be
■made on that score; 46 p.c. allows of a very fair
margin for the expansion of business. From another
point of view the position is reasonably good: in all
the bank owes £4. while the capital and re-
serves, which ate the funds that insure the creditors’
money, amount to £663,765. Pretty well everything,
of course, depends upon the securities in which the
advances and other funds have been invested. You
cannot tell merely by looking at a balance-sheet
whether Brown is good for the overdraft of £15,000
which lie has been lucky enough to get. The only
test possible is as to the profits earned on the basis of
capital and reserves combined. If year in ahd year
out they are somewhere about 8 p.c. or more it is
reasonable io assume that the securities are hdt geft-
erally overvalued. In this case here are the figures
for the past four years:—
Profits as Compared with Shareholders’ Funds.

This very energetic company has made large changes
in its capital account during the past few years,
partly, no doubt, because it was finding good use for
more money, partly because the extra cautious man-
agement knows that a 5 p.c. dividend is likely to
attract much less hostile attention than one of 25 p.c.
For a long while the capital stood at £300,000. In
1911-12 it was increased to £700,000, power being also
taken to work it up to £2,000,000. In the following
year the paid capital was brought to £BOO,OOO.
During the past year 200,000 more £1 shares were
issued at par, and of the £200,000 thus required a
present was made to shareholders of £40,000. Of
the £700,000 of capital added during the past
two years £442,500 has been supplied out of the
accumulated profits of the companv. The declared
policy of the directors is to give further strength to
the somewhat depleted reserves by strictly limiting
the rate of dividend. The idea is understood to be
that for many years to come the dividend should not
amount to more than one-half of the total profit.
The rate for the past year, .6-} p.c., is really, some-
what in excess of that figure inasmuch as it is paid
on the full £1,000,000 for the whole 12 months,
although the last instalment of 10s. of the new issue
was only payable on Ist January, 1914.
The next table shows what has been done with
the new capital:—
Liabilities.
1900. 1905. 1910. 1913. 1914.

Liabilities have increased a little, but still amount to
only £273,974, which is moderate for a trading com-
pany of this size with a shipbuilding programme in
hand. Ship property appears to be written down very
hard, which is another way of saying that the true
profits are most likely considerably in excess of the
figures shown in the table. The item stands at
£270,280 —as said just now, very small when the
size of the Mataram and Montoro, the new vessels
in the Java-Singapore service, is taken into account.
When the company launched out into vessels of this
kind for the East-Indian and Singapore trade, it
seemed doubtful whether they could maintain the high
rate of profits hitherto shown by the company’s old
Pacific Islands business. There has, however, been
an extraordinary development of the trade in flour
and sundries, including tourists, with Java and the
other human ant-heaps in the north, and the usual
honorable understanding with the Dutch Co. has
helped matters. One way and another, 8., P. might
therefore have got along very well even if they had
not dipped into shareholders’ pockets at all. Anyhow,
since 1910 there has been an increase of nearly
£150,000 in cash and share investments. One of these
investments is understood to be a considerable holding
in the Queensland Insurance Co., which in its cradle
days was practically a branch of 8., P.’s. This now
entirely independent co. has made very long strides
and its shares are at a lofty premium. But the
chances are that very properly the Burns, Philp
balance-sheet takes no account of that premium. If
this is so then there is another item among the assets
whose market value is very considerably above book
value.

South Australia had begun to think that Fortune had
no more bad seasons in her bag. Things last season
were, however, far from what they might have been.
The Winter was cold and the spring was dry, and
though later on prospects improved in the wheat dis-
tricts, red rust and other plagues robbed the farmer
Of some of his expectations. The indifferent weather is
almost entirely if not wholly responsible for the
diminished profits—diminished only by comparison
with those of the last three or four years, but still
much greater than the average of the years* preceding,
say, 1907. The gain in profits during these last few
years, though very excellent, is not altogether what
it seems, inasmuch as the capital on which dividend
has to be paid has been increased from £340,000
to £500.000. Capital and reserves combined now
total £1,035,168. The liabilities of the bank
(deposits, etc.) are £5,167.872, about £5 of public
money for every £1 of the shareholders’ cash, a safe
proportion. The Adelaide's figures are, however,
understood to vary considerably. It is the one bank
remaining with head office in South Australia; two
others fell by the way in troublous times past. And
being the only South Australian bank, and,
moreover, an institution with a very comprehensive
South Aus. share register and a fine backing of assets,
including uncalled capital, South Australia passes all
its banking business that way, which sufficiently ex-
plains the large movement in figures, especially relat-
ing to deposits.
Coming to details, these are the principal changes
shown by comparison with the last two sheets:—
Liabilities.
Mar., 1913. Mar., 1914.
Notes in circulation £16,801 £12,822 Dec. £3,979
Bills in circulation 275,358 265,710 Dec. 9,648
Deposits, etc. .. 4,155,320 4,889,340 Inc. 734,020
Liquid assets .. 2,393,505 2,988,616 Inc. 696,111
Advances .. . . 2,986,570 3,128,104 Inc. 141,534
Premises .. . . 105,627 111,321 Inc. 6,694
As before shown, the total liabilities are £5,167,872.
The liquid assets, £2,922,616, represent 67.8
p.c. of this total —a very strong position suggesting
that the manager can afford to be affable to the right
sort of client when he comes along for a trifle more
to go on with. Still one never knows for sure in con-
nection with the Bank of Adelaide, because of the
more or less violent fluctuation in the Government
account; all that can be said is that the directors are
reputedly a very cautious as well as a very experi-
enced lot, and that the cash position, apart from the
Government account, is habitually strong, and the
outlook is good. It is much too early to say what the
next harvest and wool 'elip will be, but the start is
all right, and there never was a time when Broken
Hill, South Australia’s best cow, gave a richer lot
of milk.
* * ~ * *
A Supreme Court writ has been issued
in Melbourne on behalf of a number of its preference
shareholders against the Commercial Bank of Aus-
tralia by _Blake and Riggall, acting for George D.
Meudell and other preference shareholders. It is
claimed by the plaintiffs that the Bank should pay
4 p.c'. dividends on the preference shares since 1896,
instead of the 3 p.c. it has paid. In the scheme of
reconstruction it was agreed that 4 p.c. should be
paid if earned. The extra 1 p.c. has been earned
many times over, and has been allocated to other
uses than the agreed payment to the preference
shareholders. Further, the plaintiffs claim that the
promise of the directors in February last to pay a
10 p.c. dividend to the ordinary shareholders is
irregular, for two reasons. Firstly, the preference
shareholders are justly entitled to their back money,
and, secondly, the original shareholders have not,
never had, and cannot for many years have any capi-
tal or interest in the bank. Further, the plaintiffs
claim that it was illegal to alter the voting powers
of shareholders by a new article of association giving
the ordinary shareholders the same power of voting,
namely, one vote for one share, as the preference
shareholders when the ordinary shareholders at the
present time have no capital in the concern. There-
fore the action resolves itself into three claims:' (1)
4 p.c. dividend to preference shareholders ; (2) no
dividend to ordinary shareholders; (3) reduction of
voting power of ordinary shareholders below equality
with preference. Of course, this action in no way
reflects on the credit or ' financial position of the
bank, and really the matter may be settled by a
reasonable compromise. At present, those seeking
redress are both powerful and determined.

The profit is the largest at any time disclosed by
the accounts. But there is better news even than
that for shareholders, and the chairman whispered
it to them at their meeting. Born in 1888, the
Royal of Australia speedily accumulated the cus-
tomary stock of boom assets. After the boom broke
the bank held on for some years, hoping for a re-
covery, but it refused to arrive to any extent worth
mentioning. In 1897, therefore> assets were revalued
and the capital was written down to correspond.
Since then good seasons and high prices have been
the rule, and now the chairman has been able to
announce the disposal of the last of the old dead-
horse. Moreover, a . net profit Of about £26,000
has been made on these realisations, which amount
the management has accumulated as an internal re-
serve in case the remnant of the properties to be
sold did not reach book value. There being no
longer any need for this special reserve the directors
shake it out of their sleeves on to the table:
£15,000 of it is to be added to the reserve fund
and the balance is to go to the credit of an Officers’
Provident Fund. Both these things are excellent jn- their
way, but it is still better news for the shareholders
that the monpr formerly locked up is now all 'avail-
able for new live business.
The bank ha_3 riiade reiiiarkable strides': Consider
these figures:— ,
