To give security to the new deposits the shareholders
capital has been increased in the 10 years from
£150,000 to £300;000, whilb the reserved
profits have also grown robustly. At the prfesent
time capital and reserves combined amount to
£517,165, after providing for the current dividend,
While the total liabilities amount to £2,383,715.
Thus for every £1 of the shareholders’ money in
the business there is under £4 12s. 3d. of the
public’s. Obviously the bigger the proportion of
shareholders’ funds the better the security afforded
to depositors. The proportion in this case is onb
of the best among A,ustraliail banks!. A ex-
cellent feature is the large proportion of .]iduid .asset?.
Of the tbtal of £2,385,715 owing to tho public, no
less than £1,261,233 could be paid out of ,c6in and
by the conversion of liquid assets, leaving £1,124,482
to be provided, and there are £1,653,647 worth of
non-liquid assets to do it with. Such figures as
those ought to give depositors and other creditors a
very satisfied feeling. As a matter of fact the pro-
portion of liquid assets looks unnecessarily large,
which is another way of saying that when the right
securities come along the bank appears to be in a
position to afford considerable further accommoda-
tion. Still, especially at this season of the year,
when the wool and wheat cheques have come in, it is
far better for a bank to have too large rather than
too small a pile of money in the treasury. Any-
way, a bank with large cash resources in good times
is the one that should attract the borrowing cus-
tomer. The one which cannot do anything for him
in bad times is no good to him.

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. Sd. pet
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 2s. sd.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [2

Thus, since 1912, the mine has been written down by
£BOOO, and it is evidently the intention ol the
directors to continue to allow for depreciation in
value; so that when it is quite worked out it will
be entirely written off the accounts and the co. will

that the co. has made is wonderful. Since 1904,
when the capital was £103,939, it has got in only
£3221 of outside money, yet during the same period
it has kept up a regular large dividend, added
£18,832 to its plant and stock, irrespective of writ-
ing down, and £29,235 to its liquid assets ; and on
top of that has bought a new mine and partially de-
veloped it. Of course, every good thing must came to
an end. The co.’s present mine is only a small one,
although no idea is given as to the amount of coal
left, and the shareholders must not be disappointed
if the next purchase doesn’t turn out as lucrative as
its present mine. But they have the satisfaction of
knowing that present dividends are dividends, and
not partly return of capital.
Towards the close of this year R.I.P. will
be written after the name of one of the last of the
Melbourne land boom victims. Born about ’75, the
North Melbourne and Carlton Permanent Building and
Investment Society throve and prospered through a
lusty youth. Then came the double afflictions of the
boom and a defaulting official. It sank like half a
brick, and though it wasn’t drowned outright it never
got its head properly above water again. The erring
official —an absolute masterpiece of casualness —only
entered up the books when the fit took him. And
even then it was his custom to do it in the dark with
his eyes shut and blinders on. As a consequence, a
committee has spent most of this century in an
utterly futile effort to straighten things out. It has
now decided to give the dreadful job best. The
shareholders met recently and passed resolutions
(1) ordering the committee to wind the ancient
and disorderly ruin up before the end of the year;
(2) instructing it to whack out such assets as there
were—about 3s. in the £1 —on a pro rata basis, and
(3) indemnifying each and every committeeman and
other officer against “any error of judgment, over-
sight, irregularity, mistake or omission in the books,
share register or other records of the society, or for
any other loss, damage or misfortune whatever, unless
the same happen through his dishonesty.” The latter
seems to cover all that is necessary. One can only
hope, in conclusion, that the N.M. and C.P.B. and
I. Soc. will soon be happily dead, and that when it
is it will prove to be one of those corpses for which
there is no resurrection.
* * * *
The shilling declared by the B. H. Pro-
prietary is a reminder that there are now 1,181,000
shares to participate. Something like 75 p.c. or 80
p.c. of this lot are held in Britain. The increased
support is due to the prospect of making profits out
of iron, steel, spelter and galvanising. It will
be months before a start can be made at the New-
castle ironworks, but the shipment of some thousands
of tons of heavy machinery from America and Britain
and the erection of a 60-ton crane at the jetty near
Newcastle to handle this material, indicates that fair
progress has been made.
* * * *
Ballina Gold and Mineral (N.S.W.)
affected a rather startling break from obscurity last
week. This concern proposes to puff itself up
mightily, if developments turn out as per schedule.
These are connected with beach sands, and the clean-
up that is threatening will be no ordinary clean-up,
either in fact or in consequence. Present capital,
£6OOO in £lO shares.
* * * *
Pulganbar Quicksilver and Copper, Graf-
ton (N S.W.), explains that there will be no clean-up
until the furnace has run two or three months, as the
surface of the condensers is so large, being over
8000 ft. superficial. So that part of the interest is
postponed for a month or two. The other part seems
likely to be postponed—or forgotten, perhaps—in-
definitely, for the directors languidly repeat that they
are making preparations to open up the mine. The
unfortunate mine never yet has been able to get
beyond that preparatory stage; and it is doubtful if
it ever will.
For the fortnight to April 15 the Occi-
dental (N.S.W.) hauled 766 tons averaging, according
to the official report, 12dwt. Bgrs. per ton. The re-
port also mentions that some of the stopes at No. 4
level have been beaten out. From No. 5 level 483
tons were raised, averaging just over 7dwt., so the
mine is depending on local enrichments to make up
an average of 12dwt. per ton. At No. 6 level (640 ft.)
31 tons were pulled, averaging about 17dwt., and
from the south drive 32 tons worth 12dwt. With
another machine at work this part of the mine is
expected to be a big producer.
New Burragorang (N.S.W.) since it made
a start has shipped 58 tons of its silver-lead ore for
a net return of £1056. If the mine could produce
that much in a fortnight there would be something
to rejoice over, but up to the present dispatches of
ore have been too small to make it worth while
talking seriously about profits. Latest report states Nd.
5 east is being prepared for stoping, and the rise
from No. 6 level has now broken through to the
winze from No. 5, improving the ventilation and the
opportunity for raising ore. Some old workings
are being prospected from No. 3 shaft. Only 11
tons were dispatched for the fortnight; at £lB per
ton this is worth £l9B.
* * * *
“Quidam”: Mining promoters talk very
glibly about the wealth to be amassed by applying
the “all-solution” (or crushing with cyanide) system
to various gossan outcrops in N.S.W. But anyone
who feels inclined to thus extract lucre from gossan
outcrops which overlie copper lodes, no matter how
poor cupriferously, had better go warily with his
preliminary tests and make sure that all the copper
has been leached out of the gossan; for a very little
copper has ruined many a sanguine cyanider. When
the gossan lies on a copper-bearing lode, water from
which will coat a knife, not only is copper sure to be
present, but there is enough acid from the partially
developed copper sulphides to cause trouble with
solutions which should be alkaline. Another pecu-
liarity of these gossan formations is that thev are
plentifuly endowed with soluble iron salts which are
vigorous eaters of cyanide. To people used only to
free milling, oxidised surface ores, which can be
crushed with cyanide without any chemical compli-
cations, there is much of a disconcerting nature about
ores in which the solutions turn a beautiful Prussian
blue, the zinc tailings look like new copper, and
liquor that should be alkaline turns strongly acid.
Profits may be won, but it is better, to realise the
difficulties before erecting a plant than to go bank-
rupt afterwards. Coppery gossan, from a cyanider’s
point of view, is not by any means the clean ootato.
* * * *
Mount Lyell’s investigations into the
northern workings of the Comstock appear to have
been fairly satisfactory. About 200,000 tons of 3
p.c. ore are reckoned to exist, and these should yield
a moderate profit under the flotation process.
Briseis Tin (Tas.) reports itself unable to
carry on the ticklish job of diverting the Ringarooma
River and maintaining the usual output. So the
next four months will see the output dwindle to 20
tons a month. The Briseis proper is practically
worked out, but for some years past stripping has been
conducted in the Ringarooma overburden, beneath
which there is apparently sufficient wash to postpone
the co.’s demise. How this wash is likely to pan
out the last yearly report refrained from mentioning,
for obvious reasons.
* * * *
Sultan Reefs, Blackwood (Tas.), after
much driving and sinking, is beginning to find out
that the old hands knew just when to throw up a
show. The reef is narrow and patchy, and though
tucker may come' from occasional yields, even a
small party couldn’t fatten out of the profits. The
best thing seems to be to hand the assets over to
the auctioneer; and that is to be done —if current
opinion is an accurate guide.
Mount Jasper (Tas.) has disregarded
Frank Powell’s pessimistic report, and has put up
the cash for a 200-ton concentrating mill. There
is much joy over a three-ton concentrates output that
is to be capable of covering exes., provided, of course,
it is high-grade enough. Then why stop at a mill
capable of treating 200 tons of ore weekly, when a
lode 1300 ft. long and ah outcrop 400 ft. long is wait-
ing to be drawn on for material? This problem has
hurried ’several Jasper mine-managers into oblivion,
and more will come and go unless a practical man
happens along who is capable of extracting profits
from Jasper ore or shovelling cash out of Mount
Stewart. The sanest suggestion yet made is to put'
Mount Jasper into a co. with a decent capital.
Working under tribute conditions Star of
the West, Kevington (Vic.), got out a small lot of
loz. dirt. Tlie co. has been trying to find what has
become of the shoot of gold that made the May-do
lode a dividend-payer. There has been enough for-
mation to build a cairn and just enough colors of
gold to prompt the forlorn hope that has led to the
rising on a lOin. reef east of the line. Here there
is a chance that the gold has made off in the little
shoot. If not, the other co. looks as if it has
done its dash.
When the Langi Logan South changed
directors there was a hope that the next half-year
would see the big gutter pricked. Two puddlers have
been built, some work done in the shallow ground,
and the driving carried on in the mine main and
intermediate levels. Co. has now Tom Mitchell’s
opinion that “it has been proved beyond doubt that
the whole of the leads from the north must traverse
your property (South Langi), and you have the most
valuable position on the field.” The same thing has
already been said of the Central Langi, the Upper
Langi and the North Langi, and these are about five
miles apart. Yet with all this to go on with there
hasn’t been any anxiety on the part of the directors of
the South Langi to bring its wealth to the surface.
Possibly some speeding-up may be done now that
the intermediate is nearing the deep ground, and the
bar influence is out off the main level.
* * * *
South Australian Oil Wells Co. is, floated
and goes to registration at once, allotment being
fixed for after the first meeting of shareholders on
May 13. Three or four firms in Australia, including
Moffat-Virtue Ltd. of Sydney, are indenting Cali-
fornian oil drilling rigs, and the probable petrolifer-
ous areas of Australia will be pierced for good or
evil in many places before the end of 1914.
A Bulletin man has been looking at the
Kalgoorlie mines, and here are some of his im-
pressions Great Boulder: Increased widths of ore
between the levels have considerably augmented the
ore reserves, and taken some of the sickly look away.
From appearances it seems as if present dividends
can, and will, be maintained for another three years.
.... Kalgoorlie: A number of bore holes have lately
been drilled from the bottom level of this mine, but
all of them have been in barren calc schist. Occa-
sional good finds in the upper levels have helped the
reserves to keep pace with the mill, and, intrinsically,
the property is probably as good now as it was
nine months ago, when the last official valuation was
made.... Boulder Perseverance: Working faces are
generally showing a much-needed improvement, though
the gross average for the mine is still under 6dwt.
Reserves are practically the same as. last estimate,
but the broken ore section of them has been in-
creased from 175,000 to 200,000 tons. Bottom work-
ings still wear a very lean look, and co. may now
be said to be subsisting mainly on what was left
behind or missed by former managements in the
higher levels. What the extent of these leavings
really is goodness only knows, but they are reckoned
to be sufficient to keep the mine on the profit-eafning
list for some years to come. The latter portion of
these remarks also applies to the Associated, which
some time,, ago bottomed at 1800 ft. on murderous
calc schist.’ The mine is having a hard struggle to
make ends meet and earn a little every now and then
for shareholders out of what others left behind, and
what it occasionally stumbles across in new ground.
....Adelaide Enterprise, alias Ziegler’s: Hard to
understand what the syndicate working this show is
building on. So far, some burrowing has been done
in a surface impregnation on the side of Mount Char-
lotte, and a braneffnew Huntingdon mill and other
plant has been installed. Nothing has been found
to warrant this extravagance, and, judging by de-
velopments to date, the mill and other surface decora-
tions will soon have to be sold or left to rust for
the want of work.... Golden Horseshoe: Recent losses
apparently caused by unusual number of stopes pass-
ing through low-grade sections. Position now im-
proving and good developments reported during past
year or two are expected shortly to be reflected in the
output. The heavy development expenditure in-
curred has practically opened up a new mine in the
lower levels... .Associated Northern: Iron Duke lease
breathing its last. Ora Banda lease: Lode has just
been cut at the No. 7 level, and looks poor. Alto-
gether, 400 ft. of shoot has been exposed at No. 6
level, three-fourths of which is estimated to average
40s. A winze put down to No. 7 level, however, only
averaged 20s. The 7000-ton sulphide plant in course
of erection on this show is expected to be running
in December... .Ivanhoe: The shaft on this mine is
now down 3047 ft., and is the deepest in the West. The
drives in the bottom level at 2720 ft. are in porphyry
country. The lode is well defined, but very patchy.
.... South Kalgurli: Recent developments at the
No. 2 and No. 9 levels of the South Kalgurli lease
and No. 10 level in the Hainault block have materially
added to the reserves and improved the appearance
of the mine.
A recent Mines Department estimate
figures the number of underground men in the gold
mines of the West at 7086 and surface hands at
5934, or a total of 13,020. Alluvial diggers are
computed at 425. Altogether, 213 men are reckoned
to be mining copper, 403 tin, 132 lead, and 559
coal. Several thousand workers such as firewood-cutters
and charcoal-burners are also indirectly connected
with the industry, and then a few prospectors have
also to be taken into account.
Granite got into the Edna May (W.A.)
shaft about the middle of April. Yet Southern Cross
only found the fact out when the bears were scratch-
ing up dust in Melbourne. I l 'oiks there still fancy
that gneissic “schist” is as soft as putty compared
with the primary granite. On the mine the chap
who has to knock out tucker belting into the gneiss
doesn’t see much difference between the drill-blunt-
ing stuff being butted into by the sinker, and the
abandoned diamond drill core got in the outlying
ground. Anyway, a post-mortem is to be held (gran-
ite versus gneiss) by the Mines Department at Perth.
That won’t help in the least to tell whether the next
lift will bottom on primary rock or disclose a well-
defined shoot, though the hardness of the country
seems to afford a tolerable index of what is ahead.
The last yield was again about the oz. mark, despite
the constant 2oz. prospects.
Bullfinch Proprietary (W.A.) reports “en-
couraging results” in the main shaft west drive at
300 ft. Payable sulphide ore is stated to be showing
from 287 ft. to 468 ft. along the level, and crosscuts
put through the lode at 271 ft. and 335 ft. are said
to have disclosed 10ft. of 345., and 15ft. of 265. 3d.
material. Co. also claims to have risen 80ft. on a
new ore body at the No. 2 level, and to have been
in 80s. stuff all the way. Annual meeting is about
to be held in London, but as Hamilton is at the
Boulder and Bill Robinson is spelling in Melbourne
only old-man Doolette is left to face shareholders
and bang the optimistic drum. As he is a real artist
at this sort of thing, it may be taken for granted
that the performance will prove a success.
“Oof Bird” (W.A.) : Payne’s Find’s posi-
tion needs explanation. Latest half-yearly accounts
show that £9050 has been put up to date, but £8706
of this, together with a few thousand pounds’ worth
of gold, has been expended. On what? Two or three
shallow shafts,, some potholes, a little crosscutting
and driving, and a couple of thousand pounds’ worth
of machinery. Co. now wants more money to pur-
chase rock drills to prospect for the continuation of
gold values, which are alleged to be showing in
adjoining properties, and which it declares are richer
at 140 ft. than nearer the surface. During the six
months covered by the latest accounts, 735 tons were
crushed for an average recovery of 505., but mining
and administrative expenses absorbed all this, and
£49 besides. The low average recovery compared
with official estimates was, according to the report,
due to “everything taken from the stope being
crushed.” Was a little of everything taken when
the flash assays were made, and if not, why not?
Surely the manager’s idea of an assay was not to
pick a piece of gold-specked stone out of the face
and give its value as representing the whole. Credi-
tors are owed £4408, and their security at present
consists of the aforesaid machinery, a few holes in
the ground, and the co.’s optimism regarding possi-
bilities at depth. It cannot, by any stretch of imagin-
ation, be considered gilt-edged.
Manager Bob Nicolson’s annual report
mentions a few words regarding the Ivanhoe and its
works. In August last Dr. Maclaren predicted that
the east lode would pass through the porphyry at a
vertical depth of 3380 ft., but while the porphyry
would be unreliable it would probably contain occa-
sional patches of payable ore. In order to further
test the location of the aforesaid porphyry a bore
was put down from the 2870 ft. plat. It found what
it was looking for at 3028 ft., and passed out of it at
3200 ft., and at a point 6ft. west of the eastern,
thus showing the porphyry dyke to be 27lft. wide at
the spot penetrated by the drill. A band of low-
grade mineralised ore was cut en route, and this is
assumed to be the east lode thrown west. If this
assumption be correct, its position and the location
of the eastern boundary of the porphyry would mean
an increased depth of approximately 120 ft. before the
east lode would pass out of the porphyry. It is now
proposed to bore at a similar angle from the plat at
3170 ft., when the main shaft reaches that depth, and
further prove the dip of the intrusive bar.
Sons of Gwalia (W.A.) has started work
on the main ore channel at the No. 20 level, and is
pushing the shaft down below that point. The erec-
tion of a big gas-producer plant for the re-organisa-
tion and simplification of the metallurgical treat-
ment has been completed, and the State Governor has
been asked to come along and perform a sort of
opening ceremony.
* *■ , * *
Mountain Queen (W.A.) is now scratch-
ing hard for a living by treating ore derived plainly
from a big surface deposit, and some slimes which
were allowed to accumulate prior to the erection of
the present plant. Nothing worth having is visible
at depth, and although a good deal of money has
been, and probably will be, spent on the co.’s other
property, the Transvaal, the prospect of shareholders
ever getting anything, except perhaps a liquidation
div., is very slender.
* * * *
Great Unknown, at Marda (W.A.), has
just been credited with another 1710 z., which repre-
sents a 3ioz. return. There is said to be a big pile
of loz. ore at grass, and the usual addendum, “the
reef is looking well,” is tacked on to the statement.
Several mine-buyers have nibbled at this show from
time to time, but no one has so far swallowed the
bait.
The Lake View and Oroya-controlled
Queen of the Hills (W.A.) complains that its progress
is being retarded owing to the shortage of competent
workers. However, it is living in hopes of getting
over this difficulty shortly, and, in the meantime, as
much ore as possible is being broken for the mill,
and the shaft is being pushed down to the 6th level.
* * * *
Adelaide has abandoned its option on the
Benjamin George at Coolgardie. No reason is
given for turning the show down so soon, but pro-
bably the want of something big enough for a co.
has a little to do with it.
* * * *
Brilliant Extended (Charters Towers)
shows a big improvement in the quality of the stone
crushed. The return for the last four weeks was 1540
tons for £4429, and 1100 tons cyanided for £798,
making a total of £5227. The three previous re-
turns gave £12,649 from 5004 tons of stone and a
quantity of tailings. Although the average was
slightly over 50s. per ton of stone there was a
small loss. The latest return, however, shows a pro-
fit of £1605. or 21s. per ton. There is, of course,
the discomforting reflection that, as the tonnage be-
comes reduced, costs rise. The prospect of striking
another pay shoot down at 3000 ft. vertical is poor at
present, but as the levels progress to the east there
is certainly a sporting chance. The Extended has a
very comfortable reserve fund.
* * * *
The Airmen River Co., after some years of
trouble with an intermittent water supply, is putting
in a big pumping plant to work its sluicing faces,
and is erecting a dredge (bucket) to work a
100-aere flat, which, it is said, goes between
21b. and 31b. of tin to the yard. All the tin is not
in Malaya, and there are some good properties in
N.Q., but it is not far enough away, or there are
not enough niggers to attract Australian capital.

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, May 4, 1914.
My Dear Arini, —
Hospital Saturday collectors raged with
unabated ferocity from dusty morning to
bankrupt eve. The usual live stock accom-
plices—an athletic goat, a dog or two, and a
pair of ponies—starred the bill. There was
no elephant collecting, nor did I see a
single whale. The lady bushrangers, who
swarmed office staircases and climbed almost
impregnable city fastnesses, sent a determined
squadron out to Victoria Park Racecourse,
to intercept any coin left over from the Guil-
laux flying exhibition.
In spite of the persistent attacks of Lovely
Woman, the collection (his year is behind the
1913 raid on the public pocket. About
£7717 is given as the approximate total, as
against £BOOO odd which last year’s attacks
wormed out of besieged citizens. Plainly
the boom is fading.
French aviator Guillaux gave such a won-
derful performance of gymnastics with his
buzzing monoplane, over Victoria Park Race-
course, the other afternoon, that few women
will contentedly walk mere earth for the
next week or two. From the stand the
machine looked like a long, half-dried locust,
its body stuffed with white paper parcels.
Poised on one leg, or on half a wheel or
it waited patiently till the band
had finished the Marseillaise, and Guillaux
had buried his head in its works, and every
■ other detail had been tidied up. llow to
fly, not only like a bird, but like an inverted
■ comma or a beetle kicking on its back in
mid-air—somewhere near the welkin—and
how to loop the loop, or dive from space to
space with nothing more substantial than
half a mile of atmosphere to stand on, was
■ only part of the Guillaux show.
Victoria Park Racecourse could give a good
;many points, in its trim lawns and well-kept
.flower beds, to untidy Randwick, which still
turns a slipshod herbage to the public eye.
i Someone with a cute notion of gardening
;must be the tetrarch of the pretty course near
the whiffs of Botany. Crowds of people
herded together on the green turf to watch
the French birdman turn Catherine wheels in
the atmosphere. They planted their feet
firmly on the sod, and wore their expressive
.smiles upturned to the whir of the flying
monoplane. Secretary PI. M. Somer, of the
Agricultural Society, craned his neck at an
angle of 75 degrees. The Alfred Marks
group were in the thick of the fray. Mrs.
Geoff. Fairfax had no brim to obstruct her
view, for her saucy black hat, with the white
ribbon round it (this is the millinery of the
instant), was like a small velvet pudding-
basin on her neat locks. The Strickland
youngsters swarmed round mamma, who
drove on to the lawm in her favorite two-
horse chariot. Some thoughtful person care-
fully removed the horses before the bird man
got into his swoop. French Consul Chayet
was about the proudest person present, and
the tricolor was It.
Lieut.-Governor Murray passed through
this town on his annual way to the Federal
Ministerial ear in Melbourne.
Under-Sec. for Colonies Hartmann Just
will be unofficially on view at the Govern-
ment House garden party this week. The
new A.D.C. Wallis, recently arrived from
'Gibraltar, will be inspected at the same
‘entertainment.
Mrs. Gaud Mayoress Richards lost her
father, Mr. John Booth, last week. This
sturdy old citizen passed out at the age of
82, after seeing Sydney grow from a roomy,
straggling town to a congested city, almost
deafened with the roar of its own traffic.
The late John Booth was an alderman at
the Council board of our chief municipal
fathers. Some 'years ago the old gentleman
wore the magic M.L.A. for a while, as a
member of the local bear-garden—l mean,
Legislative Assembly. The bears didffit
agree with him, and he left without regret.
Artist Penleigh Boyd, who took second
prize or highly commended or something for
his clever picture of the bush capital, Can-
berra, came to Sydney the other day. He
has been humping his easel round other
patches of Australian scenery. Artist Boyd
is still a mere youngster of 23 or 24, though
he has settled down to matrimony and trails
a grown-up kind of career behind him. One
item always mentioned is his precocious
effort, “Spring at St. Ives.” This was ex-
hibited at the Royal Academy while the
youthful artist was feeling the edge of his
first razor.
Wirths’ Circus management rushed to the
assistance of St. Margaret’s Hospital for
Women the other afternoon. A benefit
matinee for that worthy charity drew a
crowd Of sympathisers, and the Government
House Kindergarten sat up very firmly to
hear the lions growl in aid of the cause. The
South Sydney Hospital, which stands on
part of the Cooper estate (a big slice of Zet-
land), is to share with St. Margaret the
cash for this event.
Little Mrs. George Earp, who can gener-
ally be located under the brim of the Latest
Hat, is plunging into an evening with Wag-
ner before you see this. The Queen Vic-
toria Club, an aesthetic assembly which
mixes politics and Culture in homoeopathic
doses, will house this classic entertainment.
Wagner is strong meat, but So.ciety, which
knows what is expected of it, will have kept
awake during the musical explosions of Ger-
man thunder, if it had to pinch itself black
and blue.
The District Nursing Association, which
has been rather eclipsed by the mushroom
growth of newer charities, is getting a
look-in at More Cash. Notices to the effect
that the Admiralty lady will tear asunder a
sale of goods, etc., at the J. Macarthur On-
slow domicile (Bellevue Hill) have been
bombarding our Most Expensive suburbs.
The District Nurse is one of our best insti-
tutions. Trotting along mean streets to her
destination in some squalid rookery, that is
more or less tumbling down on the patient’s
head, she is the self-effacing Nightingale of
the Poor. Long hours and short pay, and
always the brown bag that contains nearly
everything except a bath tub and a mangle,
are the outward and visible signs of the Dis-
trict Nurse. Somehow, she gets less lime-
light than any other Act of Benevolence.
“Sealed Orders,” at the Theatre Royal,
serves to illustrate the brains of Beatrice
Day as Mrs. o’Mar.a, and the clothes of
Ethel Warwick. The latter lady, as the
Admiral’s wife who stole the sealed orders to
help her pay her gaming debts, is so much
like the finest fashion plate you ever saw
that the fact that she burls remarks, through
her clenched teeth, towards the orchestra
seems an unnecessary strain. Miss War-
wick has a beauty red coat, an evening wrap,
a couple of ball-dresses, and a long, lean
figure which any short, fat woman in the
audience would steal with pleasure. Re
burgling. The second scene gives a thrilling
spectacle of a patent safe being electrocuted
so that the Wrong Man can get away with
the diamonds.
Madeline Collins, a N.S.W. singer of ex-
traordinary promise, has been mentioned in
this Letter before as a youthful warbler of
the skylark persuasion. She has just won
a three-years’ scholarship at the Royal Acad-
emy of music, London. At present Made-
line, who is 17 years venerable, is studying at
St. Vincent’s College, Potts Point, and a con-
cert on her behalf is already muttering be-
hind the scenes.
The biggest social scrimmage last week
was an overflow meeting at the Australia.
The board room seethed (it is a great spec-
tacle when a board room seethes) with
women decked out in their most emotional
hats, and autumn frocks palpitating with ex-
citement. One or two desperate men looked in
and meekly fled towards the congested door-
way. Mr. Ernest Broughton, who had been
pounced on for the presentation, made a
wary progress through the throng, and got
his back against the wall with a large table
in front. Thus protected he handed a cheque
of £lOO to that well-known Sydney journalist,
Mrs. Patty Fotheringhame, who edits and
mostly writes a weekly social chronicle
called Splashes.
This popular presswoman, with a hand-
some autograph album clasped to her bosom,
a £lOO cheque and a huge presentation bou-
quet in her plump right hand, contrived to
murmur her thanks through a tear trickle or
two before being rushed off to the Winter
Garden to the largest tea party those pre-
mises have squeezed together. Mrs. Packer,
Mrs. David Storey, Miss McCarthy, Mrs.
Ernest Broughton, Mrs. G. L. Goodman and
at least a hundred other women friends and
admirers of kind Mrs. F. fell upon her neck
to tell her how pleased they were to give her
a slight token of their regard. (Pier neck
was dead tired with being fallen on before
the greetings were half through.) Just now,
they are writhing with indignation at the
sup-Pression of this affair by the inky way.
Not a feather present has even been mentioned
in a whisper, though the usual dress reporting
nibs were very much in evidence. Isn’t this a
small world, Arini?
The Sydney branch of the Bank of New
Zealand is going to change its quarters from
its present abode to its own roof. This
flitting from one side of George-street to the
other will land the Bank’s money bags in the
New. Zealand Chambers. The ground floor
of this palatial office is at present used as
the Maoriland Tourist Bureau. Mr. Harold
Beauchamp, a fine, upstanding visitor from
the Dominion, has been bustling round this
town in connection with the proposed move.
He is Chairman of Directors of the Bank of
New Zealand, and is still faithful, in the
era of the razor, to the short, clipped beard
of yore.
The Inter-State Tennis Argument begins
on May 8 and goes on till the 16th. at the
old address, Manning-road, Double Bay.
Hon sec. Ivo Kerr gripped time by its fore-
lock and sent forth placards of announcement
a week or so ago.
Mr. D. Dewar Wright and his wife and
daughter went off to Europe in the Mooltan
for a trip to other climes. They come back
across the U.S.A. G. D. Wright is the Aus-
tralasian general .manager, or tetrarch-in-chief
of the tremendous Dewar whisky firm.
During his absence abroad the local flow of
the liquid will be controlled by Mr. i\ M.
Dewar, whq hurried here from' Scotland for
the purpose with large supplies of the firm’s
goods in his sporran.
Mrs. W. A. Holman is welcoming home
contralto Ella Caspers as 1 gum this mis-
sive. The Wentworth Hotel Winter Garden
—where you can sip Pekoe and listen to
attractive music—is the scene of this gather-
ing.
A lady bearing the awe-inspiring name of
Mrs. Gertie de Wentworth James has been
dancing the te-tao in some Kensington
(London) haunt of Terpsichore. The te-
tao is a Chinese dance, which is so old that
it takes a good, hard thump to restore ani-
mation. It has been more or less moribund
for 4000 years. Mrs. Gertie de Wentworth
James ought to make it turn in its grave
anyhow.
Mr. R. Wood, ex-M.P. for Palmerston
North (M.L.), is over here on a holiday, and
incidentally had a chance of being waylaid in
the streets by Hospital Saturday bushranger-
esses. Mr. Wood is a man who made a small
local flare in M.L. politics many years ago. At
one time Fred Pirani, an Opposition mem-
ber for Palmerston North, and a very tall
man for liis size, which was almost nothing,
had achieved a scrap of reflected fame
as a sharp thorn in the side of Old Man Sed-
don. Wood, who was quite unknown, as-
tounded political prophets by beating Pirani,
to the great joy of Seddon’s followers. His
constituents liked him so much that at next
opportunity they elected him ex-M.P., and no
one has torn the job from him from that day
to this.
The dapper Consul-General for France,
Mr. _ Chayet, has been discoursing at the
Foreign Language Society’s recent corroboree.
Dr. Roth was in the chair. The notion of
meetings at which industrious persons who
try to chatter in French to anyone who will
be so obliging as to listen can hear the Right
Accent, is a good one. Also, it may assist
exasperated Australian housewives to hurl
the French language at erring and always
late butcher boys to the mental uplifting of
the same. Even such a small matter as
callings the Champs Elysees by their usual
designation instead of calling them the
Chumps Eliza, after the fashion of Perkyn
Middlewick, the butter-merchant, has a cer-
tain significance. Consul Chayet, by the
way, usually upholds the French tradition of
clothes, in a warm shade of cinnamon brown
(by day) which is coaxed to look as if it still
hung on a tailor’s lay figure. In the evening
he wears the usual sort of thing. At night
he presumably wears the usual sort of thing,
but not being a burglar I haven’t aroused
him at night.
Violinist W. Du Roulay, a punil of Sevcik,
fiddled his way through a pleasing programme
in St. James’s Hall the other evening. A
big helping of Mozart’s honeyed sweetness
opened the programme. This showed the
Du Boulay technique (which lacks nothing
but a fiery touch of the Devil or original
sin to shake some cayenne into it) to less
advantage than the florid music of Wieni-
awski’s Valse Caprice. A tenor and two
lady warblers, who took part in the recital,
had the sincere sympathy of the audience in
their heroic efforts to oblige the company
with vocal items. _ Mrs. Du Boulay at the
piano was the ideal accompanist every
fiddler is looking for.
Artist Thea Proctor is showing her ex-
quisite water-color studies in Anthony Hor-
derns’ fine art gallery. The show, which
is being officially rent asunder—l mean
Francis Suttor, M.L.C., as I
write this missive, will remain open till May
20. Australian artist Thea’s designs for
fans have made a flutter in such august
circles as the 1912 International Exhibition,
Venice, and the 1913 Ghent Exhibition. The
London Times, a journal which swallowed a
poker 100 years ago, and has never quite
recovered, so far unbent as to say of this
fine colorist’s work that it recalled Conder,
with better drawing and more movement.
Miss Proctor returns to London shortly. The
local magnate who is addicted to the pur-
chase of diamonds and motor-cars, ought to
secure a sample of Art (warranted not to
boil) before it is packed up for Europe.
The village of Nelson, tucked away on the
M.L. coast, amongst the hops and apple or-
chards, is shaking itself up in search of new
ideas. It began with a strong leaven of
conservatism as a favorite old-age anchorage
of half-pay naval officers and whole-pay but
decayed folk of other brands, who found it a
retired and salubrious haven. The modern
Nelson, however, is beginning to reach out
for fresh propositions. It is, tired of being
a home for people with locomotor ataxia—
broken but resolute gentlemen, who ask you
out for a walk on a pleasant Sunday after-
noon, and walk like a capital Z, and fall
down periodically in the middle of the road
and the middle of the conversation, because
their legs have suddenly “gone.” You aren’t
allowed to help your fallen companion up.
because that kind of heroic patient would
regard it as an insult if you implied that
anything was wrong with him, or that he
had declined from his perpendicular. So
you continue the discussion in the same old
tone, and lift your feet up and down to sug-
gest that you are still walking, while your
semi-paralysed but valiant companion gathers
himself together. There used to be half a
dozen towns in Australasia where this fall-
ing class of citizen congregated.
But Nelson has gone in for business.
Therefore Mr. Izard, from thereabouts, is
cutting up apples in N.S.W. and inspecting
the wmrm in the centre, in order to decide
whether it is the same worm or a different
one. He has decided that it is. A certain
amount of assistance is being rendered by
the Agricultural Department, and Mr. Izard
hopes to get a sack or two full of brisk no-
tions on Australian methods.
Fiji’s new Attorney-General Young, who
left Sydney for his sunburnt isles early this
week, has had what I suppose is called
“colonial experience” in the Seychelles
groups of islets. To Downing-street intelli-
gence a man who can fulfil the legal duties
of Chief Justice in those lone islands with-
out tying the Empire in a knot is the right
luminary for Fiji. Lawyer Young only spent
a few days here on his arrival from England,
and he didn’t state whether Goat Island is
larger and more beautiful than the Seychelles
or just about the same. The crags of ocean
that the Attorney-General comes from num-
ber about ‘JO, if you count them carefully,
and have now a real live Governor who com-
bines the billet of Commander-in-Chief with
his other duties. When international com-
plications threaten, and his Excellency mobi-
lises half a policeman (1 think that is cor-
rect) it is a great sight. I am told that the
Seychelles aren’t large enough to support a
hotel, but that a widow woman takes in
boarders on an emergency, and that a small
keg of beer is maintained at the back of the
church, the same being fastened by a dog-
chain to the handle of the rear door. So,
even in that quiet region there are some of
the comforts that we look for in London and
Paris and Boggabri, and other great centres
of frivol and dissipation.
Diphtheria is slinking (it’s a sly and
stealthy germ (hat doesn’t fight in the open)
round the Military College at Duntroon.
This news item makes anyone who has seen
that one-time station homestead, where it
peered over at the big woolshed on the flats,
sit up and think. In days gone by, when
dry seasons gave the Duntroon folks a tight
pinch to eke out their water supply, there
must have been a number of underground
wells and other hiding places for the neces-
sary moisture. It is not impossible that, in
the building of new premises and general
titivating of the ancient place into a train-
ing ground for Australia’s babes in arms,
some old cubby-hole of this sort may have
been overlooked, and the ancient microbe,
turning in its grave, may have heard strange
noises overhead and come out to have a look.
At last an alderman named Griffin has
arisen in his might to protest against the
axe. lie belongs to an almost extinct muni-
cipal species, and ought to be caged and ex-
hibited to a wondering public. The sight of
the beautiful umbrageous canopy (I am go-
ing to have that expression varnished and up-
holstered for use during the winter) of a
Moreton Bay fig tree, generally invites a city
father to a frenzy of sudden murder. He
feels like Nero when he rushed out to slay
his mother and become an orphan. He al-
ways wants to hire some able-bodied person
named Simpson and armed with an axe to
go forth and immediately destroy the work of
Nature, and cart away the body to a secret
municipal morgue for dead and gone trees.
At present the municipal magnates are ach-
ing to chop down the huge Moreton figs that
shade the Macquarie-street side of Govern-
ment House grounds. The protest of Aider-
man Griffin isn’t likely to stay the slaughter,
but it is worth recording. It only takes 21
years to make a possible alderman and as
a rule 60 years sees the end of him. The
cedars of Lebanon go back to the time before
aldermen were invented. And the last of
them may endure till the day when an iron
alderman, driven by steam, will blow off
superfluous energy through his escape valve
in the full meeting of the council and sound
his whistle in committee.
The new Governor-General-to-be is taking
his billet very seriously—judging from his
speech at the farewell banquet at the Royal
Colonial Institute. Possibly he feels, like
many British folk who have little or no per-
sonal knowledge of Australasia, that he is
going to actually govern this continent, as
Phillip governed the baby N.S.W. settlement
in its querulous infancy. A tactful and
agreeable figux-ehead, able to make a good
speech at a horse or cattle show, with a love
of sport, if possible, is about the actual size
of . a Gov.-General’s orb of power. It Is
after one of these Excellencies has lived in
Australia, and returned ,to the Cold Land
which lent him. that we look for practical
results. He can then talk to official bigwigs
and baldheads' in London of the things he
knows, instead of solemnly coquetting with
the things he knows not, as in the Munro-
Ferguson address.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, May 4, 1914.
My Dear Yarrie, —
By way of farewell, the G.-G. Denmans
stood on the dais, on Thursdav night, and
inspected 2000 of the Best People—people
whom they had fed during their term with a
devotion worthy of a better cause. Most
of. them were seen to be fatter than when the
reign began; but a few ungrateful ones had
gone back. Perhaps their vittles disagreed
with them. There was the usual gleaming,
blinding motor rush to the scene, and the
same old shining crawl up the drive to the
entrance. But such things have been de-
scribed before, and one might as well make
some reference to “the groaning board” (Dic-
tionary definition : “A plank laid horizontally
round which people groan re the quality of
the wine placed thereon for consumption”),
or write of “a brilliant and distinguished
gathering.” The affair did not begin until
9.30. in order to give the vice-regal diners
time to finish their cigars and scandal. A
few Parliamentarians merely hurried in, saw
their wives and daughters lose themselves in
the gaudy crowd, and then fled back to the
equally idiotic proceedings in the House,
which hasn’t got enough cash to help Maori-
land with the proposed light on the Three
Kings, but can spend days and weeks drivel-
ling. It is almost manslaughter as well as
time-slaughter. When the vice-regal people
had arranged themselves on their perch, and
the attendant Helps had strewed their super-
fluous millinery on the blue plush couch, a
Noah’s Ark procession was formed, two and
two. round the room. Tally-men saw that
the order was preserved, and that no one
broke away across the centre iff the room
until they had shown their tickets. Cham-
berlain Nevill was ticket-examiner, and he
murmured names into his Ex.’s inclined ear.
I don’t quite recollect the order of the per-
formance, but think it was as follows: First
the G.-G. shook hands with the patient; then
her Ex. shook hands; after that the butler
patted him on the back, the head gardener
pulled his leg and the cook borrowed a shil-
ling and kissed him. (On second thoughts,
I don’t think it could have gone beyond
the hand-shaking; but the other turns
should have been introduced to give it
a touch of novelty and excitement.)
Her Ex. had the blue velvet canopy
and drapes as background for her white and
silver dress and sparkling diamonds, and I
must sav she looked impressive. The blue
satin chairs were unroped (like the guests),
but the crowd had no time to sit down.
Strong hints were given by the hustling
Helps that the show was to be an
early one. Denman looked ill, and the
bulge in the chest of his Court uni-
form suggested red-flannel chest protec-
tors, porous plasters and other medical com-
forts. His lady broke the rule of the
hay-fevered domicile by having. cut chrysan-
themum blooms in gold baskets in every avail-
able place. Supper was a buffet spread, and
the most experienced feeders secured places
handy to the carved turkey and champagne.
One weightv, bare-shouldered dame of title
keut her place like a heroine, while wretched
nibblers and sippers came and went. Ap-
parently she had tossed up years before as to
whether it was to be Appetite or Figure, and
Appetite had won amidst loud applause.
The Stanley lady made a pretty picture on
the Dutch-blue carpet, in wallflower-brown
velvet and nothing much beyond ivory tulle
on her shoulders. Mrs. Hennessy wore a mag-
nificent blue dress —or perhaps it was shell
pink, or plain red with white stripes. The
Barttelot dame looked well in black velvet.
University people were there in masses. Mrs.
Professor Lyle showed a Marie Antoinette
cap amid the ospreys and feathers, and
Mrs. Bigjohn Forrest arrived unexpectedly
from the West, in clothes that were so beau-
tiful that I have completely forgotten them.
Squattocracy came in flocks —but of course
squattocracy goes to everything in flocks.
The Falkiners, of Widgewa. who have been
entertaining vice-regal people at the sleepy
homestead, were on hand, and Mrs. Pursey
Chirnside wore a dress that was startling
above the waist-line, and sun-rayed with
steel beads from waist to knee. Her entry
was sensational and entirely successful; but,
unfortunately, a latecomer appeared wearing
exactly the same type of costume. For that
crime some dress-builder will have to offer
satisfactory explanation, or suffer the con-
sequences, which will be execution in the
backyard. Mrs. Inglis wore bright lettuce-
green—a sort of thank-Heaven-the-drought’s-
over effect. Mrs. Captain Hall, in blue and
gold, matched her husband’s uniform nicely,
and her sister’s Parisian costume of old rose
and silver, was a poem in rags. A final
select supper to the Pride of the Market,
amidst scarlet dahlias, wound up the famine
relief proceedings of the amiable pair. The
Denmans have endeared themselves to the
digestions of many people who really should
get enough to eat in their own homes.
A Voice from the Graves —a man’s voice:
“Last Saturday afternoon, for the first time
in its long and honorable career, our old
original cemetery at West Melbourne was
enlivened by a vice-regal function. Pony
Governor Stanley came along on his own, in
a dark-grey suit, to hoist a laurel wreath
half-way up the plain, inexpensive column
on John Batman’s grave. It was a poor,
home-made wreath, quite in keeping with
other features of the ceremonial in that
dilapidated God’s-acre. Two or three little
squads of Boy Scouts were the guard of
honor at the approach to the grave. Gaud
Mayor Hennessy went down to meet the Gov.
when his motor landed him amidst the for-
gotten mounds and broken tombstones, and
together they sauntered to a bit of a platform
erected over the place where Batman was
buried exactly 75 years ago. Old folks and
Boy Scouts constituted the bulk of the
audience, what time the inevitable speeches
were being uttered from the platform, and
hymns by the “festival choir” (Batman’s
festival, perhaps) were proceeding from
somewhere behind the platform. One or
two men in the crowd ostentatiously took
their hats off, as though they were in church,
when the feeble assemblage struck up “The
Long Day Closes,” which scared a number of
other ancients into likewise revealing their
baldness, but the weather was so good at
the time that nobody broke the solemnity
with a sneeze. Among the speechers, lawyer
Brennan, M.P. for the Cemetery district,
scored the most points, but Gov. Stanley ran
him close. The early history of Victoria
is his Ex.’s strong subject. He has read
it up. And he talked quite interestingly
about Batman days for quite a long time.
Afterwards he had to sit and listen bare-
headed to more speeches for about an hour,
whilst the motley gathering was going to
pieces. A few visitors had dropped into the
function, it seemed, not so much on account
of Batman’s deathday anniversary, as to
make inquiries about the bit of cemetery
which the City Council or somebody pro-
poses to add to the adjacent vegetable
market. It is the Jewish section of the old
Cem., and the idea is to remove the four or
five tombstones of long-forgotten Hebrews to
the south end of the eraveyard. Surely
no surviving distant relative of the deceased
can object to the proposed shifting of tomb-
stones. Where they stand at present is a
fenced-off reserve, behind the house of the
caretaker. The Jewish quarter is not in
the Cemetery proper. The caretaker’s house
was built on some of it, some of it was
dug up to make flowerbeds, and the remain-
ing portion is backyard. Close to where the
late Montefiore lies sleeping the caretaker’s
fowls are laying eggs.
A Gentleman Help of the Denman house-
hold is being called away from these shores
just when he is immersed in a sentimental
episode. The girl in the case is the daughter
of one of the richest wool families of the
Western District, and the lynx-eyed chaper-
ones are prophesying an early engagement. A
squatter’s wife has been making things easy
for the devoted pair by inviting the girl to
her station home when the Help was around.
A fine old lady—a once well-known and
plucky follower of the Melbourne Hounds —
had an exciting experience the other day that
proved her nerve to be as steady as ever.
A horse harnessed to a cart laden with soft
drinks got mixed up with a tram in which she
was travelling. There was a series of
crashes and screams, and the air was filled
with flying bottles, brown streaks of ginger-
ale, harness ends and waving hoofs. Every-
body was confused and helpless. The old
lady alone sat tight and offered a few words
of advice that helped materially to clear up
the tangle. When the other women passen-
gers had arranged themselves in a row on the
kerbstone and were straightening their hats
and nerves, the old lady was busily engaged
trying to comfort a frightened child. That
job done, she boarded another tram and went
her way. If she felt sorry for anyone it was
the badly-driven ginger-beer horse.
Cook-sculptor Webb-Gilbert is off for a
course of European study with rich Ameri-
can Myers as his backer; so the past week
has been punctuated joyously with farewells.
Artist Hal Gye organised one dinner party,
and the Victorian Artists had a good-bye
spread on Saturday night.
Clarence Dyason, who ranks as one of the
big mining men of Bendigo, and has always
been rated as a matrimonial catch, is to
marry Miss McClure, a charming young per-
son. Clarence’s sister, Emily Dyason, is
the pianiste who has been lecturing about
the composer with the sneezy name under
whom she studied for so long.
Dr. Nyulasy is housing some of the Maori-
land Medical Congress people who have come
over for the jubilee of the Melbourne Uni-
versity Medical School. The bachelor doc-
tor has just one sister now to hostess for
him and chaperone him to functions. (You
will remember, my dear Yarrie, unless your
memory has entirely failed, that, when he
went across to Maoriland for the recent
Congress, he gave the other sister in holy
matrimony to a good, kind man who wanted
to buy someone else’s hats for the rest of
his natural life.)
The newly-wedded Mrs. Kenneth Niall has
descended on this city to welcome sister Mrs.
Carr-Walker who is fresh or frozen from
England. They are both staying with the
parental Griffiths, at Toorak, and are attend-
ing the current frivols in clothes that are
calculated to madden all feminine specta-
tors.
Winnie Honey, the artistic scholarship
winner with the doll-like profile, was busy in
London painting her Australian picture,
when the thing was slashed by a suffragette.
It really looks as if Winnie had hired that
hatchetejffe to give her an ad., for, on her
merits, she was rather small game for the
militant theatricals.
The Repertory Theatre Club got a thous-
and pounds out of the Denman furniture and
old clo’ venture. The Club’s legitimate share
(after the Arts and Crafts people had col-
lected their tenth) was a mere £BBO. But
the Denman lady coaxed the A. & C. to
hand over an extra £2O, and make the other
lot an even £9OO. That much accomplished,
the insatiable vice-regal lady extracted an
extra £lOO from some rich people she had
stayed with in the country, and so made up
£looo—which is a nice round sum and very
pleasing to stand off and look at. The Arts
and Crafts did a great deal of the work in
connection with the exhibition, and their
self-sacrifice reminds one of the worm that
didn’t turn.
State Governor Stanley opened the Vic-
torian Artists’ show last week with some
difficulty, being short of intelligent remarks
or something of the kind. He usually starts
off with a carefully arranged speech that
fairly gleams with midnight electricity; but
he is rarely able to stick to the point—not
that it matters in the slightest. On Monday
last he burbled along more or less about Art,
all the time casting appealing looks at his
pretty wife, who, I understand, really knows
something about picture-making. (Now for
the real business!) Her Ex. wore a peanut-
shaped hat that looked as if it might have
cost Is. 6d. in Chapel-street; but it was
adorable on her head. An ordinary woman,
if she were rash enough to make the same ex-
periment, would probably be denounced and
crossed off every visiting book in her suburb.
(Perhaps, after all, the hat cost ever so
many guineas, and its plainness was the
highest form of Art.) The Best Fed, as usual,
crowded the stairs, looked critically at the
things on the wall, and tried to make appro-
priate noises, but mostly failed. Some of
the artists hadn’t shaved for days, and the
living originals of a few of the pictures
trailed round in the regulation droopy
clothes. Also Bernard Hall wore lemon or
yellow gloves. I don’t know why.
A mixed show of the work accomplished
by five young Arts and Crafts enthusiasts
is due in the Centreway, on Tuesday, May
12. Viola Jackson is the only girl in the
bunch, and her job is leather work. Her
four young men associates are Henry Har-
vey (sculpture), Tommy Pridgeon (home-
made jewellery), Harold Herbert (water
color painting), and Mr. Tompkins. I am
not quite sure what Mr. Tompkins does and
I give him the prefix “Mr.” because I have
forgotten his Christian name. The unin-
vited are urged not to sit at home and grieve
over the non-arrival of a gilt-edged card,
but to come along and view the show.
Marshall Lyle had a good idea for a Reper-
tory Ball set. He wasn’t anxious to get
into fancy dress, and a lot of his friends felt
the same way. So he organised a “Butter-
fly on the Wheel” gang and sent out word
that anybody in ordinary dress could join in
as spectators in the court scene. He him-
self reserved one of the barrister’s parts,
and Dr. Maloney’s 17-year-old daughter, a
pretty wisp of a girl, was cast for the part
of the Butterfly who was caught on the legal
Wheel.
The jubilee of the Melbourne University
Medical School has been joyously raising the
old dust. The women graduates had a din-
ner of their own at the Oriental Hotel, where
they carefuly imitated, as far as was possible
or convenient, the time-honored feeding pro-
cedure of mankind. The jubilee opened last
week with a party at the University, with
State Governor Stanley and his lady in the
centre of the vortex. The tea occurred in
the stuffy place that was once the Museum,
and I may remark right away that no amount
of varnish will ever rid the place of the
smell the whale left behind it. The Fox
painting of President Allen was unveiled
with much ceremony and a bouquet of 10-
minute speeches. The wives of the profes-
sors supplied the tea and cakes and lent their
own silver and china. One of them was
incautious enough to say that the prepara-
tion for the spree was much better than the
spree itself; but she was immediately sup-
pressed and taken away kicking in a bag.
There is a long programme of jubilations to
be performed, and every suburban medicine
man is rushing his daily visits in order to
be on hand.
Mr. and Mrs. Phillips Fox’s joint show
(scheduled to open on May 5) will remain
so until May 16. The Denmans have prom-
ised to climb the Athenmum stairs and have
a look round.
There is to be a Tango supper at the
Hyams’ new ballroom in Flinders-street,
next Saturday. The Tango class is giving the
spread by way of a “bon voyage” to George
Morgan, of the Dandies, and Elsa Langley
and H. L. Austin, of the Pierrots, who are
off to Britain in a week or two. The three
young people learned their tango from the
energetic Hyams sistern, and were among
the first to introduce the awful disease to
Melbourne audiences.
Harry Lauder has never been ill in his
life; but, directly he tells you so, little
Mrs. Lauder implores him to touch wood,
and Harry dutifully does so, bumping his
forehead as a placatory act to the gods.
Fellow-artists say the time to hear him talk
freely is just after the show. Then he rattles
along in high feather and recapitulates his
successes of the evening. “Hear the laugh I
got from the fat man in the front stalls?”
or “I broke up the ‘dreuth’ of that dour body
in the box before curtain, eh?”
Concert artists who come here in the wake
of stars all seem to think Australia a good
proposition from a box otlice point of view.
Jacques Pintel, pianist, is the latest to pay a
return visit on his own. Jacques was the
little, plump, pale accompanist of the fiery
Calve; but he is capable of an interesting
show all by himself. On Thursday night he
gave quite a brilliant musical party in the
Auditorium, when he tore off some attractive
compositions by Russian musicians, as well
as a few of his own* brewing. The audience
seemed to be the same old audience that goes
loyally to all the high-grade noises.
The Town Hall will have been aired some-
what before the famous Terry lady gives her
first discourse there to-night (Thursday).
Another of Edward Goll’s piano recitals
came due on Tuesday, this week, and the
Town Hall, not the Auditorium, is the place
where Goll reveals the soul of Chopin and
makes his audience feel good. So the muni-
cipal barn will not seem quite out of the
running for the Elect when they come to
hear what Ellen has to say about Shaks-
peare’s heroines and her sweet old self.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The Queen Adelaide Club extended the mitt of wel-
come to Lady Galway on Monday. The mitt in
question took the form or disguise of a lunch. The
new dining-room where the meal occurred was hardly
able to recognise itself, so crammed was it with
roses and autumn leaves and Sheffield plate, and so
forth. Sixteen committee-women put on their best
clothes, and saw that the bottle was passed round
with regularity and dispatch. Lady Galway wore
black, and looked charming.
On Tuesday the club stood her tea —people need
food badly after a long P. &0. voyage. About 150
members flooded the new rooms in a laudable attempt
to show the new-comer what the youth, wealth and
beauty of Adelaide could do in the way of clothes.
She seemed paralysed with admiration; but the look
may merely have meant, on the other hand, that she
wished to goodness she was back home in a com-
fortable wrapper and with a cigarette handy. Looks
are baffling things to interpret when you come to
think of it. Thue reception-room wore gilded baskets
of autumn leaves, tied with bows of gold gauze; one
tea-room was studded or garnished with pink and
mauve chrysanthemums, in bowls of Ruskin china;
and in another were brass bowls of salmon-pink
dahlias and delphiniums. The third was a mass of
roses and ferns. The general effect was something
fierce. Lady Galway wore amethyst crepe de Chine,
and the C.J.’s dame inhabited oyster-colored bro-
caded crgpe de Chine. Mrs. Bronte Smeaton’s smart
frock of Indian red was topped by a black hat, and
Mrs. Herbert Rymill and Mrs. Napier Birks were in
blue. Mrs. W. Margarey and Mrs. Dudley Hayward
looked well, and so did about 140 others. The
names and addresses of the Ten Horrors who wore
pre-historic garments and ruined hats shall not be
divulged publicly by me this side of the tomb.
The W. It. Bakewells put in the first few days
of their honeymoon at the John Downers’ house at
Aldgate. They are now in Melbourne—that is, if
they haven’t already passed on to Sydney.
Mrs. H. H. Wigg and her daughter and Miss Alice
Campbell are also travelling. As against this there
is the fact that Mr. Russell, of Sydney, and his
daughter are the guests of the John Downers on
Pennington Terrace. You will remember that the
Downer lady was a Russell.
The North Adelaide Golf Club was opened for the
season on Saturday last, with afternoon-tea and a
competition or two. Miss Sanderson and Miss
Angove and Mr. Beresford and Mr. Horton secured
the competition prizes, and most of the guests
secured afternoon-tea. The Way dame was there, and l
Mrs. Bishopscourt Thomas, also Mr. and Mrs. W.
Pope, Mrs. Eversley Thomas, Miss Herring and Miss
Horn, Mrs. 11. Martin, and Mr. and Mrs. W. B.
Wilkinson.
The Fortnight’s Fair in aid of the Blind Institute,
which opened in the Exhibition Building on Friday
afternoon, promises to be the bazaar of the century
as far as this village is concerned. For weeks —yea,
months- —committees scattered in every corner of
Suburbia and in. every country town have been work-
ing to collect funds and materials for the various
stalls. And now the looting has begun in earnest.
Concerts, pageants and the rest have been planned—
in a word, none of the conventional lures of our
modern civilisation have been overlooked. If this
prosperous community doesn’t ante up a deal of its
superfluous cash in the good cause there will be
astonishment, not to say fury, in the bosoms of right-
thinkers. (I haven’t used that expression since
childhood. Then the printer made it read “tight
drinkers,” and I was blamed for his error and van-
ished into a cloud of disgrace.)
Another of the Kidman maidens has gone to Eng-
land with a gemmed ring on her finger ; and Gilbert
Monks is the young man who had the joy of paying
the bill. The girl is Elsie, of the family of the
late Sackville, the “Cattle-King’s” older brother.
She is on the Medina, which by this time ought to
be as near as it can get to Cheshire, where the
young man’s people live and the cheese and the cat
come from.
Dr. Ramsay Smith, coroner and hygiene expert in
Adelaide, has told a Register interviewer that the
present much-abused fashion in woman’s dress is
really an absolute reform, the “true hygienic policy
of dressing,” having at last been stumbled on —by
accident, no doubt. The air that gets to the skin, the--
doctor claims, is at least as important as the air that k-
is breathed. Woman is properly ventilated now!
Then R. Smith unwisely abandoned the position of
expert for that of prophet, and made the reckless fore-
cast that “the fair sex have discovered that these-
latest fashions of theirs are so comfortable that thev
will never depart from them.” As if that would
trouble them, when some self-appointed male author-
ity in Paris sends word that they are to dress some
other way!
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

“Pick”: Because he was eowpunrhor on a
Jamberop dairy where no tests were made to deter-
mine the qualifications of the female joss, “A.J.W.”
(B. 9/4/’l4) calls me a N.S.AV. Labor Government,
a deficit and a suspended sinking fund, and declares
that scientific dairying is an unknown quantity on
the South Coast of the Ma State. The great majority
of South Coast dairymen do not make up their own
stuff at all. They supply to a factory (generally a
co-operative concern), where they are paid only by
tlie butter-fat-test results. Their inclination towards
profit, therefore, impels them to work scientifically
for the betterment of their herds. 1 lived for 16
years amongst South Coast dairymen, and never met
one who could not tell me how much Strawberry or
Brindle was worth to him per month. Then, in
order to check the factory manager’s estimate of
his butter-fat yield, every dairyman invests in a
milk-tester. They are cheap enough (355., or there-
abouts), and as milk-testing is a special practical
lesson taught in nearly all dairying-centre schools the
youngsters can keep the records if, as “A.J.W.” says,
the boss cocky doesn’t do it himself. To conclude, I
wish to tell “A.J.W.” that 1 am not a Labor Govern-
ment,■ and I am no relation whatever to the £4 per
day which was paid to Neilscn. If he hints again
that I am any of these things, 1 will shoot him dead
with a brick.

Gouger : I have noticed that men living
among the mountains of N.Q., where for the major
part of the year the climate is cool and the rainy
season is regular, have mostly got black hair. I have
known men with light brown hair to have a crop of
almost black hair after a 12-months sojourn amongst
these mansions in the skies. When I came to the
mountains uncharitable people called mv auburn
locks “ginger”; now the aforesaid locks are of a
fine, glossy murk. Likewise a mate of mine, whose
head foliage was prematurely grey, is now midnight
color at the end he ought to think with but doesn't.
“The Pariah”: At Bright (Vic.) three
miners broke into a wombats’ burrow, which, on in-
vestigation, was proved to have penetrated just over
100 ft. from the surface. This, is fairly strenuous
labor for an animal of the wombat size. Is there any
estimate of the wombat’s age or antiquity? Bones of
him have been found in the limestone below the first
lava flow from the Warrions (Vic.), and it is a long
tinee billion generations since that district was the
Devils distillery. AVhile speaking of birds may I
mention that the goldfinch is becoming very common
in the bush south and cast of Colac (Vie.). He makes
**■ joyous addition to our forest
life, and, to judge from the num-
ber of nests 1 have seen in the
past three months, he is going
to obey the Creator's injunction
to multiply and replenish the
earth. [And then, just when the
editor hoped that his correspond-
ent was going to throw some light
on the sources of the Amazon, the
shortest road to the South Pole,
and the causes of water hyacinth,
and the smell of crocodiles, this
diffuse contributor broke off. What
is the good of a diffuse contributor who breaks off?
He is in the same category as a headless and stoniach-
less commentator without feet.]
“Mimosa”: In a recent Uuixutin pur. a
writer wanted to know how it is that “bribing” (or
wrongly called biltong) had never been used to anv
extent in Australia, as it is in S. Africa. The
reason is that the process of making the right goods
is a secret. This scribe had his fare paid from
S’Africa for the express purpose of showing one of
the leading Australian meat works the way to make
the proper Boer article. The Australian experiment
was most successful, and the article was. sold on the
South African markets as “dor beste Boeren beltong.”
'the beste beltong sold for years was in the years
1906 and 1907, and S’African'ders, who will remember
the splendid quality of the goods during these years,
may be surprised to know it was Australian and not
Afrikander beltong. Before the meat was turned into
beltong it was. worth sd. per lb., and when it became
a pound of beltong it was worth, with handlin'*"
salt and pepper, 7id. per lb., or BJd. per lb. landed fn
S Africa, and the price realised there ran from Is. 3d
to 3s. per lb. But Australia would not act up to
instructions, and lost the business by puttin- too
much pepper and salt on the goods, notwithstanding
that they were advised that the first four shipments
(each of an average of 10,0001 b. weight) were all
that could be wished. South America now gets a
little of the making of “Boer” beltong.

Tekelo”: The time is nearly due to sing
the swan song of the yamberas, the beautiful and
graceful war-canoes of the Western Solomons. With
the passing of the present generation of old men
native naval architecture will become a lost art. It
supplied one of the finest examples of native craft-
manship in the Pacific. The decay of head-hunting
and the growth of trade is responsible. To-day the
native gathers a few thousand cocoanuts and buys a
prosaic dinghy or cutter. He finds this more con-
genial than spending laborious months in the construc-
tion of a marine poem of wood and shell. Only on
rare occasions nowadays are these dwindling fleets of
gorgeous, high-prowed, 30-men canoes launched. Fol-
lows a spectacle I shall never forget. A palm-
fringed bay on Vella a-swoon in a brilliant forenoon.
Suddenly a shout! “Here come the Yamberas.”
Round a distant point swing six giant canoes, all
their shellwork a-glitter in the sunlight, the glisten-
ing bodies of the natives swinging and swaying in
unison. As if wielded by one man the 180 paddles
dip, swing and flash. “Thunt!” “Thunt!” “Tliunt!”
slap the paddles against the canoe sides at every
stroke, and the rhythm of it is irresistible. By this
time -the shore natives are frantically excited, but
the oncoming warriors appear not to heed them.
Half-way across the bay the stroke is changed.
“Thunt!” ! “Thunt!” ! “Thunt!”——! go
the paddles now, the tap ' being omitted at every
alternate stroke. Suddenly all is quiet! The canoes
are gliding noiselessly on, the paddles dipping swiftly,
but silently. The speed is incredible. They sweep
along thus to within 200yds. of the beach and then
open out for the last grand burst. Like a signal gun
roars out the paddle song—and away they go. One
hundred and eighty glistening, dripping demons swing
and sway in a mad struggle for supremacy. The
crowd rocks and roars. The time is perfect and in-
conceivably rapid. The wizard hulls foam and
leap like live things. Perched in the high stern of
each a native is performing prodigies with the steer-
ing paddle, whirling it from side to side with won-
drous dexterity and skill. The towering prows rock
and sway dangerously under the tremendous power of
each baresark impulse. And so amidst a lather of
foam, maddened water and shrieking populace they
surge to the beach. Vale! yambera.

The Oldest Inhabitant: Somebody has
again been hazing the tame historian on S. M.
Herald. Publishing a picture of Mitchell’s historic
tree, he says Sir Thos. Mitchell, in 1835, named the
place “Port Bourke.” As a matter of fact, Mitchell
built a stockade as a protection against the Darling
blacks and named it Fort Bourke.

posed of. When night fell the prop stack at the mine
had assumed colossal proportions. So had the cheque
that Bill bore homewards in his vest-pocket.
