“Arno” : Mati Takoa was the chief of the
local tribe, and a man of some standing, even in the
eyes of the pakehas. He took to wife a handsome
but frivolous wahine, and was much devoted to her ;
so much so that she presumed to flout him openly
and make love to one Watane, whose ardor was never
more than lukewarm. Mati, observing the facts of
the case, expostulated with his wife, and pointed
out that if she persisted in wooing Watane with
caresses and cookery, he (Mati) would be
obliged to cut her throat. He spoke with
calmness and dignity, but the silly woman
only laughed. One day Mati, coming home
unexpectedly, found her again entertaining
her lover. Watane, much relieved at the
husband’s arrival, departed, leaving the
prepared feast of pig to its rightful owner.
Mati made good use of his knife on the
pork, being hungry, and then set to work
to sharpen it (the knife, I mean, not the
pork), explaining to his wife that he was
preparing for the promised operation on
her throat. Even then the self-deluded
woman laughed, trusting to his undoubted
affection for her. It was not until he was
ready to begin that she attempted to escape. Then
it was too late. Mati finished the job neatly and ex-
peditiously, laid the body out in correct state for the
tangi, called his relatives and explained matters, and
walked some miles into the township and gave him-
self up to the police. The Court handed Mati 20
years, afterwards shortened to 15, and after that
Mati, grey and sterner-looking, again took his place
at the head of the tribe. Experience had not soured
i him, nor destroyed his faith in woman, for soon he
' married again, choosing a wahine as young and
handsome as she whom he had executed (for he did
not count his deed murder, though aware of pakeha
opinion and deferring to it). With such a warning
the lady was not likely to attempt any philandering,
and the two still live happily ever after, or words to
that effect.
* * * *
Pick . Can anybody tell me how the
Mack cockatoo so unerringly locates his titbit, the
white wood-grub? I can understand the fowl’s
ability to select the right kind of tree for wood-
grubs, because when after eel bait I can pick them
out myself; but I have to chop the wood into
fragments, and follow the passages along the limbs
and roots, sometimes for yards, before I catch up
to my grub. Cocky simply alights upon a branch,
or clings to the tree trunk, and after a few hops
and tappings with his beak, pecks straight in to
the required depth, and drags out the oyster. It
also worries me to know why the worm calmly
awaits the coming destruction. There is nothing
of the Jack Donaldson about this more than any
other worm; still he has plenty of time to move
on, for the cockatoo will sometimes spend hours in
beaking an opening to the larder. Apparently,
since he always strikes grub at the bottom, the
worm never moves the fraction of an ell.
“Eddyson”: Shannis McGowan and Shin
Halligan were enemies most magnificent from never
a cause man knew of but the native and natural joy
of combat. They were both small, scrubby men, with
small knowledge and artifice in battle, and never a
taste of respect for civilised usage or the quality of
ringcraft. When Shin was moved to get at Shamus
he consulted no lexicons, nor concerned himself at all
with rules of etiquette and table-manners for pugil-
ists. He just got down to work, his business being to
stop Shamus as soon as possible, and sit by on a
fence in homely pride while the relatives of McGowan
towed him home by the heel. Nor was McGowan a
tidy or punctilious fighter to the extent that would
excite remark. If he could get a kick in it was not
for him to neglect his opportunities, or fly in the face
of Providence. He kicked and was thankful to the
blessed saints. Give the heroes three pints apiece
and set them together in a 10-acre paddock, and they
would provide a whole half-day’s diversion. Let Shin
or Shamus have six long ones true to measure, and
of his own volition he sought his horn foe high and
low. There was an occasion when Shamus was out
with a bell the night long, but Mrs. Halligan had
Shin in the cellar that night, and was sitting on the
door, the full 14st. of her, and wouldn’t let him up
to do battle for the honor of his house. One night
a hailstorm most terrific struck all Bungaree broad-
side on, and Shamus awoke to find hunks of missiles
as big as spuds crashing through his windows.
McGowan reached for the two hobs of his chimney,
and went out with a bound. A slat of ice biffed him
on the head, and his heels went up, and he hit a cruel
world with the nape of his neck. Up came Shamus again
whirling his bricks, but before he could take aim a
hailstone like an ostrich egg bounced on his skull,
and down went McGowan to the bottom of the sea!
He was some little time coming up out of the great
waters this trip, but eventually he balanced on his
heels once more, and there was a good red brick in
each fist. Biff, biff, biff came the hailstones big as
melons, and finally “crash” the emperor of all hail-
stones, a young iceberg, splintered on the frontal
lobes of Shamus McGowan, and the great head broke
under it. McGowan was gathered up later, and taken
to a hospital to be renewed and renovated, and it was
two days before he recalled his own name and his
purpose in life. Then, with one wild eye peering
out of his bandages, said he: “ ’Tis no dishcredit t’
me, dochter, dear, me bein’ here. Didn’ he have his
whole fam’ly at me—didn’ he? Didn’ he have him-
self V every dom Halligan in th’ disthric’ trowin’
gibbers, so ut a poor man had nivir a chance at all
wid th shlathers iv them like rain. But, docthor
dartin’, may be yid make out th’ bit order for me
fer wan dthray-load iv thim fine, round pebbles big
ez yer fisht from th’ Crown puddlers. ’Tis very busv
I’H be with no time t’ waste waitin’ when I come
well. Halligan has took V throwin’ gibbers; V will
yeh hurry wid me cure, docthor darlin’?—for never
was a McGowan of thim all a thought behind-hand
wid a brick.”
Gome Ancient Crimes and Criminals.
H. B. Irving is said to be editing a book
(ye gods) on the subject of the murder at
Whitechapel (London) of Harriet Lane, by
her paramour, Henry Wainwright. He was
the second notorious murderer of that name,
if certain discrepancies in the spelling are
overlooked, for one Thomas Griffiths W aine-
wright made something of a splash in this
country long ago. T. G. W. was an Eng-
lish gentleman of the worst description, an
officer in the British army, a painter of
some value, a journalist of size (his pen-
name of “Janus Weathercock” was well
known in the London Magazine about 90
years ago), a magnificent spender of money,
a vigorous forger and an energetic poisoner.
Most of the people whom he poisoned were
heavily insured in his favor—one of them
tor £lB,OO0 —-but “Janus Weathercock”
always professed to be an artist. He repu-
diated sordid motives, and declared that he
poisoned liis sister-in-law, not because lie
had pecuniary objects in view, but because
the girl had “thick ankles,” and was a blot
on the landscape.
Thomas Griffiths Wainewright died of
apoplexy at Hobart in 1852.
The Wainwright that H. B. Irving is
alleged to be concerned with at present had
been a London dealer in horse-hair, or some
such merchandise—a flash, stage-struck fel-
low who knocked about among cheap actors
and used to shout tripe suppers and eel pies
for Last End ballet girls. All the surround-
ings of his fatal indiscretion were sordid
enough, and Heaven only knows why he
should be put into a book 40 years after his
decease.
Having killed his woman when his funds
were running low, he buried her in a cellar.
Ihe arrival of some perfectly ordinary
tradesmen to do repairs filled him with an
idea that the remains were being looked for.
whereas the remains were of such small
account that they hadn’t even been missed.
8o he hurriedly dug up the corpse, and
packed it carelessly in canvas, and sum-
moned an odd-job man to assist him in re-
moving the pared.
Wainwright left his assistant in charge
ot the thing whilst he went to hail a cab.
and the inquisitive outsider fooled about
with the bundle until a dead hand burst
through the stitching. The police couldn’t
tail to follow, up a clue like that, so Wain-
wright was duly tried and condemned. Ilis
theatrical tendencies remained with him to
the end. On the night before his execution,
it was said, the warders gathered outside
the ontei* iron barred door of the condemned
cell (the solid inner door being open), and
gave Wainwright an enthusiastic encore for
his recitation of “The Charge of the Light
Brigade.” Perhaps it was this incident
which appealed to H. B. Irving’s theatrical
imagination. '
Some people,, by the way, held that it was
Henry s brother Thomas who murdered
Harriet Lane. Thomas is said to have con-
fessed on his death-bed that he did the deed,
but then the death-bed of a man named
ihomas doesn’t amount to much, especiallv
when he dies in gaol.
The odd-job man who held the bundle of
dead body while Wainwright went for a cab
came to a bad end, of course, like the person
who made a little limelight for himself out
of the Mount Rennie horror in Sydney. His
name was Stokes, but that had nothing to
do with his bad end. From being an honest
street-corner loafer, odd-jobber, beggar and
lamp-post leaner he developed into a sort
of prophet. He got swelled head. The lime-
light was. too much for him. He declared
that a voice from Heaven had told him to
open the bag of remains which he was hold-
ing while Wainwright went for the cab. Lie
declared that another voice from Heaven
bade him chase the four-wheeler containing
Wainwright and the gruesome relic, so he
girded up his tail-coat and ran. He at-
tempted to address public meetings about his
“wrongs,” being seized with an idea that he
was some kind of a dispossessed heir or
dethroned king. A few cranks of the kind
that used to follow the Tichborne Claimant
gave him their countenance. They “took up
his case” whep he hadn’t one. He was handed
ss. on a public platform by a very rich man
to keep him in beer till the alleged clouds
of contumely and scoff had drifted by.
I inally he stole something worth about 2d.,
and the gaol swallowed him up, and he was
no more heard of. j
When you come to think of it, an amazing
number of people are no more heard of.
Gouger ’: My only excuse is that he was
drunker than he ought to have been and I was more
desperately impecunious than usual. His chief griev-
ance in life seemed to be that a hand of his watch
had lost itself. Examining the timepiece with a
craftsmanlike air, I guaranteed to replace the broken
hand for half a dollar. The watch, my swag, and
myself were soon in debate at the rear of the pub.
'I o make it look like proper workmanship, I took
20min. in painting the hand on the watch (this very
pen did it) before I took the masterpiece to its
waiting owner. He was enthusiastic, and showered
four drinks upon me and a dollar as well—all -on
account of the fine way the new hand worked.

The London “season,” beginning in May,
will have a Great International Fasting
Competition for one of its side-shows. All
countries are invited to send along their
most durable non-feeding citizen, and the
bloke whose stummick stands the longest
spell of gnawing pains will get a 50-guinea
cup in addition to “first” money. The placed
fastens will get cups, and £2OO will be dis-
tributed in cash prizes among the also
starved.
If Customs Minister Groom can bellow in-
furiatedly like 14 Tummas Bents it is to be
hoped that he will do it when a deputation
from Colac (Vic.) Council presently asks
him to remit the duty, amounting to
£97, on a motor road-roller that it has
imported. There is no special reason why
a municipal council —which is merely a num-
ber of citizens in concrete —shouldn’t
help to protect Australian industries,
just the same as individual citizens. One
of Colac’s councillors excused the importa-
tion on the ground that that class of roller
isn’t made in Australia. And it isn’t likely
to be if the duty is to be remitted every time
a wealthy district like Colac goes and does
a cadge.
A pathetic phase of Sydney life was re-
vealed by a discursive policeman at the Cen-
tral Court the other Monday. Talking of
drunks (and there is a lot of talk about
drunks—“over-Sunday drunks”- —at the Cen-
tral on the morning after the Day
of Rescht) he mentioned that it had become
habitual for many persons to become hope-
lessly sozzled in doorways on Saturday night.
They try to take the beer home for use on
the Lord’s Day, but meet with the tempta-
tion of an intolerable thirst, and succumb.
The road to ll—, that is to say, to a non-
alcoholic Sabbath, is paved with dead
marines and beer-stained doorsteps.
A lot of Permanent and Reproductive rail-
way plant is liable to be sent to the scrap-
heap by a new screw system lately patented:
that is, if the thing on a large scale will
work as pleasantly as does the model. The
carriages get their motive-power from a cable
in a slot below, but it is an enormous screw.
It never stops, and passengers get on, as in
the Moving Platform, while the open car
is crawling by at three miles an hour. The
“platform’s” weakness was its slow pace
throughout; hut here a station section has
the spirals only a foot apart, while in the
tunnels—the thing is designed mainly for
underground or tube work —they are Bft.
apart. Thus the small carriages speed up
automatically to 24 miles an hour on the
between-stations run. The advantage over
the plain cable is that the carriages never
release their grip. Therefore, it is claimed,
no drivers (or brakes, or signals) will be
needed. llow passengers are to get on in a
crowded station while others are getting off
is not stated, and the slightest slip, on an
unstopping, automatic system, might be seri-
ous. The man who fell at the forward end
of a platform, and left his body behind while
his head was being whirled away to the next
station at 24 miles an hour would he tempted
never to travel by the blessed thing again.
“A. Cheffar” : The most striking example
of a man’s “double” that ever came under
my notice was in a case heard in a Sydney
police court, where William Brown was
charged with a rather serious assault. Seven
witnesses of good character fully and un-
hesitatingly identified Brown as the assailant.
The late W. P. Crick defended the accused,
and his cross-examination consisted of a
couple of questions only: “Are you positive
this is the man who committed the assault?”
and “You’ve no doubt about him?” The wit-
nesses were dead-sure. “Pa” Delohery was
hearing the case, and Crick remarked to
him: “Your Worship might very well won-
der why I am apparently wasting your time
in defending this man in the face of such evi-
dence as you have heard. But you’ll see a
peculiar development presently.” When the
case for the prosecution had closed. Crick
said: “I am about to make an unusual appli-
cation to your Worship. But I hope you’ll
grant it.” “vVhat is it. Mr. Crick?” said
the Bench. “I want your Worship to have
all the witnesses taken out and kept in a
room for a few minutes.” The Bench thought
it was certainly a peculiar application, but
granted it, and the witnesses were taken to
the magistrates’ room. Crick then asked
that John Smith should be called. Upon
that being done there came into the court and
stood alongside Brown that man’s living
image. Brown, looking in a mirror, could not
have seen a more perfect reflection of himself.
Each man was about oft. 9in. high, fair-haired,
blue-eyed, fresh-complexioned, and heavily-
moustached in exactly the same tint of light-
brown. They were square-shouldered, smart-
looking fellows, dressed in blue serge suits of
exactly the same shade and cut, and bore the
unmistakable air of chaps who follow the
sea for a living. As the two stood along-
side each other the witnesses were called in
one at a time and asked to point out which
of the men had committed the assault. The
case fell with a crash.
Probably the best summing-up of the pre-
sent situation in Federal politics was sup-
plied a day or two ago by Sydney Tele-
graph :
While the general prediction is that nothing- ex-
traordinary will happen immediately, yet the situa-
tion is so complex, and can so easily be disturbed,
that every suggestion of future probabilities may be
completely dislocated by a sudden intervention of
some novel or unexpected incident.
Profound cogitation over this matter leads
to the conclusion that something will happen
unless it doesn’t, and it probably won’t; but,
on the other hand, it may.

The Athanasian creed was burlesqued by Ilone, the
publisher, and he was prosecuted .for it. lie turned

St. James’s Old Melbourne Cathedral was named
in compliment to James Smith, a. squatter, who built
the first little wooden church there. Thus Coppin,
cutting up James Palmer’s Hawthorn property,
named it St. James’s Park.
After long delays, Archbishop Clarke has managed
to open (lie transferred St. James’s, in King-street
north, corner of an insignificant little thoroughfare
named Batman-street. Yet it only got that name a
decade ago. An indignant citizen wrote: “No! In-
stead of Collins-street being named after the man
who deserted Victoria as no good (Talk about
Amerigo Vespucci and Columbus!), it ought to be
named Batman.” Whereupon another demanded that
it should be called. Fatman. And so Collins will
stick till the last syllable of recorded time, both in
Slowbart and Melbourne.
The new Old St. James’s is slightly different out-
side, but miraculously reproduced inside. As Arch-
bishop Clarke said, “You can imagine yourself in the
Church as it opened in 1842.” Meanwhile they are
collaring the dibs for the old site, where there was
oceans of room for the reconstruction. But then the
old site was valuable and had to he turned into
money. Any old site is good enough for a church,
but a warehouse is particular about the company it
keeps.
When Earl Grey was energetically push-
ing his pet hobby, town planning, in Maori-
land recently he mentioned that most Fog-
land towns have only one acre of open space
to every 635 people, while Letchworth Garden
City, the alleged marvel of modern town plan-
ning that the frantic Earl is always whoop-
ing about, lias one acre of open space to
every 290 of population. When the Earl’s
whoop had died -down somewhat a tame
architect in Maoriland pointed out to him
that Christchurch (M.L.) possessed one
acre of open space to every 157 of popula-
tion, thus proving that “the biawsted kolo-
nies” are not exactly wandering along in
heathen darkness after all. The town-plan-
ning Earl’s reply was silence.

The paraffin craze is spreading over Maori-
land like the coming winter. Large quanti-
ties of the refined, liquid oil are being swal-
lowed in the belief that this deodorised first
cousin to kerosene is capable or curing evert -
thing from Bright’s complaint to common
corns. The suffragette sex is going specially
strong on the new cure, and, in Auckland
particularly, an enormous sale, at fancy
prices, for this oil is springing up. Doctors
started the craze by ordering doses of the
lubricant for indigestion, and, of course, the
glad news that it worked wonders soon flew
tit) and down and in and out and all round
about. “Try paraffin, my dear,” is now the
stock -advice at tea-sipping and gossip con-
tests, and anyone who doesn’t consume oil
like an engine these days is looked upon as
being very ancient goods indeed. Doctors
are now loudly j>rolosting that the best cure-
all is still “Exercise,” with a big E; but
.point out that if you eat too much dinner
and can’t squeeze' time to jruiL a- mile you
can do worse than gulp an ounce of paraffin.
It would be an ideal substit me for exercise
in no-license districts if it only tasted differ-
ently. but it doesn’t. Nothing ever does.
There are crimes and crimes. The worst
crime in England is the laying of a drag in
fox-lmnting country. A drag is a trail of
aniseed or some other -compound, which puts
the hounds off the scent of the fox. Recently
a malefactor or anarchist laid a drag across
country which was infested by the Tedworth
Hounds; and the rightly infuriated Master
of the Tedworth Hounds issued the follow-
ing letter to tenant farmers: —
Another gross, insult to myself, as Master of the
Tedworth Hounds,- was perpetrated on Friday m the
form of laying a drag from Longstock. I look to
you, the tenant farmers in the limit, to take every
precaution to prevent such scoundrels from laying
drags across your land in the future. Those who
commit these outrages are of the lowest type of
scoundrels, and not fit to bear the name of English-
man. Those, who know their names and are shielding
them are every hit as had. I look to you, the tenant
farmers, to use every, effort in your power to show
up these blackguards and bring their names before the
public.
The pact about not being fit to bear the
name of Englishman must have hurt, as it
was intended to. The quaint thing is that
London Times prints this delightful effort as
a mere news item under the heading: “In-
terference with Hunting: A Master’s Pro-
test.”
THE POORER THIEF.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Plum” Williams, the prince of Australian criminals,
furnished in the end a striking lesson in the futility
of law-breaking. Williams lay in a Melbourne
hospital unrecognised for a fortnight, and died of
physical collapse, his warring against society having
simply worn him out. But Williams liad friends.
They foil fid him in the hospital mortuary on Sunday,
and buried him with honor yesterday. “Plum” would
have shared a pauper's grave had these pals not dis-
covered him in time. —Melbourne paper.
Poor “Plum,” the buried King of Thieves,
Financially was “bust” ;
For him no club or paper grieves;
Upon his grave you’ll find but leaves.
He should have been a Trust.
He should have been a Trust, I say,
And robbed with might and main,
Attended office ev’ry day,
And burgled in a wholesale way,
Say down in Flinders-lane.
The Trust is strong. It never dies.
It has ten thousand hands,
And twenty thousand eager eyes,
And books and books of figured lies.
Alone the poor thief stands.
Alone the poor thief stands and fights
The Law —his eager foe.
The rich thief up two marble flights
Behind plate glass enjoys the rights
That only rich thieves know.
Have done! have done with warehouse doors
And midnight prowling! That
Is useless. Thieve on several floors,
With splendor and plate-glass and stores!
If you’re a thief, BE FAT!
What wasted frame is this they stow
Down there with honest grief?
Friend, take a last long look below!
lie took the Retail Way, and so—
Here lies the Poorer Thief!
N.SAV.

Yarraside fragment:—
Watt’s Cabinet spent a vigorous day lately con-
sidering its business programme for next session.
This is how it panned out: The handling grain in
bulk proposals, already fully inquired into, were post-
poned pending the report of an expert to be imported
from Canada; the alleged White Slave traffic was
handed over to John Weary for deeper inquiry';
Williamstown Graving Dock undertaking was de-
ferred to give Watt a chance to try and squeeze a
few pounds out of Prime Minister Cook ; the timber-
seasoning impasse was hung up pending the report
of an “independent and competent man” ; the
Itiverina railways project was pushed aside for a
Itoyal Commission to probe into ; and the Gippsland
harbor works are to be reported on by experts with
a view to subsequent consideration. However the
Cabinet reached finality on two matters. It agreed
to give a couple of shillings or so for a memorial
tablet to be placed on the building now being erected
on the site of the first Government House, and it
decided that in future landowners should get wire-
netting for their river frontages at half-price. It was a
strenuous day’s work, and at the finish of it there was
not a Minister but carried on hia brow the grim
marks of brain fag and utter nervous exhaustion.
THE UNHOLY RUSH.
He did not feel altogether competent to speak with
authority on borrowing matters, and he stood open to
correction.
The President. Compared to you, we are mere
amateurs. (Laughter.)
—From conference of State Premiers.
AUSTRALIA: “ Amateurs!! Heavens, I wonder
what professionals won id be like.”
The Weary State’s ornamen-
ta l Minister of Railways mooted
a new principle in railway con-
struction lately, when suggesting
to a deputation of Ardmona
fruit-growers and Wyuna closer
settlers that a cockspur line
which they asked for, to give
connection with Mooroopna,
should be built and worked by
the interested municipality, or,
that failing, by themselves —the
fruit-growers having offered to
guarantee the Department
against loss if the State would
tackle the job. It is a quaint
idea —and suggestive when care-
fully insuected. If the munici-
pality proposed to build the line
by borrowing, then the State,
could do it cheaper; if it hoped
; to build it out of its revenue from
rates then it would have to
neglect necessary road-mainten-
ance and other work. Also, as
this particular shire gets a large
annual subsidy from the State,
the project would merely amount
to robbing Peter to pay Paul;
while if the idea was that the
settlers should tax themselves
to carry out the work, the State
could just as well do the taxing.
Perhaps Watt foresees the end
of his borrowing. capabilities.
After reading 257 more paragraphs about
the N.S.W. Town-Planning Association, pub-
lished amid, much "applause in the Sydney
Tory press, The Bulletin again asks with
disgust why these people don’t go out and
plan some towns. They can’t do it by hang-
ing round the big cities which are either
planned already or have grown up casually.
The habit of lingering outside the S. M.
Herald offite on town-planning business is
especially futile, seeing that the Herald’s
own office looks like an ancient and tangled
crow’s nest in a wilderness of mean streets.
Among the names of those who want the
Liberal nomination for the Senate in N.S.W.
appear some ancient recollections. One is
ex-Sal. Army man Bruntnell, who got the
miserable; dilapidated “chuck” at Annandale
last year, after having been in and out of
Parliament for quite a while. Another is
Pumpkin Smith, who was a State member
in N.S.W. 34 years ago, and a State Minis-
ter 25 years ago, He had a brief spell in
the Australian Parliament, where he acted
as political dresser to George Reid, and blew
the bit of fluff off him and adjusted his coat-
tail when the great Yes-No was going to
make a great Yes-No oration. Then, in
1906, Carr beat Smith at Macquarie, and he
never rose again.
A cablegram posted from London last Fri-
day was to the effect that “for weeks the
country has been trembling on the verge of
civil war.” Well! The Bulletin knows
a man named Thompson, who has been trem-
bling for weeks on the verge of killing his
neighbor’s dog, which barks at night. There
isn’t much difference in the size of the two
events.
The S. M. Herald last week, discoursing
re the alleged brotherhood of Britain and the
United States, worked off the old gag that
“blood is thicker than water.” It might
have added that wooden-headedness is thicker
still. _ After all it isn’t blood but language
that is thicker than water. The Englishman,
who is an expatriated German, gets on fairly
well with the Highland Celt who is supposed
to have come from Spain, and with the local
Jew who came from Palestine, and with the
acclimatised Slav in the United States, be-
cause he can talk to them. Yet he doesn’t
make much progress with his German cousin
at Berlin because as a rule he can’t talk to
hi m.
Last Saturday—same day as S.A. Agent-
General Kirkpatrick., .returned, to Adelaide,
leaving his post filled by a deputy until F. W.
Young goes to London towards the end of the
year—that State got a cable from its Trade
Commissioner in England that he, too, wants
to come back. It seems a Heaven-sent oppor-
tunity for letting one man fill the two jobs.
“Major” Norton has been a good man for
S.A.’s Trade since 1906, but lias been very
restless of late. His, last six years have
worked out thus: Ist, on hi& job; in London ; •
2nd, back in Adelaide as head 'of Produce
Department, varied by a run north to report
on trade prospects in Asia; 3rd, on his job,
in London ; 4th, member of a private firm, in
England ; sth and 6th, on his job',' in London.
And now he resigns again, abruptly.
THE SPENDTHRIFT.
The boom in the State revenue continues.
TAXPAYER: “ Ugh! Wot's ther use of giving
'im ther 'oof. He'll only do it in.”
A young immigrant was presented at Mel-
bourne City Court Bench last week as a
"rotter” who might be given the option of re-
turning to hjs native England. He owned
up to three convictions for theft in Australia,
also he pleaded guilty to being the son of a
clergyman. From which interesting admission
it appears, that when a parson has a con-
spicuously black sheep for a son he doesn’t
rejoice in his parental opportunities to lead
the young animal to repentance. The re-
verend shepherd doesn’t say to himself that
charity begins at home, and the first duty of
a soul-saver is to rescue his family. On the
contrary, he packs the scapegrace off to Aus-
tralia in the disguise of a “desirable immi-
grant.”
“Green Tape,” from the edge of the scuffle:
The ructions .between Premier Holman’s crowd in
the N.S.W. Parliamentary Labor caucus and “Dick”
Meagher’s push has revived the old rumors that the
former has his eyes fixed .Londonward on the Agent-
Generalship. It is practically a gaping secret that
Holman would have been in the billet before this
but for. the fact, that Beeby’s revolt at a critical
time made him Mrsh back from England to draft
into line again the wavering Labor ranks. “Tim”
Coghlan is now about due for retirement, and it is
said does not wish to go on. If he throws his
marble in at once some chairwarmer will be found
until Holman is ready to flee from the tottering
precipice of debt he has built up.
OTHER NATIONS.
Auckland, April 24. —Referring to defence, in
the course of a speech yesterday, the Prime Minister
(Mr. Massey) reminded his audience that they were
not many days’ steam from nations that, although
friends to-day, might be enemies to-morrow. He did
not wish to mention names.
THE PAINTER OF THE WORD PICTURE:
“ Wonder if they'll know who I mean it
for.”
Governor Macgregor, of Queensland, has
expressed the opinion that England’s great
enemy is luxury. Well, about nine-tenths
of the British people dodge that enemy very
successfully.
“Maorilander”: The trouble with the
Henry Holland type ,of man is that he re-
presents destruction —not construction. After
all the coral insect which does its humble
scrap towards building an island is greater
than the person who burned the library of
Alexandria.
The Oldest Inhabitant sees a friend off: —
John Bennett, who was deemed the doyen of thea-
trical managers, has crossed the Divide at the age
of 85 years, and has been buried at Gore Hill
(Sydney). He was a Sydney native, born in Lower
George-street, where his father, Gabriel Bennett,
carried on a boot and shoe business. John started
out early in vlife to make his own way. He told
this writer that the oldest memory he had was Sam
Terry’s funeral, with its military band and Free-
masons’ display. That was in 1838. Mr. Bennett,
in his early manhood, was the head of a large iron-
mongery in Melbourne. From that job he drifted
into the amusement business, and became associated
with theatricals in the late ’so’s. G. V. Brooke
was one of his earliest connections, and he was asso-
ciated with most of the stars who visited Australia
in the days of Bennett’s activity. Late in 1871 he
became lessee of the “Old Vic.,” in Pitt-street, which
stood <on the site of McCathie, Limited. He opened
trade with a company which included George Darrell
and his wife, the lady we knew in our youth as
Fanny Cathcart (she became Mrs. Robert Heir, and,
as a widow, married Darrell). The theatre was burnt
in 1880. Bennett, who was uninsured, took the
Gaiety in Castlereagh-street for a season. It was
not an attractive house. In later years he was a real
estate speculator, Rosehill racecourse being one of
his ventures. Likewise he owned Brush Farm, after-
wards resumed by the Government for reformatory
purposes. In the grounds of Brush FaTm the re-
mains of the historic or bogus or alleged George
Barrington were buried. Mrs. Bennett, a daughter
and six sons survive the old manager.

LUXURY!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He would like to know what at the present moment
was the greatest enemy of England. He believed it
was luxury. The people at home were realty too
well off. —Queensland Gov. Macgregor, at St. Georye's
Day dinner.
The poor o’ London shook their rags
Beneath a wint’ry sun;
The poor o’ London slept on bags,
When summer days were done ;
And when I asked them what was Wrong,
And why they wailed: “O Lord 1 How long?”
They cried: “The curse that dogs our days
And frets with woe our thorny ways
Is neither rags nor Hunger keen,
Nor shameful want we may not screen,
Nor hiting wind, nor icy sheen,
But Luxury !
I laughed to hear them answer so,
And said: “Good folk, ye rave!
And speak like them through life who go
In purple to the grave.
How can ye say ’tis Luxury
That brings about the wrongs ye dree?
When all your bread’s a bitter-black,
And night by night a bed ye lack—
When gaping roof and bitter sky,
And shrunken form your tale deny
That all your miseries rooted lie
In Luxury?”
“ Tis Luxury still,” the answer came
In accents weird and wild ;
“The gilded lord, the pampered dame,
The silken-coated child—-
’Tis they that breed our hapless state,
And shut in Pity’s face the gate;
’Tis they that drive us, meek and dumb,
To herd in lane and slimy slum,
While God’s green land is fenced here
To form a haunt for fallow deer.
Ah, yes, the source of ev’ry tear
Is Luxury 1”
And even as their tale they told
A chariot thundered by—
Its lackeys, brave in green and gold,
Looked down with scornful eye;
A countess with her belted carl,
Her diamonds rare, her priceless pearl,
Sat back and smiled serene and proud
Above the gaunt and grimy crowd,
And wondered, doubtless, how ’t could be
That certain folk profess to see
The root of Wrong and Poverty,
And half the ills of England Free
In Luxury !
QUEENSLAND.

TO WILLIAM STEWART, formerly of
Mangonui, Auckland, New Zealand,
but last heard of in Queensland, and to all
persons claiming by, through or under him.
Pursuant to the directions of His Honor
Sir Robert Stout, K.C.M.G., Chief Justice
of New Zealand, undyer Section 25 of the
Public Trust Office Amendment Act, 1913,
you are hereby called upon to send in to the
undersigned, on or before the Ist day of
October, 1914, your claim- to part of the
estate of CHRISTINA STEWART, late of
Mangonui, Auckland, widow, deceased, who
died on the 9th October, 1880, intestate,
failing which such estate will be distributed
among the persons entitled thereto disregard-
ing your claims.
Dated at Wellington this 19th day of
March, 1914.
J. W. MACDONALD,
Solicitor to the Public Trust Office,
Wellington, New Zealand.

merly of Wellington, New Zealand,

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Tbe God of Winter came out of doors in
Sydney on Saturday, and regaled himself with toe-
ball. It was a great and splendid opening. Nearly
40,000 people attended the Rugby League’s matches.
Another 5000 or 6000 wandered in to look at the
Union games, and the Australian Rulers and the
Soccer crowd had their thousands. Altogether, it
was a fine start —and a strenuous one. No less than
about 10 men had to be carried off the field with
twisted ankles, broken fingers, concussed brains, shat-
tered collar-bones and what-not. League and Union,
Soccer and Australian Rulers all have to make up
their minds that these accidents, in the words of
Minister Hoyle, “Must Cease.” There is no reason
why they should occur. They are due entirely to the
crass brutality and unscrupulous play of a few
selfish limelight-seekers. As far as they can the
football scribes should reprobate this sort of tommy-
rot, and unhesitatingly denounce the man who is
guilty of careless or rough play. And, as fame is
the thing' these rotters are out to get at the cost of
anyone’s limbs or life, the influence of the press is
mighty. If they receive bricks instead of plaudits
they will play the game properly.
In the League competition an amazed world
watched the Premier Club, Eastern Suburbs, over-
thrown by Glebe. It was only by a narrow margin
truly, but it was enough. The figures were 14 to
12. At the first, the premiers raged like pampered
lions, and piled up 12 to two. And then the but-
chers, in all their glory and their red giuernseys,
pulled themselves together. They let loose a splendid
series of charges, in the van of which poured forth
the mighty kicker, A. B. Burge. In a few minutes
the scpre was lifted to nine to .12, and then the
half-time whistle went. In the second half a tre-
mendous contest raged, till at last one Algie strug-
gled over the line, and the mighty hoof of Burge
added the extra points. Eastern Suburbs, though it
raged like the heathen and imagined .a vain thing
with its feet, never made up that two points, and
the whistle of fate blew; it out. For the rest, North
Sydney trampled mightily over Annandale by 23 to
13. The legs hardened by climbing the wild hills
of North Shore were altogether too strong for the
limbs that toiled among the sandy deserts of Annan-
dale. South Sydney handed out the sire of a hiding to
Balmain by 14 to nil. It was awful. Newtown
scrunched Western Suburbs by 15 to five —a veritable
dogjwalloping.
In the Union 'division the results were much more
even, i Eastern Suburbs vanquished Glebe by 14 to
nine after a game that was almost a replica of the
League contest. With the wind behind it Eastern
Suburbs piled up 11 in the first half, but when Glebe
got the breeze at its back there was a mighty change.
Still, Fortune was on the side of the tricolor team,
and Eastern Suburbs finally triumphed. University
hurled Randwick (a new club) on to its back, and
then danced on the prostrate one’s bingey to the
tune of 20 to five. Newtown and Balmain had a
ferocious struggle, and when the breeze blew away
the dust and the dead and the unsupported state-
ments in the debris of false prophecy, it was dis-
covered that Newtown was on top with 24 points to
19. Western Suburbs swallowed Manly with 22 to
six. North Sydney and St. George, after much labor,
swam out with li each.
The tide of disaster that started with the
defeat of the premier team of the League rolled on
to the Soccer division. Annandale, State champion
team and holder of the coveted Rawson Cup, .was
overthrown and killed stone dead by Canterbury with
one goal to nil. It was not a big defeat truly, but
when a champion team fails to even score it leaves
a smell of death lying around. Sydney fell on
Northern Suburbs at the Sports Ground, and smote
it with the jawbone of a lion (four to nil —which
is about equal to 30 to nothing in League and 5000
to 50 in Australian Rules). Balmain’s slaughter of
Glebe was even worse, the butcher suburb going down
by six to nil. Balmain was in a frenzy of delight
over it, for the Tattered Suburb takes its hoofball
very seriously—especially the Soccer portion of it.
Away at Clyde, where the railway congestion is and
the air is ripped with the yells of angered shunters,
Granville cold-bloodedly slew Newtown by six to one.
The way Soccer is growing on the public fancy is
enough to keep the lords of both League and Union
awake at nights.
The Australian Rulers started their compe-
tition the week before, and Saturday was, therefore,
their second round. It produced no surprises in
spite of the reports of sundry daily papers. The
premier club, Sydney, booted North Sydney off the
face of things with 62 points to 16! South Sydney’s
69 to Paddington’s modest 34 left no room for argu-
ment, and Newtown just topped the century with the
neat 100 to Central Western’s 58. Central Western,
it is worth remembering, is a new club, and made a
very creditable stand against so formidable a crowd
as Sydney can put into the field. The three winners
are now level. Next Saturday Newtown has the bye,
and Sydney will crash into South, and great will
be the crash thereof. At the present time there is
a lot of friction over clearances of players from both
teams —in fact, it might be said the envy, hatred
and malice generated by these clearances have left
both Sydney and South Sydney without a single Chris-
tian virtue between them. It will be a strenuous
go as a result.
* * » *
The chief collision in Melbourne League
hoofball on Saturday concerned St. Kilda and Carlton,
and again a delectable exhibition satisfied most of
the 22,000 onlookers. Saints’ system was a trifle
the better and their kicking less erratic, so they
held a large enough lead to swear by up to the middle
of the last quarter. Then the Old Blues put in some
persistent rushes, scored two goals and got three
points ahead —and great was the joy of their bar-
rackers. But there was still 10 minutes to play, and in
that space the determined Tricolors added two goals
and some behinds and ran out 13 points ahead—4B
to Carlton’s 35. This feat so upset some ill-regulated
Carlton supporters that they rushed the ground and
valiantly charged the umpire, a rather competent
and quite impartial official, whom the law fortunately
rescued. Carlton’s committee is hereby advised to
take drastic steps to suppress these disgruntled
cowards. If they are allowed to thus “get to” an
umpire who fails to please them at this early stage
of the proceedings there will be a case of man-
slaughter before the finals are reached, and that won’t
be good for the game. No spectators are permitted
on the playing area at some of the grounds, and there
is no reason why Carlton blackguards should be
allowed extra latitude. The promising Geelong team
came to town and performed a pleasing turn against
South, losing ultimately bv only 11 points—sl to
South’s 62. The other disturbances were mere exer-
cise canters for one side, Essendon downing ’Varsity
by 86 to 50 ; ’Wood smothering Melbourne by 67 to
43; and ’Roy jumping on Richmond by 69 to' 28.
During the latter scuffle a Riclimondite was put out of
play with a twisted ankle, but as an opponent was
equally misfortunate with a broken rib the handicap
was nicely adjusted, so to speak. That was probably
little satisfaction to either sufferer.
None of the Melbourne Ass. games pro-
mised absorbing interest, though, as it turned out, the
new Hawthorn team gave the premiers (’Scray) a
shock that may do them good, and, could it have
lasted would probably have put them down and out
for the day. As it was the board, at the close of the
riot, showed ’Scray 84 points, Hawthorn 64, which is
distinctly promising for novices. Port, alleged to
have a chance of stopping ’Town’s victorious gallop,
discovered said chance to be Buckley’s, and collected
a paltry 37 points to ’Town’s 85. Essendon made an
even worse exhibition of Brighton by piling up 124
points to 45. North demolished P’ran with 74 to 40.
And the Brickies of Brunswick filled the dittoes of
Northcote with astonishment by 113 to 32.
As is generally known, Soccer Football ; s
now organised on an all-Australian basis, and a few
weeks ago the second annual congress of the “Com-
monwealth Football Association” was held in Bris-
bane. Queensland, Victoria, South Australia, Tas-
mania and Noo Sowth were represented by one dele-
gate each, and the honorary secretary of the N. S.
Wales Referees’ Association attended to bring about
the creation of a Commonwealth Referees’ Associa-
tion. An interesting fact brought out was that in
every State the Association game is on a sound
financial footing, and that in every State it is growing
in popular favor. In N.S.W., where the game has
to contend with two forms of Rugby and the Aus-
tralian rules, 1913 proved a record year. One out-
come of the congress is the possibility of an Aus-
tralian Soccer team being present at the next Olym-
piad at Berlin. But in this writer’s opinion the
Association would be better advised to spend the
money in the development of the game locally. It is
Frank Thorn is too prickly for Lee Johnson
(Sydney Stadium).
also proposed to arrange for the visit of an English
team —and that would have a distinctly stimulative
effect on the game. But the first thing to be placed
on a thoroughly sound basis is an all-Australian
championship. THAT would be an excellent thing,
and do more to foster the game than all the foreign
trips that the Association is ever likely to be able
to afford.
“J.G.” : Bendigo Senior Football Associa-
tion or League has fallen into a large lump of hole
through running after more gate money. _ Years ago
—in fact, pretty well ever since Australian football
was made a gate-money game—Bendigo had four
clubs in the district competition. Sometimes the
quartette was composed of three Bendigo clubs and
one from Eaglehawk. But for the last few years the
associated clubs have been Bendigo City, South Ben-
digo, Eaglehawk and California Gully. The Gully
team’s home ground was at North Bendigo, about
midway between ’Go and ’Hawk, as the cabbies call
them. It was a bit out of the beaten track, and
crowds did not roll up in thousands as at the ’Go
and ’Hawk grounds. Still, the patronage was fair.
This year, however, the big panjandrums of Bendigo
hoofball thought that the Gully team should be
emptied out of the League, because they said it was
too weak to put up a decent scrap with the others,
although, as a matter of fact, the club had a better
record than some. But everybody knows that was
not why the Gullvites were enrptied out on to the
roadway by the Bendigo League. The reason was
that the ground did not produce enough of the great
joss cash. So California Gully was shot dead. The
League potentates than thought that they had only
to ask Castlemaine to come into the vacant chairs,
and so make up the four clubs, and have a couple of
matches every week, and fill the League’s coffers.
The Castlemaine Club didn’t bite too well at the
bait, so some of the bigwigs of the League went
cap in hand to the Castle-ites, and entreated them
to join in with Bendigo. Castlemaine, after toying
with the proposal for a week or two, finally tossed it
away. In desperation the Rochester Club, runner-
up in the Goulburn Valley competition last year, was
invited to join the Bendigo League. Rochester, how-
ever, turned the idea down at once. And now the
Bendigo League has only three clubs, which means
only one game per week instead of the usual two.
The senior toe-ballers will not have the same interest
in the competition as they would have if all the
clubs were engaged instead of each club having a
bye every fortnight, and one match a week won’t fill
up the cashbox as much as two a week.
* * * *
W. P. McElhone, “Fighting Mack” of the
stormy days of the Cricket Board of Control, has
decided not to stand for re-election in the gloomy
month of July, when his term of office expires. He
will be missed. For 21 years he has been in the
front rank when there was battle to be done, and
for years and years there was constant battle.
McElhone kicked and clawed his way through, and
stamped the opposition flat. He was hon. secretary
of the Board of Control for 51 years, and was chair-
man in the wild times during which the “Players”
were in rebellion. He is a solicitor in a large way
of six-and-eightpence, and is also an alderman.
* * * *
“0.K.”: In last Bulletin one “Mont-
alex,” in order to introduce a few personal remarks
upon firearms, says that “0.K.” is much in error;
about old-time pistols, certain kinds of which shot
extremely well.” After which, he extols the virtues
of century-old duelling pistols, etc., and proceeds
to air his knowledge of the subject under discussion.
Now, I would like to remark that I never denied the
shooting abilities of old-time pistols, and was not
much in error, or a little in error. “Montalex”
makes a false allegation as a text for his yarn. That’s
all. What I said was that a lady killed an editor
with one of four bullets from a revolver, which she
was able to conceal in her muff' before she started
to give herself four or five chances of hitting a
vulnerable spot. With an old-time pistol she would
have got only one chance of so doing. Also, she
would have had to cock the hammer before pulling
the trigger, and when she pulled the trigger a mere
flash in the pan might have resulted. I never
dreamed of suggesting that a five-chambered revolver
was equal to a good old duelling pistol as an arm
of precision at a few yards range. But who will
deny that it is a more likely weapon for a lady to
get the opportunity to kill an editor with. In thus
replying to the statements of “Montalex” I make
no special complaint against him in particular, but
I protest against the growing tendency to find a
false excuse for writing “corrective” paragraphs.
Why charge me with error when I didn’t err?
At the Otago (M.L.) Lawn Tennis tour-
nament the other day Miss F. Campbell annexed the
Ladies’ Singles Championship for the sixteenth time
in 17 years ! She first won this event away back in
1896, and every season since, with the single excep-
tion of last year, when she did not compete, she has
had her name inscribed on the championship banner.
That is a record hard to beat, and as the lady is
playing as well to-day as ever her name will prob-
ably go on decorating that banner for years to come.
The “inter-collegiate” sports (confined to
Prince Alfred’s and St. Peter’s Schools) in Adelaide
were won this year by the former—6l points to 50.
The only notable performances were by Fowler, for
the winners: 17sec. in the hurdles, and 41 l-ssec. in
a 300yds. hurdle. But in old-boys’ events, Leslie
Wallmann, Australian champion and an old “Saint,”
did the hurdles in 15 4-ssee. The meeting between
him and lan Pender (“Old Prince,” credited with
time a fraction better than even that), was eagerly
looked for, but somehow did not come off.
At Victoria Park Racecourse (Sydney)
on Saturday Guillaux, the amazing French aviator,
did everything that a man could think of. Also, he
did some things that only a raving madman or a
man drunk with speed and mobility would dream of.
Looping the loop is a trifle to him. He loops it
two ways. In one case he flies over his own head
backwards, and in the- other he dives under his own
feet. Soaring over one’s own head is regarded as
an extra-special feat among airmen, but to this
writer’s mind, diving under one’s own feet is worse.
However, in the sum total they are
the same, except that in one case the
man is outside' and in the other inside
the circle that is being described. But
the astounding fact is that for a
time he flies upside down, and the
man is actually suspended in the car
of the aeroplane between it and the
earth. It is thrilling, amazing, won-
derful, and after seeing Guillaux fly
one comes away wondering how on
earth men manage to kill themselves
in aeroplanes. Yet, nothing is more
certain than that the end of this
Gallic daredevil will be smash." He
tempts fate and’ defies it. As long as
his engine works and the wings of his
wonderful mechanical bird do not break, all may be
well. But—the first accident and Guillaux will pass
out in a blaze of glory, and the ruins of him and
his machine will probably fall on the crowd and do
a heap of murder.
At present in Australia there are practi-
cally no laws or ordinances governing - aviators. It is
even an open question if a man who committed an
atrocity 8000 ft. in the air could be haled before the
statutes. That is, if he committed it in an aero-
plane. If he did the job on a mountain top he
would be amenable; but it is doubtful if, in the
present state of the law a foreigner who flew off a
ship 31 miles out with a stolen maiden could be
arraigned in an Australian court of law. However,
the maiden can probably take care of herself. The
party to be considered is the humble crawler on the
earth. Bast week Guillaux flew over Sydney, and a
wonderful sight he was. Other aviators have flown
over the city. And if any of them had crashed
down into George-street from even 3000 ft. and landed
on the tram this writer was in, the community would
have suffered a horrible loss. It is not right that the
community should be exposed to such risks.
The present scribe can’t be spared. And,
after seeing the ease with which Guillaux flies on
his nead and does the awful corkscrew dive, it is
certain that aviators will become almost as frequent
as dragon-flies. But they should not be allowed to
do showy _ gyrations and take needless chances
over the city. Neither should they when giving
exhibitions be allowed to fly at groups of people to
show how close they can go without taking the heads
off the people aforementioned. It’s a fool’s game,
and if the aviator hasn’t the sense to eschew it, the
Law should compel him to do so. Also, it should
compel Guillaux to change his name to Giddyox.
The glory in Sydney bowlsdom on Satur-
day was the overthrow of the lions in the struggle
for the John Young Shield. Among the many
debacles the picturesque one was the squelching of
Ohatswood by Randwick. Chatswood is the premier
club of the season, and Randwick biffed it out by 86
to 78. City, the holder of the Shield, was also
obliterated, by Petersham, the figures being 89 to 81.
It was something to cheer about. It illustrates the
solemn fact, brethren, that the mighty can always
be rubbed in the grass.
* * * *
The Sydney baseballers blew once more
into the limelight on Saturday and made a hefty
beginning. But the finish was poor. The base-
ballers, strangely enough, though mostly cricketers,
were out of condition, and the games were poor.
Paddington and Leichhardt supplied the tussle of the
day, and Paddington won by four runs to three.
Burwood overwhelmed Mosman-North Sydney by 20
to six : Petersham scrunched ’Varsity by 15 to four;
Waverley knocked Lane Cove to blazes with 10 to
nil. By the way, it is whispered that another Yankee
team will shortly descend on Australia.
Sydney lacrosse blew off the mark also on
Saturday last, but failed to produce anything worth
rushing to drink over. Certainly Mosman’s narrow
defeat of Petersham by four goals to three was a
strenuous business; but it was far from brilliant.
Also, the drawn game between Eastern Suburbs and
St. George with one goal each, was a deadly per-
formance of stodge. Balmain overthrew Manly by
12 goals to two, and the triumph of Sydney "over
University by eight goals to four was the ‘bright
streak in a wilderness of dreary competition.
* * * *
“Ike” Earnsliaw, who died at Sydney last
week, was probably the ablest and most cute trainer
of horses the Ma State has ever mothered. The fact
that a prad was in the Earnshaw stable had a de-
pressing effect on the brute’s price with Judas, the
son of Iscariot O’Fergus. He (Earnshaw) trained
nags for 26 years, and among them were some of the
best and greatest winners. He was a mighty man,
who talked little and talked horse not at all.
* * * »
At Wagga (N.S.W.) race meeting last
week three horses picked up the whole programme of
six events —a sort of monopoly that is fortunately
very rare.
* * * *
The idea of sending an English Eight Oar
crew to Australia has fallen through, as the Thames
Rowing Club has cabled that it will not leave its
own backyard this year. After all, it is just as well.
This international business can be easily overdone.
What is wanted is some scheme by which 20,000'
people will be induced to go rowing on Saturdays and
Sundays. Events like the visit of an English crew
encourage 16 men to row and 20,000 to pay to see
them do it. Which is no good to Australia.
The threatened upheaval of the N.S.W.
League of Wheelmen eventuated last week, when 81
members carried a vote of censure and sack and threw
the lords of the game into the gutter. Anew con-
trolling staff immediately took charge. Chief among
the new arrivals was " Larkin, M.L.A., of Rugby
League fame, as president. Larkin has tons of
energy, and if .he makes, only half the success of
cycling he did of the youngest brand of football the
wheeling game will boom once more. In fact, it will
roar.
■* * * *
The great Dunlop Relay Test finished on
Saturday last; and- on the very last day Northern
Suburbs (Sydney) team went out and landed the
bacon in four hours, six minutes, bo seconds. The
Ixion Club of Melbourne was second with six min-
utes 15 seconds more, and the Maoriland Marshland
Club got the third place with four hours, 13 minutes
28sec. The prizes were £IOO, £SO and £2O. The
individual honors were carried off by Maoriland crack
P. O’Shea, who rode 10 miles (unpaced) in 20min.
42 2-ssec. He got £lO for the fastest relay rider,
and the Coburg (Melb.) team won two progressive
prizes of £lO each during the contest. The previous
world’s best for the 100 miles under similar circs,
was four hours 29min. 30 seconds, put up by the
Christchurch (M.L.) Cycling Club away back in 1908.
* * * *
Half Australia was waiting eagerly on
Tuesday morning to hear how Jerry Jerome fared
with Eddie McGoorty in Brisbane on Monday night.
Thousands had declared that they firmly believed, if
Jeremiah could unwind his usual blizzard, the great
American would be overwhelmed. But, alas, for
fond hopes. Jeremiah retired in the sixth round
with a damaged wrist and, apparently, a sick feel-
ing ; and the triumphant Amurkan walked out and lifted
the large end of the purse. In Melbourne on Saturday
Morey and Daniels put up a more or less tame contest
full of hard work and genuine sloggings. But there was
nothing spectacular about it. Nobody dropped dead,
and it is not on record that anyone took to drink.
It was a good, hard-working scrap, at the end of
which the classic hand of Angelo Marre descended
on the head of Daniels. Jannese and Ryan clashed
on the Monday following, for what they called the
Bantam Weight Championship of Australia. Jannese
had Ryan completely queered in the eighth round.
At Sydney Stadium next Saturday night our Hughie
Mehegan will fight a return battle with Milburn
Saylor, and make an attempt to wipe out the memory
of that unexpected knock-out. But it will be purely
a case of speed.
* * * *
The scrap between Thorn, the Australian
ancient who came back to the ring and did well,
and the Tahmmy Burns protege, Lee Johnson, on
Saturday night, at the Sydney Stadium, was nothing
to write home about. Thorn got all over the colored
boy after the sixth round, but had no more chance of
knocking him out than he had of being whirled
to Heaven in a fiery chariot. The Brown Boy ceased
to hit after the sixth and devoted himself entirely
to defensive tactics with a view to seeing the scrap
out. All of Which was very natural if not spectac-
ular, for Brown Boy had broken his thumb, and felt
altogether too sick to smite his brother bruiser.
Viewed from that standpoint Brown Boy’s perform-
ance was highly meritorious and elegant and master-
ful. What sort of a mess he would have made of
Thorn had his thumb been right is hard to say. He
might have made no mess of him at all; but certain
it is that although Johnson was a damaged person
Thorn failed' Mo mark his coffee-colored dial. The
little white man with the hard face and enormous
head was warned for using the heel of his palm—-
and well he deserved the warning. But considering the
various and Several parts of his anatomy Saylor hits
’ with, the warning to Thorn seemed somewhat of a
joke. Still, it is always pleasing to see the referee
warning anybody who deserves it- —it makes for
confidence; and, as everybody knows, confidence is
capital—a thing this country and everybody in it,
with a few favored exceptions, requires more or less
badly. The combat or disturbance came to a natural
end in the 20th round, when Harald Baker placed
his elegant right hand on the rugged head of Thorn,
and made a wonderful picture of Beauty and the
Other Fellow.

Weary John Murray, Chief Secretary, has
issued an order that promotion in the Vic.
Police Force shall for the future proceed by
the rule of age only. Hitherto a policeman
credited with special smartness and efficiency
stood a chance of being lifted over the fat
heads of senior officers. A case in point
occurred a while ago. A sergeant was moved
into the first class nine months ahead, of his
time, and thus became due for a sub-inspec-
tor’s billet previous to four or five other ser-
geants who had been a longer time in the
force. This was strictly according to regu-
lation 1748 of the interminable Police Code,
but it aggrieved those other sergeants whose
advancement had been more in accordance
with the Calendar. Therefore they ap-
proached Weary Murray, the great advocate
of the Go Slow doctrine, and breathed in his
friendly ear, with the result that he over-
ruled reg. 1748 of the Code and decided that
Superiority in police service must stand aside
until Seniority has crawled ahead of it.
There is no short cut to sub-inspectoring.
The sergeants march along the same route in
single file, and, excepting such as may be
pushed off the track by the Chief Commis-
sioner for being very drunk or what not, all
the survivors get there. in order of time.
Sometimes Death steps in and makes a gap
which the men fill up, and Death, being a
cynical joker, usually makes his gap in a
wrong place. The stupidest policemen never
die. They just live on until promotion
crowns their efforts.

Presenting two ways to get up early
Big Ben will get yon up on the in-
stalment plan, a little at a time, by
ringing every other half minute for
ten minutes, so you’ll wake up grad-
ually. Or he’ll do the whole job all
at once, with one long, straight, five-
minute ring.
You can set him to do it as you
choose, and shut him off short in
the middle of his call either way.
That makes him two good clocks
in one, to suit everybody’s taste in
early rising.
He plays no pranks. He won’t go
off before it’s time and rob you or
your full measure of sleep. He won’t
go off behind time and rob you of
your.work,time. It’s Big Ben’s busi-
ness to run on time, to ring on time,
and to stay on time.
Big Ben attends to his own busi-
ness and helps you attend to yours
by getting you and the farm hands
out early.
Then he sticks around the house
and keeps time all day for the women
folks so they can have your meals
on time.
There never was a clock that fitted
in better with the farm work.
He’s triple-nickel plated, and so
handsome you’ll want to keep hipi
in the parlor instead of a bedroom.
Stands seven inches tall from the top of
his head to the tips of hi.s toes; has big easy-
winding keys, large hands, and big figures
that you can read at a distance on dark morn-
ings, and is built of good implement steel so
he'! l last for years, He’s doing this kind of
work in millions of homes to-day.
Big Ben is made in La Salle, Illinois,
U.S.A., by Westclox. His price is 1216,
postage 1/- extra. If you cannot find him in
your town, send a money order to his makers’
Australian office, Westclox, 63 Pitt Street,
Sydney. They will have one mailed to
you at once by the nearest jeweller.

“Hamer”; The half-dollar dies hard. When the
Commonwealth took over the silver coinage from
Britain in 1910 the half-crown Was sentenced to
death. The Mint has not issued since 1910 any of
coins which, when new, so resemble a full moon
in a clear sky, and ■/.yet they seem as numerous and
popular as ever—of as scarce and popular as ever, aa
the ,case may b?. Long may this state of things con-
tinue. It- is just as easy to borrow 2s. 6d. as 25.,
and a long way—-in fact, . two whole beers —more
satisfactory.

It is Whispered that synthetic butter is now being
produced as a by-product of palm oil, and that dairy
establishments 6f the future will be the soap factories.
The buttet prflauded fi-fiiri cdc’oa.ntits is uniform, and

said to be insatiable and to be glowing daily. , A
few, years ago bananas were' a luxury only the very
rich could afford. 'Then Joe Chamberlain, who was
Secretary, of State for the Colonies, cast his eagle eye
dh the West. Incjies, which at the time were in a bad
way. He. pelsuaded Elder, Dempster and Co. to
establish a dine. Of StCattiefs ;tO bring in large and
regular supplies of fruit. After sOme initial diffi-
culties were overcome the trade began tO boom, and
since then £7,250,000 have been • expended, on the
building of 57 steamers specially designed for the
banana trade. A usual cargo is 71,000 bunches, and
during the month of February 840,000 bunches of
West Indian bananas were landed in Eurone. And
the demand groWs every day. The West Indies seem
to have reached about the limit of tbeir supply, and
fresh sources are being tapped in Uganda, where the
famous Semliki Valley grows things so fast that the
fruit can be detected swelling visibly. But still the
howl is* “Give uS more!”
“Gazer”: Our method of handling bread is specially
designed to . spread disease. Cooking kills the germs
that come “to'®"With"dtif daily meat and milk. Fruit
and salads can be cleansed with a dip in formalin
solution. But bread can be neither washed nor, for
general use, cooked, and its soft, absorbent surface is
an ideal harbor for microbes. :..j In the journey from
bakehouse ,to table we give it every chance to do its
worst. The bread-carters continually handle the
horses’ reins, and in between times those reins lie
on the ground or are chucked, down in. the corner of
the stable. The hands ."of the. carter ’ often have sores
on them, by which the most terrible diseases may be
transmitted to customers. Also, the baskets in which
the bread enters the house lie at the side of the
drivers’ boots, or are trailed a few inches from the
roadway under the cart. Usually several loaves are
in the basket when the housekeeper makes a choice,
and she, as a rule, paws over the lot before selecting
one. . She doesn’t always wash * her hands first. In
fact, the less her habit of washing the,greater her
habit of pawing. Finally the loaf comes to us on the
pub or cafo table, and every customer grabs it and
encircles it with his fist w’hile he saws a chunk off.
Some provide, rails instead of loaves, but they are not
many. It is explained that there is such a waste
when rolls are used. Here is a job to the hand of
our, health officers!
* * * *
The Customs Department has become diligent in
respect of certain patent medicines calling themselves
fruit preparations and displaying a picture of luscious
vegetables on the wrapper. The Customs argue that
the unsuspicious public would believe that the mix-
ture, exhibiting, say,'a watermelon outside was made
from watermelons, while, as a matter of fact, it may
contain wafer enough but no melon. So, henceforth,
any life-drops, cure-alls, pidk ? me-ups, etc., in bottles
having’fruits or' flowers emblazoned thereon must
really contain a decoction of the emblem. But, so as
not to unduly hurt the feelings of the artists who
draw the pictures and the philanthropists who dish
up the mixtures, the threat to prohibit incorrectly-
described philtres will not take effect until Jan. 1,
1915. Meanwhile the purchasing public must cure
itself by its faith in the picture on the cover.
“Hamer”: The sorrowing relative at three hob a
moan has no monopoly of the freak verse business.
A firm of house agents was guilty of this in the S.
M. Herald last Saturday:— '
“Ah, well for us all some sweet home lies
Deeply buried from human eyes.”
The allegedly buried home was in Ashfield, and the
firm wanted £3500 for it.
“John P.”: The native race of Maoriland
is having its collective leg pulled in great
style. The present “king” is a young fellow
known as Te Rata, and he possesses a more
or less adviser in Tupu Taingakawa. Tupu
having heard rumors of the general gladness
of London and of the gayness and glitter
thereof, decided to get himself and “King”
Te Rata sent along to interview George Five
on behalf of the Maori race. But a great
collection of Maoris, assembled at Waihi at
Tupu’s invitation, resolved, by a large majo-
rity, that the Time Was Not Ripe.
That didn’t sidetrack Tupu and “The
King.” They booked passages by the Niagara
from Auckland, and departed in a whirl of
“kia ora.” The mission to the Cold Country
is to obtain restoration of the lands confis-
cated as the result of the Maori wars, and
to obtain fishing and forest-rights, in accord-
ance with the spirit of the Treaty of Wait-
angi. And if nothing is doing in that direc-
tion there are plenty of pubs and racecourses
and billiard tables to fall back on.

No white teeth gleamed your lips between;
When on your face I’d dote,
I’d look straight down your throat.
When in those dear, dead days you smiled,
Youfi grin Was ragged, jagged, wild;
Hut nOW white ttibblets shew
Like tombstones in a row.
They fascinate me whdn they slip,-
And hang detached from either lip,-
, Then, with a clicking ctasli,-
Back to their stations dash.
You are not now so dear to me
With all that white machinery;
You fret me greatly, coz,
When o’er your plate you buzz.
You kissed With warm, soft mouth last week ;
Now you press pfircSlairt ’grfirist my cheek 1
God’s mercy ! On the heath’;
You rub me on your teeth!
Ah, draw your fangs to show you’re true,

That guillotine, my dear,
Snips off your words too clear.
Return them now, my snapping maid,
Whatever you have on them paid !
Some stranger from the south
Will cram them in her mouth
And tell her husband on the land,
“A bargain! Got ’em second’and!”

Talking of Ellen Terry—her famous jubilee testi-
monial performance at Drury Lane, London, was
undoubtedly the biggest compliment ever paid to an
actress. “Everybody” of note figured on the general
committee, from leading literary, artistic and scien-
tific lights down to earls and dukes by the dozen and
half-dozen. The gathering of stars included Melba,
Duse, Caruso, W. S. Gilbert, Genee, the Bancrofts, the
two Coquelins, Mrs. Pat Campbell and all the. actors
worth mentioning.' The souvenir programme was
enriched with special paintings and drawings by
Alma Tadema, Edwin Abbey, John Sargent, Byam
Shaw, Bernard'Partridge, Walter Crane and others.
Something; niore .than distinction as an actress was
needed to account for Ellen Terry’s popularity with
people who are not addicted to the habit of throwing
bouquets. She is a great artist, and a remarkably
winsome personality. .
“Moana”: According to “C. Ockney” (B. 2G/3/’l4)
Gilbert once said to Sullivan, “What’s the matter
with Barrington? lie's singing in tune!” It is a fine
imaginative effort on the part of “Ockney,” or_ who-
ever was responsible for the yarn. Gilbert notoriously
knew absolutely nothing about music. To him the
thing that is alleged to soothe the frenzied zoo.-in-
habitant was jtist plain noise. The only tune he
could pick out when he heard it was “Gor Save,” and
he couldn’t be absolutely sure of that unless the audi-
ence rose up and began to struggle with its coat.
A lot of people in Australia must have heard, or
heard of, Georgina Burns, longtime a prima donna
of the old Carl Rosa Opera Co., which cleared the
way in Britain for the bigger touring enterprises of
Beecham, Quinlan and others. Georgina Burns is
now a penniless old. woman; and G. R. Sims at
latest date was collecting votes for her admission to
a home for incurables.
“Bloggs”: “Come Over Here” is booming in Mel-
bourne, whereas it was a disappointment in Sydney.
Inter-State jealousy is the usual explanation of the
fact that . a show which goes well in one capital
slumps in another. An idiotic theory, to
my mind. I sat through the first night of “Come
Over Here” in Sydney. It was awful. The unfor-
tunate Dix had been given about a fortnight to write
the “book,” and in addition he had had to produce
the show. The comedians had not had time to
construct “business” of their own. They were as
dull as clerics. Never have I seen a deader house.
The press, with the exception of one misguided
paper, heartily. damned the production, either .with
faint praise or outspoken abuse. Six weeks later, I
went again. It was a different show. That excel-
lent humorist, Jack Cannot, had written an entirely
new part for himself, in a manner of speaking. I
don’t know if the management' forced him, despite
his cries of protest to accept 14 per cent, of the
gross takings in recognition of his feat; but anyhow
it was worth that, or more, to them. The nigger
humorist was infinitely less depressing. A number of
weak items had been cut out. Things went with a
gratifying rush, where aforetime they ■ had dragged
hopelessly. But b!y this time "the damage had been
donei The revue had earned a name for pretentious
dulness. It arrived in Melbourne after having en-
joyed the advantage of three months’ rehearsal in
Sydney. Of course it boomed. Any show which
is tried for months on one dog is likely to be well
received if it is submitted later, in its perfected
form, to a very similar variety of dog.
“Stargazer”: The profession is passing this yarn
about with small compunction. Two people saw a
quaint strartger in the street. “That’s the cele-
brated William Howler,” said one. “It does look
like him,” said the other. They moved up for a
closer inspection, but the alleged identity passed on.
“I could swear ,it was William Howler,” repeated
Observer No. I with growing concern. “Let’s follow
him, and see what impression he makes on the
crowd,” said No. 2. They followed. A few others
showed interest, and took up the tail. “It’s he all
right,” said No. 1. Just then the gentleman dis-
cussed gave a blind man a halfpenny. Then the
followers turned away. “Mistaken identity!” said
No. 1. “But it’s the dead ring of the great William
Howler in all other respects,” said No. 2.
“Jay”: I give “Sealed Orders” three weeks, at
most, at Sydney Theatre Royal. It isn’t a bad bellow-
drama. But its success, in London, was due as much
to the fact that it was a sort of topical revue when
it was first staged as to its merits as a play. The
Flower Show, the fashionable gambling resort, Chris-
tie’s, etc., are all familiar features of every London
season; dirigibles were in vogue when “Sealed
Orders” was written ; a war-scare was on, and everyone
was talking about Dreadnoughts and clamoring to be
shown a Dreadnought, if only on the stage. The
hoodwinking of, an entire ship’s company by a bogus
potentate, who ca-me aboard in state and received
the honors due to a ruling monarch, was fresh in the
popular mind. Bond-street Was full Of CagliostrOs.
And so on. None of these things has much interest
for Australians. And the play ish’t nearly good
enough to live on its own. It might have dime big
business had it been re-written skilfully on Aus-
tralian lines. The foreign enemy could ■ have been
an Asiatic Rower. The battleship could have been
the Australia. And set-pieces of local interest could
have been substituted for representations of places
with Which this country has no acquaintance. A big
scoop will be scoied by some enterprising manage-
ment, on these lines, some day.
“Alva”: Ruth Fournal, played by Dorothy Whit-
aker in “Sealed Orders’.’ at Sydney Royal, has a rare
distinction among heroines of melodrama. She is not
pursued by a villain who wants either to marry or
to ruin her. On the contrary, her father desires to
make her rich and happy, and the only lover in sight
is the one who almost proposes to her the first time
he sees her. It is something to find, a melodrama
ingenue who is not flying all the time from violent
assault or poverty, as most of them have to do.
“Ami”: Although Titheradge seems to be our chief
daffodil expert, he came first to Australia with no
knowledge of flowers or taste that way. Fishing was
his hobby, and he practised it at Riddell's Creek,
towards Macedon (Vic.). There he fell in with the
local nurseyman, an intense daffodilian. Titheradge
was impressed by his enthusiasm, and said “You
ought to boom your fancy.” “Ah,” said the fancier,
“if I had your position, as the most favorite actor, 1
could do it.” With much persuasion he induced
Titheradge to accept a selection of the finest bulbs,
and undertake to plant them. The nurseryman had
some influence with the Melbourne Ladies’ Club,
known as the Austral Salon. He induced the mem-
bers to apply to Titheradge for a lecture on daffodils.
Easy-going Titheradge complied, and fagged it up
from botany manuals. After meeting with rapturous
applause he became really obsessed with the flowers.
When he left Australia for the second' time he sold
his collection of bulbs for- £5OO.
The British rights of “Antony and Cleopatra,” the
film which is said to be the last word in moving
pictures, have been sold by auction for £B5OO to
Messrs. Ruffles, of London. The sale drew all the
chief figures in the cinematograph world to the
offices of the Cines Company, where the auction
was held. The bidding started at £4OOO, and rose
by five hundreds to £B5OO. It was explained by
the auctioneer that the rights were for Great Britain
and Ireland, the Channel Islands and the Isle of
Man, and would run for three years. The Aus-
tralian rights have been secured by Australasian
Films ’ Ltd. at a big figure, and arrangements have
been made with Spencers Ltd. to control the Sydney
screening at the Lyceum. ‘
“Private Boxer”: There is a lot in heredity.
Viscount Dangan, son and heir of the much-divorced
Earl Cowley, has just married an American girl, May
Picard by name, who joined the musical comqdy "show
in which he was supering, to fill up, as it were. This
despite the fact that he had previously plighted his
troth to the lovely Pearl Aufrere, of the London
Gaiety. It may well be that Pearl will go for Dangan
for “breach,” as Phyllis Broughton, a Gaiety dancer
of years gone by, went’ for his dad when he was
Viscount Dangan. Phyllis got £lO,OOO, but, con-
sidering the high cost of living nowadays, Miss
Aufrere’s dividend should'be bigger.
