IN BANANA LAND
Died at Windorah (Q.) a few days ago
Michael Ilackett, aged 97. He had battled
with the climate of the Northern State since
he arrived there from Galway (Ireland) 90
years ago.
Delicately mentioned in Brisbane dailies
that “six chemists” were prosecuted and
fined for selling as refined paraffin a crude
product that was a fraud and a sham.
Incidentally, it transpired that the stuff
was bought at Is. 9d. a gallon and sold at
Its. 6d. a pint —a profit of something like
1000 per cent. When Billy Milk-oh drops
10 per cent, of water in the can Brisbane
journals print his name and address in full,
but when Queen-street druggists perpetrate
their little job they are considerately alluded
to as “six chemists.” Evidently the drug
business is an anonymous concern.
“Rangan” writes: When once a curse gets
on a railway or a North Shore bridge it is a
hard thing to lift. The work goes like the
Devil on his way to mass. The deviation
from Drayton to Toowoomba was promised
by politicians 47 years ago. After a short
respite of 20 years (during which the pro-
mise had been repeated perhaps 2000 times,
and caught 10 times as many votes) a sur-
vey was taken. After another short interval,
this time of 10 years, Parliament authorised
the proposed line. Then after an interval of
six years some of the costs were placed on
the Estimates. Finally, when another 11
years had flashed past, bringjng the time up
to a week or so ago, the Minister for Rail-
ways rushed to the front, and turned the
first sod. Given another 15 years or so to
import the rails, and five more to get the
sleepers, then—why, then we shan’t be long,
provided we get the money.
VICARIOUS POLITENESS.
“By crikey , Cohen! That’s a nice bit o’ goods you raised your hat to. Who is she?”
“I don't know her name. But she’s a friend of my brother’s , and I borrowed his hat
this morning.”
“Rangan”.: Bananaland is feeling the need
of decentralisation even in its sport. In the
selection of two candidates to represent
Queensland in the Bisley team, the Centre and
North were ignored and the tests confined al-
most solely to Brisbane riflemen. The rest of
the State, especially the North, reckons that
something will have to be done. As a start
. the.N.Q. Rifle Association has shown its faith
in its crack shot, Grosskreutz, by financing
him to Bisley as an independent competitor.
Half the elderly Tories in Brisbane are
looking expectantly forward to Denham’s
next batch of nominations to the Leg. Coun-
cil. It is safe to predict, on past experience,
that the Cabinet will appoint no one who
appears likely to survive his appointment by
12 months. Singular to notice that the
farmers, who are the backbone of Q. Liberal-
ism, haven’t a single representative in the
House sacred to bucolic stolidity.
Apparently the importing disease is in-
curable in Australia. As an instance, the
Australian branches of the British Medical
Association propose to publish a medical
journal, and their first inspiration is to im-
port an editor from England. If they got a
man from Thursday Island or Cairns they
might do better. Plain British diseases like
scarlet fever and phthisis and rheumatism
are known to all practitioners, but up in
Northern Australia, where the tail of Asia
slops over into this country, there are im-
ported complaints that no ordinary British
medico would know from a bar of soap.
A Texas (U.S.) cotton-ginner, in a big
way, E. E. Wood, was in Brisbane lately
displaying some magnificent cotton that he
himself picked at Capella, Central Queens-
land. Wood asserts that Central and West-
ern Q. are ideal places for cotton-growing
and that scores of American planters would
come across if they knew the country’s capa-
bilities. He scouts the old yarn that cotton
needs cheap colored labor to make it a suc-
cess. “The community where I live,” he
says, “is exclusively white and all the’work
is done by whites, a man making at cotton-
picking from Bs. to 16s. a day.” Yet 50
years ago the Southern States of U.S. were
supposed to be climes where the white would
degenerate and the black alone could perform
manual labor. How the white Texan or
Georgian would laugh at the “degenerate”
twaddle of our Australian alien-coddlers.
The Motor Boats of Brisbane.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The motor-boats of Brisbane have shown
their shining heels
To all the graceful sailing craft that lift their
lazy keels.
lhe wind that rules the sailing boats, she is
a fickle Miss
She sweeps them from her with a squall and
swings them to a kiss.
rhe king of Naphtha is a wight who caves
for no wind’s guile,
She cannot fright him with a frown, nor
move him with a smile;
But right across the silver ridge, the ripple-
chain he rives
And takes no heed of drift or flow, but
straight ahead he drives.
The sailing boats of Brisbane they flirt a
snowy wing,
In amber seas, by amber shores, as mighty
birds they swing,
Or where the rocky eyots hide their caves
with walls of palm
As nesting birds they dream and doze
against an opal calm !
The motor-boats of Brisbane they skate
along the tide,
By Norman Park, by Hamilton, into the
bay they ride,
Through every reach the engines throb, and
cross the sleeping bars,
To cut, like diamond on glass, the reflexes
of the stars!
One night I saw the twin white moon the
mirroring water holds
Sink hidden in the floating weeds among the
river’s folds,
Across her couch the bold god smote, and
seeing she was fair.
He flung the blossdmsTrom her bed and left
her beauty bare.
But when a sailing boat crept by, it spread
its sail to hide
In chivalry her modest breast beneath the
whispering tide.
The motor-boats of Brisbane are products of
to-day;
They fight the power of wind and wave and
rule, the water way.
The sailing boats of Brisbane, courtly and
proud and kind
Are Old-World cavaliers who shake their
ruffles in the wind!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Bbisbane, May 2, 1914.
My Deab Thabgomin dah, —-
The most noticeable event in a somewhat
dull week was the bal masque, which
masqued at the South Brisbane Technical
College on Monday night. The decorations
were mostly greenery, with scarlet poppies
(made of paper) and Japanese lanterns. The
Macgregor was not well enough to be pres-
ent, but Lady Macgregor, upholstered in
apricot brocade, and the Talbot aide turned
the lustre of their countenances upon the
company for an hour or two. Julius Napo-
leon Knight and Irene Brown added the
radiance of their picturesqueness somewhere
near midnight, and judged the fancy cos-
tumes. Evelyn Hutton and Eric Doyle
were first past the post, as it were, and no
protest being entered the dividend was duly
paid out. The lady represented an Arab, or,
should I say, an Arabess? And the gentleman
also hailed from a neighboring desert for the
occasion. Incidentally, there has been a lot
of heartburning over the judges’ decision.
I here generally is.
The committee was mostly made up of
pretty girls, backed by Mrs. Doyle (wife
of the medical man), Mrs. Duff, Mayoress of
Toowong, and others. All things considered
and duly reflected upon in a spirit of Chris-
tian piety, it is rather difficult to understand
why the ball wasn’t more of a success. Only
about 100 dancers gyrated on the and
the gallery was not full. The proceeds—if
any—go to the General Hospital, an institu-
tion which seems to be a combination of the
poor who are always with us and the Old
Man of the Sea.
One of Brisbane’s popular girls, Vesta
a daughter of the late Colonel Drury,
C.M.G., sailed for London on Wednesday
Her husband-to-be, “Ted” Marks, recently
of . the Queensland Geological Survey, is
waiting for her over there with two eyes
glued on the horizon, and soon after she ar-
rives the hitching process will take place.
JLhe John Hunter Browns, well-known
Queenslanders now in London, will comfort
the bride under their hospitable roof until
the appointed day. On Monday afternoon
Mrs. Harold Saltmarshe and Mrs. Dick
Clarke, two sisters of the bride-to-be-if-all-
goes-well, fed a large covey of her friends at
tbe Cafe Eschenhagen. It will be some time
before either member of the proposed part-
nership returns, for after the wedding Mr.
Marks goes to Dublin to study medicine.
His father, C._ P. Marks, M.L.C., combines
the art of healing with the duties of legisla-
ting. Dr. Alec Marks is a brother, and Dr.
Espie Dods a half-brother. It isn’t often
that one family can boast four medical men.
Architect Robin Dods, now settled in your
village, also belongs.
Evans Watt, son of Mr. and Mrs. H.
Watt, of Woolwich, Sydney, placed the
small gold circlet of matrimony on the finger
of Elsa Sachs, a pretty Brisbane girl, last
Tuesday. The bride was absorbed in crepe
de chine, with the usual etceteras of Limer-
ick lace. Ivy Cory bridesmaided in palest
pink, and, Walter Sachs gave his sister
away in the post friendly manner. The
moon is radiating the Blue Mountains.
At the Women’s Club the other night such
weighty matters as “The Problem of Domes-
tic Labor,” and “The Position of the Stay-
at-Home Girl” were discussed. When the
brain strain became too intense, musical
items were trickled soothingly into the pro-
ceedings. If there’s nothing better, and the
tobacconist and the little chapel round the
corner are closed, music is fairly soothing.
A very savage brbast (not beast, please)
told me this.
Government Entomologist Tryon. who,
with Dr. Harvey Johnston, has been floating
round the world trying to collect evidence
against the prickly pear, with intent to
destroy same, came back to Brisbane on
Tuesday night. Java, India, S. Africa and
London (is there any prickly pear in Lon-
don?), Algiers and Egypt were ransacked.
The U.S.A. also were given a turn, and an
attempt made to get into Mexicp, but the
party was turned back by the sound of un-
holy revelry there. Now experiments are
being made at Dulacca, but the pear doesn’t
seem a bit depressed.
Major Watson, head teacher of the West
End State School, died on Monday. He was
quite well on Saturday. Major Watson (who
was originally a Yorkshireman) has been in
the State service since ’63.
The East Brisbane Croquet Club was re-
opened last Saturday, a goodly sprinkling of
croquet-elect happening along. Mrs. Hether-
ington, this year’s president, handed out the
prizes to the winners of the various events
The Ascot Croquet Club also rolled up its
sleeves last Saturday, and held its annual
meeting, when the popular Mrs. Bulcock
was elected president, or full-back or pace-
maker or something. Mrs. Frank Hall, who
whirls a masterly mallet,, is captain or chief
whirler.
The Catholic Club assembled to a man on
Sunday night to farewell J. F. Donovan,
who is departing for the City of Fog, the
Emerald Isle, and other tourist resorts. Be-
sides a load of good wishes, the departing
one collected a gold watch presented by the
various sporting bodies with which he has
been associated. Father Byrne handed over
the goods with his customary elegance.
The two Holmes sisters, who leave for
Sydney next week, were farewelled at the
Ladies’ Rowing Club shed on Saturday last.
Their luggage will include the usual framed
photograph—this time of the boathouse and
champion fours.
Mrs. Amery and her daughter, of your
obscure and dilapidated yet unduly inflated
city, are visiting Brisbane, and are staying
at Auchenflower with Mrs. Rupert Knight,
a sister of Mrs. Amery. A few days ago tea
and scones were eaten in honor of the
visitors at the Women’s Club.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“Kai” : No wonder the loan money evap-
orates, when Government officials make such
fearsome blunders as that of Mackay harbor.
The engineers reckoned that concrete piles
would find bottom at 30ft. The first one
sent down refused point blank to find bottom
-at least it hadn’t found it when it quietly
sank out of sight. Engineer Boult gasped
like a stranded fish and ran to tell the Har-
bor Board (that august body of saddlers,
commission agents, etc.) that the pile was
down but the game was up. He then recom-
mended the use of screw piles or something,
but he did not say how they were to be paid
for. The Board got panic stricken on the
spot over its wasted £20,000, which was the
approximate amount already spent. The
brilliant asses might as well have flung it
into, the ditch that does duty as a harbor.
Chief Engineer Cullen still thinks that Flat
Top can be connected with the mainland;
but then, he has not to find the cash. The
farmers in the Mackay district, who have,
are not inclined to agree with him. In any
case the scheme was rotten from the first, ir
the light of the fact that Newry Bay lies
only 20 miles north of Mackay. A deep,
roomy port, which could shelter the Channel
squadron, it is an ideal harbor. But Queens-
landers are like Cornstalks. They don’t
like ideal harbors. Townsville and Port
Alma are living proofs of this good old Aus-
tralian characteristic.

LACTOBACILLIN E.
The bacillary treatment of bacterial and fermenta-
tive affectations of the alimentary canal.
Lactobaeilline pits the vigorous lactic ferment
against the less resistant organisms of decomposition,
with the result that the latter are immediately
destroyed.
Lactobaeilline is prepared under the scientific
supervision and control of
PROFESSOR METCHNIKOFF.
It is prepared in tablets to be eaten with meals,
and by checking intestinal putrefaction exerts a re-
markable influence towards the attainment of perfect
health. It is of special value in all affections of the
digestive tract, auto-intoxication, colites, intestinal
catarrh, chronic dyspepsia, etc.
Every cylinder of tablets bears the words
“Lactobaeilline preparee par la Societe le Fer-
ment Seul Fournisseur du Professor Metchnikoff.”
Without which, none are genuine.
Lactobaeilline is sold in boxes containing eight
cylinders of Tablets—price, ss. 6d.—and pan' be
ordered through any chemist, or obtained direct from
the Depot Societe le Ferment, Australasia Chambers,
406-14 Queen Street, Brisbane ; or branches : Vickery’s

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
A’o notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MSS. will be returned
'• unless stamps accompany.
Mel: Please don’t write about the United
States as “America.” It is no more America than
Brazil is. And don’t write about India, which lies
north-west of Australia, as “the East.” And don’t
talk of far-off north-western places like Greece and
Turkey as “the Near East.” And don’t describe
England as “Home,” for this is our home. Also, try
to get out of the habit of describing France, Ger-
many, etc., as “the Continent.” They may be the
European continent, but this little bit of ground is
our continent. .. .H. Bale: About the most tiresome
obituary item is that to the effect that “the deceased
leaves a widow behind him.” Did any deceased ever
shovp his own widow in front of him?....One Who
Knows: You don’t make the case against him clear.
It reads like a personal grievance. No room for per-
sonal grievances in these days of national calamities.
A Female of the Speeies: Please leave B. J.
Hansen and his Vancouver offices alone. He burst
into tears, in a literary manner of speaking, over
your last assault on his premises. If you could only
be made to understand the unpleasantness of having
a Vancouverite sobbing on one’s shoulder by post you
would appreciate our feelings in this matter... .Will
Jarratt: “March” fills us with distrust. The earth
isn’t “motherwise, impatient of a birth” in Australia
in March, though it may be said to be in that inter-
esting condition in England. Now, if you had said
September-—but then the lines wouldn’t have scanned.
....F. It. Golinsky: The Churches get nothing from
the Government in Australia. .. .A.H.: Your master
rhymer’s certificate is hereby cancelled.... Henry K.:
We can’t publish same—in fact, we would scorn
to publish same. .. .Distinguished Author of “Fly-
ing”,: Try drowning. .. .M.K.T.: If we published
poems like yours in this “valuable paper,” it would
no longer, be so. .. .G.W.T.:—
You (singing) : When oftimes at eve I am turning
away.
All weary at heart from the cares of the day—
TJs: You’d better go now! You’ll be hurt if you
stay!
. ...Jas. M. B. (Q.) : For Heaven’s sake, don’t para-
phrase the daily paper in bad verse ! If you haven’t
any original remarks about you, stand aside and
allow the procession to pass along A.R.M.: A
little of “Hope”—not much, but enough to go on
with:—
“There comes a time,” hear Brutus call,
“Should man strike with the tide”
Strike while the water’s hot, so to speak
G.T.M.R. (S.A.): Not drivel, but still not acceptable.
...4 Jonathan N.: No cow, thanks. .. .Rupert A.L.:
Not- worth while. .. .Breaker’s Mate. No use to us.
If it is of any use to any other paper, we will be
vastly astonished. .. .A.E.F.: Too fragmentary, and
there is no originality about it....R.M.V. (Perth):
We didn’t ask you what was music. We didn’t even
ask you what was tripe. . . .A.E.C.: A weary item. .
Jean L.: We would rather give you money than
space, but we will give you neither O.K.S.: No!
Some pens are mightier than the sword, but your
sort of pen isn’t the equal of a paper-knife....
Walter M.: The midnight horror isn’t a subject for
light verse. It should be pursued with disinfectant
and fire and the Board of Health K.: Well, the
only thing wrong with it is that it is stodgy. And
stodginess blots out a multitude of virtues. . . .
Stale M.: “Fuzz” won’t go E.W.C.: Unsuitable.
....Nero: Fiddle away—your manuscript is burn-
ing! .Harold. There are some people we would
rather die than meet. There are some manuscripts we
would rather perish than read to the bitter end.
Were you born as dull as you are, or has it gradually
got you down like ingrowing old age?. . . .F.G.T.Y.-:
There isn’t much wrong with it. But it is one of
those things that don’t require to have very much
wrong with them.

W.E.G. (M.L.) : (1) They are of no use to us. (2)
We will give you nothing for them—not even male-
dictions. (3) They are not suitable for reproduc-
tion. They are only suitable for burning. .. .Howa:
A very small, flat and wooden sketch of somebody or
other who is practically unknown. It may be' like
him, but we hope not; if it is, his nose is badly

G. Cook: A faint, scratchy nightmare. . . .Dick F. T.:

“H. Singemoine”:—
Cabled foolishness in connection with the twopenny
row in the smaller half of Ulster:—
“The following true story is going the rounds. A
noted Field-Marshal sent for a certain General at
theiWar Office, and demanded that he should draw
up a plan of campaign against Ulster, whereupon the
General replied, ‘l’m damned if I do!’ The Field-
Marshal, without a word, turned on his heel, and,
going to Col. J. E. B. Seely, then Secretary of State
for War, told the latter that ‘the game was up.’ ”
There are two sorts of papers which specialise in
futilities like the above. In The Wails of Derry
Chronicle the Noted Person is mostly a sturdy Prodis-
tant. He is addressed by a Certain Priest. Con-
troversially speaking, the anonymous cleric is imme-
diately,-trailed, in. the mud —the which goes to show
that the R.C. religion is only fit for slaves and imbe-
ciles, and should be exterminated.
When London Society trots out the blue-mouldy
old device the Noted Ope is usually described as “a
R-y-lty.” j The latter tells a Certain Duke that if a
Certain (unnhmed) Politician (of the Party to which
the paper, is hostile) doesn’t presently abandon his
disloyalm'ntics he (the R-y-lty) will take “certain
steps.” No one can make more than a vague guess
as to the identity of any of the persons indicated in
such yarns, but it impresses the remoter suburbs, and
that is the whole ambition of sheets like London
SocibW. /
The anecdote on the above lines which some ass
at. the- bther end of the cable has thought fit to cable
to Australia bears its refutation on the face of it.
The type of man who rises to be a General has at
least some rudimentary ideas of discipline. It is his
way to treat his superiors as he expects his own in-
feriors to treat him. It may be that once in a life-
time he is driven, on principle, to disobey an order
and to do it flatly. But in such an event he would not
take pride in his mutiny. He would not comport
himself towards the inoffensive official who was but
obeying his superiors when he gave the order, after
the fashion of a rowdy inebriate who had been asked
either to leave the bar or be thrown out. There
are ways and ways of refusing to obey a military
order ; and when the person who gives it is a Field-
Marshal- and the one .who receives it is a General,
there is nothing surer (outside the crudest sort of
bellowdrama) than that the ordinary courtesies of
life will be observed on both sides.
But the cream of the incredibility lies in the
alleged Field-Marshal’s attitude. This man—one
of the princes of his profession, a soldier who has
fought his way to the top presumably by the exercise
of courage, resource and resolution—meets with one
lonely rebuff. It is enough. He eaves in utterly.
He does not reason with his insolent subordinate.
He does not consider the question of dealing ruthlessly
with him by way of discouraging the others. It
does not occur to him to send for another General.
The only course of action that he can conceive is to
hurry distractedly to the War Secretary, and announce
—-doubtless after a burst of sobs —that “the game is
up.” If Britain really possessed Field-Marshals of
that miserable description the national game would
indeed be up. However it doesn’t. No civilised
white nation is hampered with such freaks; except
in the lower walks of the drama.

The Inky Way.
“Chink”: I note that as a passing spasm in the
March instalment of his “Confessions” in The Lone
Hand, “Hop” remarks, and gently deplores, the lack
of reciprocity among Australian papers generally.
It wasn’t always so. Back in the ’so's, ’6o’s and
’7o’s there was neither a plenitude of stereo news
or weighty auctioneer advts., and the early Vic.
country sheets exchanged in most harmonious fashion.
I call to mind particularly the Ballarat Star, Gee-
long Advertiser, Ovens and River Murray Adver-
tiser, and Maryborough Advertiser. Some issues of
these journals I’ve seen with a score of pithy pars
and short articles lifted from each other and always
with copious acknowledgment. And it wasn’t be-
cause there weren’t capable men on the respective
staffs in those days. There were; witness James
Smith, on the Maryborough paper. But James was a
busy man, being as, I fancy, “Melb” remarked in this
column some time ago, up to his neck with theological
articles, extraordinary spiritualism, biology, arboreal
mining, marine astronomy, and researches into the
ancient lore of the Moabites, Jacobites, Mosquito-
bites and Dogbites. Also, he did some amateur
Pythagorism.
“Chink”: The Stawell (Vic.) News struck trouble
recently, and gave its readers a dark-blue hint that
it knew the exact nature of the trouble. Witness
this: —
“No ‘Copy.’—Owing to our reporter getting ‘lost’
last night, and failing to appear before we went to
press this morning, we regret that several items of
interest were not available for publication. The
opening of the skating season at the Skating Rink
in Wimmera-street, a meeting of the Ladies Rifle
Club, and several other matters have suffered neglect.
They say ! Henceforth the editorial department
and reportorial work will be under the supervision
of the proprietor.”
“Charivari”: The best notice of Tenniel is by
Furniss. He shows that Tenniel had not the least
invention. All his Punch cartoons were planned b.v
other men —for instance, “Dropping the Pilot,” b.v
a Beckett. This pleased both the Emperor and Bis-
marck, but we can well imagine it pleased Bismarck
most. A friend bought the original drawing and
gave it to Bismarck. However, it is feeble in the
artistry, for Tenniel was then on the wane. See him
to more advantage in “Beware.” This remarkable
prophecy appeared when the Franco-German war
broke out. Napoleon 111. and the Prince Imperial
were riding on, with ghostly legions, to destruction.
Not so much in the drawing, as in the overpowering
idea. Tenniel’s most pungent cartoon was suppressed,
and was not made public till long after its date.
This referred to Disraeli’s purchase of the Suez Canal
shares with the money of the Rothschilds, Jews all
round ! Consequently Tenniel depicted the Israelites,
in modern garb, returning over the Red Sea. The
proprietary thought it too hot, and Tenniel hastily
scraped up something else. That unsurpassable en-
graver, Swain, co-operated with Tenniel. The most
celebrated suppressed engraving, lately published, is
one by Thackeray for Vanity Fair, depicting the
surprise of Steyne and Becky by Rawdon. The
Steyne was too much of a likeness of the Marquis of
Oueensberr'-. “Old 0 ” Furniss says the famous
weekly meetings of the Punch staff only arose be-
cause Tenniel had no invention.
THE PUBLIC EDUCATOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
JOURNALIST.—Required by a leading metropoli-
tan daily, a young Gentleman, 17 to 20 years of
age, as Journalist’s Cadet. Good education essential.
Commencing salary, 30s. per week. Good prospects
for energetic, ambitious youth. Applications, stating
full particulars, to CADET, Herald Office.
—lnspiring advt.
My gentle youth, draw near, I pray,
And let me closely scan
Your classic brow —of course, you say,
You are a gentleman?
Just so!
For, understand, on common lads we place our
sternest ban!
You seek a billet on our Staff—«•
An honor, my young friend!
The Melney ‘ Morning Heragraph,
Its countenance will lend
To none
But educated gentlemen, who with its “tone” will
blend !
You understand me? Yes; quite so!
Your age is—Seventeen !
What special subjects do you know?
To what schools have you been?
I—see !
That quite explains your natty dress and gentlemanly
mien !
No history ? Dear, dear—that’s bad !
Still, do not be downcast —
We’re strong on history, my lad —
A thing we’re making, fast!
Oh, yes t
We like our men to have a thorough knowledge of
the Past!
You’re weak on grammar, are you ? Well,
That matters little here!
As long as you know how to spell,
You have no cause to fear —
You see,
A journalist who could not spell would be considered
queer.
You’ve never heard of syntax—eh?
Your composition’s poor
Oh, those are faults to which we pay
But little heed—we score
To-day
By stating publicly we need those little things no
more!
Now, general knowledge. Let me see:
I’ll question you on that !
Who first invented & s. d. ?
Quite right. Jehoshaphat!
And who
Was Galileo ? Yes; quite right—he proved the
world is flat!
And who discovered this fair land,
And wrote a chatty book?
No —no ! You don’t quite understand !
Not Tasman—Captain Cook !. .. .
But, still,
Your general knowledge is not bad, and, more, I like
your look !
Upon the whole, I think you’ll do!
At any rate we’ll try;
We’ll make a journalist of you
Ere many years go by!
Your pay,
Considering your tender years, will be distinctly
high!
The fact that you have much to learn,
I discount—so to speak ;
All journalists must start to joum.—
And if you’ve lots of cheek,
You’ll d 0... .
Your salary, to start with, will be one-pound-ten a
week!
Now, go—and set the world on Are on one-pound-ten
a week!
X. s.w.

Guardian Angels.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Churchill despises patriotic sentiment. “What
does Australia want with battleships,” he coolly in-
quires, “when Japan is her guarantee from assault ?
The First Lord of the Admiralty despises Australian
manhood if he thinks it will go into heroics over the
prospect of having a colored nation as its guardian
angel.— Melbourne Age.
The Britons had lost the habit of war, and were
helpless before these fierce foes. In their despair
they asked the Saxons to help them against the
l’iets and Scots. — English history.
BROTHERS; even those of you who are
already in the sear and yellow leaf,
and full of years and iniquity,
Sometimes, I doubt not, let your thoughts go
back to those days of antiquity
When mother tucked you into your little bed,
After your little prayers were said ;
And, having said good-night,
She most inconsiderately took away the light.
Then came, my brothers, that dread half-hour
in the day of a child;
When your mind was filhd with weird imag-
inings and fancies wild
Of Bogey-men and Hobgoblins, Ogres and
Demons; so that, for a space, you lay
Filled with a child’s vague fear of the dark,
and longing for the day.
Then, to comfort you, there came the thought
That guardian angels, as you had been
tauglitj
Hovered ever near
To watch over timid little boys and girls and
still their fear.
Is not that what mother said?
And, in vour childish mind you pictured a
feathered friend roosting benevolently
at the foot of your bed.
Then were you filled with solace deep ;
You sighed contentedly and went to sleep.
Brother;
I would speak to you of another kind of
mother;
Of our political mamma or historical mater :
Mrs. Britannia, to wit, who lives on the other
side of the equator.
You have doubtless seen her pictured upon
certain coins of the realm,
Sitting on the sharp edge of a shield, holding
a pitchfork, and wearing an absurd and
elaborate helm.
That is the lady; our dear old mum;
Mother of a large and parti-colored family
that has giveh her much trouble and
promises more in the years to come.
Hitherto she has tucked us bed,
And, for a trifling cash consideration, to
allay our dread,
Has, so to speak, left us the light
In the shape of a few more or less efficient
warships that might or might not be of
use in a fight;
But that was neither here nor there
So long as they served their purpose, and,
like the candle of childhood’s days, dissi-
pated the shadows and the attendant
thoughts that scare.
But, behold, my brother, we are no longer an
infant nation.
We have doffed our swaddling clothes, and
have gone into pants, and top-hats, and
motor-coats, and split-skirts, and other
habiliments of adult civilisation.
We are no longer young enough to pet and
fondle, to nurse and bounce and dandle;
And, behold, mother has taken away the
candle!
This is well enough ;
And nobody would be complaining if the dear
old lady didn’t try to fill us up with the
stuff
That was designed alone for infant ears,
And to allay imaginary fears.
She forgets, the poor, old worried mum, that
we have, so to speak, arrived now at
years of discretion,
And (if you will pardon the expression)
Endeavors to pull her trusting offspring’s
leg with the old, old tale
Of the beautiful and ever watchful guardian
angel that will never fail
To banish the naughty, nasty bogeys, the
wicked ogres that lurk
Around our little bed.... Brother, that
guardian angel gag won't world
We happen to know a little about this
saffron-colored seraph, this Mongolian
cherub to whose tender care our doting
parent would leave us ;
And, unless our eyes deceive us,
He bears a most remarkable resemblance to
the ogre that we fear!
We have not the least doubt that he will
most obligingly hover near
Our little cot.
But we are very, very anxious concerning
certain little childish possessions we
have got.
We have our own m-ivate opinions about the
sort of watch he will keep ;
And we have Wisely, if rebelliously, decided
that WE WILL NOT GO TO SLEEP ! !
Speaking of guardian angels and other birds,
I should just like to say a few words
In conclusion
In reference to this guardian angel illusion.
It will be remembered that mother herself,
when she was young, and not so handy
with the flatiron of war as she is to-day,
Had a little experience of her own in that
way.
It was a Saxon guardian angel, with fierce
whiskers and a spear,
That poor mother put her maiden trust in;
and it would appear
That he treated her in a very shameful and
ungentlemanly style;
For, after he had expelled the Scot burglar
or the Piet fowl-thief or whoever it was.
he remarked, with a sinister smile :
“Well, now that I am here,
My dear,
I think I’ll stay for a while.”
And that’s how mother got married.... He
did marry her in the end, or so I under-
stand,
And made an honest woman of her, and in
time they built up a very respectable
home in the land.
But, after all, despite his morals, he was a
white man. and a decent sort of n fellow.
And things might have been very different if
his color had happened to be yellow.
Since th«n, if any reliance can be placed on
the histories that adorn my shelf,
Mother has gone in rather largely for the
guardian business herself.
And this she has done, I must confess,
With considerable success.
She has played the benign guardian angel,
at one time and another, to quite a num-
ber of simple and unsophisticated folk,
Who. when her guardianship has become too
insistent, have not always appeared to
appreciate the joke.
But, my brother, this is what I should very
much like to know :
Since the old girl knows so much about this
thing, through personal experience, tchy
does she want to go
And put up that rusty old hlilff on her inno-
cent and confiding little son?
In the circumstances there is only one thing
for him to do, and the lesson cannot be
learned too soon: The only reliaMe guar-
dian angel for children of his age is a
gun !
I don’t know what you think about it,
brother;
But, speaking privately and strictly between
ourselves, I think it’s pretty crook on
the part of mother.
VICTORIA.
DEN.
The Spirits.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
[N a devotional silence we await Professor
Wylie, psychologist and mental healer,
lie is also a dreamer of visions, who
di earns alwavs with one eye open for the
main chance.
He conies piesently, a cherubic man with
indications of a crease around his waist-
coat and a hint of an embryo fold at the
back of his neck. His eyes are hard, and
glittersome as a cat’s. His nose is thick,
and his lips are curly.
let, with all these indications of a materi-
alistic bent, he is savagely ascetic. He
gives his word on it.
One of the Professor’s “cures” by “sug-
gestion” takes the chair. Another, a lady in
a weepy silk costume, officiates at the piano.
Iu fact, most of the office-bearers are grap-
pled to the Professor’s side with hooks of
steel gratitude, for “by suggestion” he has
cured every one of them of something. Even
a torpid liver has become active at his
supreme behest.
Our quavering voices rise in song. The
Professor thanks some “Great White Spirit”
in advance for “blessings,” and introduces
his subject as “Dreams.”
While sizing up his audience, he orates
in a deliberate manner of “Functioning on
the Astral Plane.” He is quite familiar
with these mysterious phases of our exist-
ence. With nractice, it seems, we may be
also. His spirit, go he asseverates, leaves
liis body and goes forth —week-ending, pre-
sumably—to Peru or Maoriland, returning
on Monday morning simultaneously with
the milk.
On no conditions could he stay away
longer than, say, dinner-time, at latest, for
has he not invited us all to his Monday-
night Mentality Class? Of what use our
attendance there should the teacher’s spirit-
ual r>art be absent? As well be given the
nut without the kernel!
He proceeds to expound dreams. It is
our spiritual self, our aura, which is the
real Mackay, and gets there every time. He
declares “our everyday visible external self
is merely a husk, and of little value except
to work for wages.” He arranges dreams
in groups, even allotting to the nightmare
a place. He is down on hearty appetites.
“How,” he asks decisively, “can the spirit
of the glutton rise above his surcharged
stomach in answer to the call of other
spirits to function on the Astral Plane?
Weighed down by butcher’s meat, his brain
excited with the fumes of spirituous and
malted liquors, the sleeping glutton becomes
a mere arena for lights between his im-
prisoned spirit and its rescuers. Hence the
nightmare. How dare the glutton then
aspire to spiritual flight? No, no! Leave
him to nightmare!” advises the Professor.
“To the swine his sty !”
But Earnest Inquirer, sitting alongside
the Sceptic with the .wide grin of a success-
ful cornerman, wants to know.
“What,” she asks herself, “of the tubby
individual who, after wading through a
heavy, four-course dinner, quickly followed
by three dozen oysters washed down with
‘Bulldog,’ sleeps dreamlessly as a babe?”
Earnest Inquirer knows of one such. In
the whole course of 40 years of gorge he
has never drear l ~d a dream. What is the
deduction? Has ho no aura? Is his sleep
simply a lizard-like coma, or is he indeed
quite brainless, and therefore exempt? It
may be the fault of the visiting spirits.
Possibly they are respecters of persons, and
draw distinctions. To them, this obese, pig-
eyed type of person may be no class —prac-
tically non-existent.
“To get good results and ecstatic experi-
ence, let the student of the psychic dine
sparingly,” advises the Professor. “Let him
quaff pure water; let him be ascetic, in
readiness for the ministrations of the spirits.
Don’t eat crayfish, friends, and then com-
plain at not getting the spirits!” Great
twittering. “All you’ll get is the night-
mare !”
This sally brings down the house. It
rocks in its appreciation. Only one or two
see nothing laughable in the crayfish jest,
but they are people devoid of all sense of
humor.
When the loud laughter ceases. Earnest
Inquirer again looks interrogative:—
“Will even a spirit then renege, at the
whiff which recalls the good old earthly
times, when fish and bottled ale were in
circulation ?”'
She turns to her neighbor, but meeting
only the wide grin of deriskm. passes the
question.
“There is no such ecs-tatic joy,” chants
the Professor in his curiously deliberate man-
nrr of speech, “as —to —rise—from —the
bod-dy leaving all sin and troub-ble, and to
soar a-way and a-wav. Manv times in my
own ex-pe-rience my astral self has so soared
in com-pany with other bright luminosities
to the limits of Snace.
“Once, as an instance. I well remember
meeting Mrs. Gull a daughter of old Dr.
Drugge, known to many of you in Mel-
bourne. She. though ap-parently _ sleep-ing
in her room at her home, was functioning on
the Astral Plane. Though the mother of a
lovely babe Mrs. Gull found her greatest joy

WEEROONA.
Truth.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
LONG had I labored vainly, in the dark,
r a dim study on a sordid street,
Thinking to conjure up the vital spark,
That on the hilltop only one may meet.
Then came a wind that blew my papers wide,
Threw wide my door, and made but light
of me,
And cast me on a sunny mountain side,
from those clouded ways, and left me
free.
Yet, with the inborn curse to be immured,
Soon 1 sought out a narrow house of stone
-Lhat still within its walls might be secured
lhat little world that rounds in self alone.
Therein a study, up a flight of stairs—
Once more that foolish hiding from the
light;
quickly, from the recollection, wears
The late uplifting of a wider flight.
True, in my study is no pane of glass;
i\or will its window (‘lose against the
breeze,
And binds and bats and flying beetles pass
As freely in and out it as they please.
Through all the hours they come, but not to
stay;
.They only whisper, passing, in my ear
A mu ys , the same impatient: “Up, away!
Ihe lady Truth can never enter here!”
“We are liet airy messengers, and fly,
keeking her truant vassals, high and low.
Oome! khe is waiting in the woods near by,
Wearily questioning the flowers that
blow.
Then comes the breeze again and I am borne,
blinking and stumbling, from my lurking
place,
Into the light of rosy, glowing morn,
lo meet the gracious goddess, face to face.
“Man loves me, loves me not”—she flings
aside
The broken blossom, and. with shining
eyes,
Springs to her feet, and stands in all her
pride,
Robed in the morning' mist, all dreamy
wise.
Now, with a queenly movement, she has
thrown
The light robe from her, like a waking
dream;
And, with her glowing hair about her blown,
Has plunged her fair white body in the
stream.
Tis not to hide her naked loveliness,
Rut for the joy of water, clear and keen,
Isussmg her freshness, with a pure caress,
lo faintest rose tints, dewy crystalline.
Then, on the farther bank she lies at ease,
I ensive, and pleased, among the flowers
that lift
Glad eyes to hers, while, wafted on the
breeze,
A-down the stream those broken petals
drift.
Now the nymph speaks, as from a far-off
sphere,
Filling the scented air with sweetest sound,
ko that I rather breathe her words than
hear
Wisdom and music wreathing me around :
l arn for love of man, free as the flowers
±o the pure breeze, or as the water weeds
Are to the stream through all the sunny
hours,
Or, to the dews of night, the grassy meads.
But breeze and stream and dew are spirits
And ask no tribute for the gifts they bring
kave as my worshippers may take of mine,
-b air reflex of unstinted offering. 99
Shine, sun! Blow, breeze! Smile, flowers r
and ripple, stream!
Truth has bespoken me, as ne’er before •
lVnw m n 1 n ra T' ened , f r ,m a lifelon e dream;
JNow shall I seek her in dark wavs no
more.
MAJORCA, SPAIN.
